THE RED PAGE.
JAMES BRUNTON STEPHENS.
Toward eve’s calm quietude I turned away
Fioin busy citied life and sought the wood,
That I might find relief in Solitude.
And with the dying of the western day
I ventured deeper in its calm, and lay
Me down aweary on the green, to brood
On those half-hidden majesties : the food
Of silence and sweet imaginings. Yea
In all that peace was naught to break its rest!
Yet not an hour had sped to Time ere came
A weeping angel laurelled as with Fame.
Then said I: “ Man ! O wherefore art so drest t
What seekest thou?" “Peace !" he cried, “a king is
dead.”
And wending through the darkness bowed his head.
E. G. BORSHAM.
Pilgrimage.
TWO summers ago I saw the Narrara and the
places where Kendall lived and dreamed
in the years gone by. The cottage he
dwelt in looked as if it had been built to
stand a siege. Its yellow-grey walls, stained by
time and weather, were thick as the walls of a
mediaeval keep; its rooms were dim, and cool,
and sepulchrally quiet. The furniture looked like
antique relics of a dead past. I spoke low and
shivered involuntarily. The house reminded me
somehow of a forgotten tomb in an ancient forest
which I had read of in some old-world legend. I
seemed as if the strange and melancholy spirit o
the dead poet who once inhabited this old tomb
still haunted the place. I felt that if I turnec
around suddenly I would see his hollow, mournfu
eyes gazing at me out of the shadows.
» * * *
The. house appeared to me to nave an uncanny
fungoid life of its own, a baleful vitality tha
made one think of leprosy. What a devil of
place for a poet to inhabit! I would as lief liv
like a toad in a rock. Yet what an ideal dwelling
for a writer of elegies ! But no : sorrow is bes
expressed by contrast. The grief that is too deep
for tears comes to us mostly when we are in the
midst of pleasant surroundings on quiet sunny
days, or
“ When some green afternoon
Turns to the sunset and is loth to die.
It is the knowledge that all this beauty wil
pass away that makes us sad, and cause
us to weave garlands of sorrowful rhyme
On the other hand, it is the revolt from presen
unhappiness and gloomy environment that lift
up our hearts with visions of coming joys, and—
on a larger scale—encourages our belief in
Heaven, the legend over whose gate shall be —like
that which was once over the gate of Medmenham
Abbey— Fay ce que Voudras .... The name o
the poet is connected with a geological feature o
the district. “ Kendall’s Rock ”it is called, if ’
remember rightly. He asked for bread and they
gave him a stone, as usual.
» * • *
Orara ! Name of music. Kendall delighted in
these melodious native appellations with theii
open vowels and rolling consonants. Yet when
uttered by an aboriginal they sound more like in
articulate elementary noises than human speech
I knelt down by the Orara the other day anc
drank a palmful of its cool, crystal water to the
memory of the singer who made it famous amongs
Australian rivers. Do you remember the delicate
lines:
A rose-red space of stream I see,
Past banks of tender fern,
A radiant brook unknown to me
Beyond its upper turn.
Its singing silver life I hear,
Whose home is in the green
Far-folded woods of fountains clear,
Where I have never been.
Ah, folded woods that hold the grace
Of moss and torrents strong,
I often wish to know the face
Of that which sings your song.
I thought of these lines as I sat in the shadow o:
a green jungle and listened to the ripple of the
river below, and the cooing of bronzewing
pigeons in the trees above. And I rememberec
how, on sultry summer days, when the city was
like a vast stone oven, and the air was full of hot
dust, and life was a baked curse, I used to conjure
up visions of the cool green woods by the Orara.
And there I was at last. Everything happens
sooner or later. What you wish for in youth that
you shall have in old age, piled up, says the sage.
Miirger had no lack of good dinners when his
teeth were decayed, and his digestion ruined by
cheap feeding and long fasts. But, after all, that
has nothing to do with the Orara, which, by the
way, I did not see at its best in this season of
drought. Kendall idealises it, of course. That
was his way. Nevertheless, it can flow on its own
merits as a charming little river, beloved of birds
and other melodists.
.* ■ » * *
Kendall lived at Grafton in his earlier years.
Old residents to whom I have spoken on the
subject seem to have but one recollection of him.
He arranged to give a lecture on Australian
Literature —an easy matter in those days, one
would imagine. When the time arrived he came
upon the platform, glared at the audience, gasped
—and fled. The chairman on the occasion rose to
the rescue with an address on beetles, or some
other nauseous but interesting vermin, entitled
“An Hour with the Microscope.” That is prac-
tically all that the eldest generation of Grafton
remembers in connection with the sweetest of
Australian singers. He was employed in the
office of Lionel Michael, an old-time solicitor,
who was also a writer of verse, and whose end was
tragic and mysterious. His (Michael’s) body was
found floating in the Clarence River one morning.
People whispered of foul play, but nothing could
be proved. The following lines, which were
found on his office-table, and which were the last
he ever wrote, seemed to confirm the theory that
he committed suicide. I copied them from his
mouldering gravestone, upon which they are still
faintly legible
Truly my trial has been sore
In sorrow have I woke and slept
And held a face of calm before
The world that guessed not that I wept.
And yet, somewhere, somehow I know
, There must be mercy yet for me :
v Man will have none for me, I know—
My Father ! I will go to Thee.
But there appear to have been dour Christians
in Grafton who had less confidence in the mercy
of the Almighty. In the Presbyterian portion of
the graveyard is a tombstone with the ferocious
inscription: “The wicked shall be turned into
Hell and all the nations that forget God.” That
is the grimmest remark I ever saw inscribed on a
gravestone.
VICTOR J. DALEY.
The Australian Landscape in Art .
THE first thought impressed by the present
exhibition of the Victorian Artists’ Society
is one of regret at the .small number of pic-
tures that deal, in any artistic manner, with
the Australian landscape—perhaps the most refined
and paintable landscape in the world. The
majority of our painters ignore Australia, and
give us hackneyed “bits” of French and English
scenery, stodgy portraits of politicians and nonen-
tities, or “still-life” studies. Yet Australia
offers even a better field for the painter than for
the author. “Our country” is not a land of
stories, of history or traditions, of ballads and
sagas and folk songs. But its lack of historic
interest and romantic glamour is balanced
by the beauty of the Australian land-
scape, its richness and purity of colour,
its original forms, its wealth of broad and
harmonious “masses.” The Australian painter
has virgin soil to work in. Perhaps European
art, the art of the castle and the battlefield, has
had its day. Certain aspects of life and nature
nave received from Velasquez and Rembrandt,
Raphael and Titian, Turner and Corot, complete
and final expression. Nothing is to be gained by
endeavouring to do again what has been done
better by the “old masters.” But the Australian
painter can conquer in fresh fields. There is no
need that he should be merely a “genre ” painter,
the painter of the accidents and externals, the
“ local color,” of colonial life. He need paint no
“ views” of typical bush scenes for the benefit of
the English tourist, nor trivial “anecdotes,” such
as the “Selector’s Wedding,” “The Squatter’s
Last Stand,” &c., for the benefit of the illustrated
penny papers ; but he should endeavor to catch the
spirit of Australia and give some personal and
poetic interpretation of her characteristic beauty.
With such thoughts, I looked round the Gal-
leries, and found few pictures that reproduced
either the spirit or the form of Australia, far less
both ; indeed, most of the works conveyed nothing
of Australia at all.
Bernard Hall is a capable painter, with really
excellent technique. But he paints'only “still
life” studies, tea-cups, palettes, vases, etc., nude
figures sitting on wicker chairs, nymphs in im-
possible meadows. His work reeks of the French
studio and has no intimate, personal charm. He
never paints Australia. J. H. Scheltema gives us
a “Landscape with Cattle ” ; it is probably meant
to be Australian, but it is purely German in
feeling, like hundreds of oleographs that we see
every day in Collins-street windows. Walter
Withers, too, rarely paints Australia. All his
pictures are sweetened with English sentiment,
suggestive of the happy homestead nestling among
the trees, the ploughman homeward plodding his
weary way, and the old rustic bridge by the mill.
Miss L. Riggall has a picture of “ Australian Wild
Flowers,” children playing in the bush, that is a
slight but agreeable work. Hal Waugh depicts
the real Australia in his large canvas of “ The
Dro ght,” and deserves commendation for his
good intentions. But his picture of countless
dead sheep and skinny horses on the parched
plains has hardly sufficient poetic insight to
transmute such material into a work of art. It is
like one of Lawson’s least worthy stories ; but
nevertheless marks a step m the right direction.
Ford Paterson is, in my opinion, one of the
few artists who paint Australia convincingly and
artistically. His latest work, “On the Coast,”
depicting a Brighton scene—the weirdly-twisted
ti-tree, the opalescent sea, the blue sky, ana girls
in pink “decorating” the beach—is genuinely
Australian. The trees are Australian, and the
atmosphere, the sea and the girls also. He has a
smaller picture of Healesville, with its misty
purple hills, long wnite roads, gaunt gum-trees,
and winding creek, that breathes the very atmos-
phere of the bush. These works are genuinely
Australian, and “personal” and artistic, too.
Yeats, Moore and others are endeavouring to
produce an Irish art for the Irish ; and our Aus-
tralian writers and painters might do worse than
endeavour to produce an Australian Art for Aus-
tralians. If it be really Art it will afterwards
appeal to the whole world.
L.B.E.
Daudet and his " Sapho."
IT would be a strange irony of fate if Alphonse
Daudet, author of “Kings in Exile,”
“Jack,” “Artists’ Wives,” “Tartarin,”
and other works of dainty humor, of real
force, and of poignant irony, should be remem-
bered after death only by his “Sapho.” Yet if
the verdict of this decade stands that will be so.
“Sapho,” on its publication, was the greatest of
financial successes. It has since been steadily
read by a few literary students, and by a very great
many of those patrons of letters whose parcels are
sent through the post, “ securely packed and safe
from observation. ” In 1901 it was dramatised by
two or three different people, and one of the
“Sapho” plays secured Royal patronage in holy
England. In 1902, that the “Sapho ” boom con-
tinues is shown by the publication of several
new editions in various languages. One of
there is an English version by G. F. Monkshood
with a pretentious “Translator’s Note,” which is
in English' as bad as the French of the book is
good, and suggests the necessity of a translation
of the translator’s effort. Such words and phrases
as “hetairai,” “faites,” “gestes,” “pudibond,”
“kalpas,” “aviditas vitae,” “charis,” “phro-
nema sarkos ” bristle over the four pages of the
note. It only requires a cheap-book-of-reference
degree of culture to recognise the meaning of
these scraps of Latin, French, and Greek; and
there is not one of them but has a good English
equivalent. Mr. Monkshood could surely have
won his obvious purpose better by writing plain
English, and affixing as a note : “lama person of
culture. I have my scraps of French, Latin, and,
marvellous to say, also Greek.”
Of more consequence than the translator’s bad
taste in nob writing English is his bad taste in
asserting with emphasis that Daudet’s “ master-
piece” is “Sapho.” Well—at the risk of being
called “pudibond”—“ Sapho” is from'first to
last a disgusting book, altogether discreditable to
the great writer who produced it, not in the least
atoning by its measure of literary grace for the
infamies which it suggests. English translations
—fortunately for England—do not as a rule give a
clear idea of the monstrous and imnameable vices
with which it deals (though Mr. Monkshood has
more nearly succeeded in that than any other
translator). When Zola wrote “La Terre”
Daudet was one of those who joined
in the protest of the Realists that the
bounds of decency had been overstepped and
that the Master of Realism had gone to an un-
necessary length in picturing the disgusting.
That protest was certainly justified, but “La
Terre” is pure and beautiful compared with
“Sapho.” Daudet never repeated the experi-
ment of ‘‘Sapho”-: he took the monejr which
poured in for it, but made no second dive into the
depths of the unspeakable. Probably he regretted
the book sincerely—this ugly blot on his artistic
life-work—and his punishment now is that it is put
forward as his “masterpiece;” he, the writer of
delicate and playful irony, with a sense of humor
which was that of a refined Dickens, is condemned
to be famous on the pedestal of an infamy. The
punishment is surely as great as the crime.
Daudet’s “Artists’ Wives,” though aone into
English, lias not had the attention it deserves.
To the short-story writer it may be put forward
as a model of almost perfection. The book con-
tains a dozen or so short sketches ; all with the
same motive, impressing with humorous irony the
argument that the “artist"’ —man of letters,
painter, musician—is a poor life-mate for a woman,
or that any woman is a poor life-mate for him.
The poet whose grand themes are jarred by the
constant reflection of his wife on her unhappy fate
in not marrying a rich linen-draper ; the romantio
wife who leaves her quiet and staid husband for
the fierce delights of life with a poetaster, because
he has written verses with the refrain that “he
believes in Love as he believes in God, ” and
who is disillusioned by a cold garret and the
duty of swathing the rheumatic limbs of a grumb-
ing rascal who evidently was a bit of an Atheist ;
the great composer who, having married an
Italian peasant for her beauty, shivers ever under
the torrents of her bullying abuse —are all painted
with sympathy and humor. On large canvases
“Kings in Exile” and “Jack” are of equal
artistic beauty, whilst the “ Tartarin ” books are
classics of humor. These too, are in good clean
colors—if not in all things quite suitable to the
“ young person.” Yet to most of the English-
speaking world Daudet is only the author of
“Sapho”!

Some correspondents—
“Quilp N.” : “Kiwi,” writing about copyright of
printed books in Maoriland (“ Red Page, 13/6/’2), has
hardly got the hang of ray remarks. I did not say there
is no copyright allowed to the author or publisher of a
book in the province. I said there is no provision made
for registering copyright. The English Act he mentions
(International Copyright 1886) is of course applicable to
Maoriland, and there fs also an ordinance dated 15th
March, 1812, which reads thus :—“The author of any
book which shall hereafter be printed and published,
and his assigns, shall have the sole liberty of
printing and reprinting such book for the ‘ full
term of 28 years, to commence fi-om day of
first publication of same, and also, if author shall be living
at end of that period, for remainder of his natural life.”
And the ordinance goes on to say that any person in-
fringing this copyright “ shall be liable to action at suit
of author or his assigns, double costs of suit allowed, and
shall pay £5O to His Majesty for public uses of the
colony.” But, despite all the laws which are in force
giving copyright to authors, there is no means of register-
ing the same, and anyone may claim authorship of a book.
In short, writers “ have no papers to prove ” their author-
ship, unless they go to the expense of registering in the
United Kingdom at Stationer’s Hall.
F.M. : Be poets and Civil Service billets (19/7/’02):
Is your usual doctrine of “ straight and thorough ” to be
ignored both as regards the Civil Service and the open and
honorable payment of men to whom the nation is under
obligation for work done and national heritage increased ?
If a poet is to have a “ haven of rest, ’ why not painter,
sculptor, singer? And will any one of them find the
requisite liberty and inspiration to exercise the divine art
of imagination amongst red-tape surroundings ? And
then what about the service ? Are you also amongst the
promoters of sinecures ? Or is the leisure of a working
billet supposed to be enough ? Hr much opportunity
did Kendall find at Camden Haven ‘ And, farther afield,
and in a larger way, how much—how very much—was
lost to the world because Matthew Arnold was mewed in
a billet till the spring-tide of song in him had ebbed ? The
straight-out patronage of the old days was better than
the modern billet business. Better still would be direct
acknowledgment and payment by the State for all work
done which actually increases national possessions. In
countries not quite so raw as Australia twentieth century
conditions give scope and afford sustenance for masterful
genius of any sort. My contention was, and is, they would
do so here if the great papers of the continent, daily and
weekly, had progressed on the lines they kept 25 years
ago. Stephens found room and encouragement in the
Queenslander, so did others, writers and artists, in the
Australasian and Sydney Mail. The talk of these papers
to-day is of bullocks, and the “artistic” display is of
crude and cheap reproduction of bullocky subjects. At
no time in history was any great city of the United
States so raw, mean, and consequently sterile in this way
as are Sydney and Melbourne to-day.
[Quite so. But the papers wont reward Literary Genius.
The State won’t give pensions to Literary Genius, and a
fair substitute is to give L.G. an easy Public Service
billet with liberty to write out of hours. Such is good for
L.G. because ensuring bread and butter, and we are un-
principled enough not to care if it is theoretically unfair
to the Civil Service—theoretically, for L.G. is rare and no
State department will be crowded with it to the prejudice
of the Public Service.]
“The Web of Empire,” by Donald Mackenzie
Wallace (George Robertson & Co., Sydney), is a
dull, flat, insipid record of the recent tour of the
Duke and Duchess of York—relieved only by
some very charming pictures. This commonplace
report of a couple of commonplace people’s doings
doesn’t deserve such fine illustrations.
“Elma Trevor” (George Robertson & Co.,
Sydney), by Florence Darnly and R. L. Hodgson,
is a trifle above the average novel in its treatment
of a hackneyed theme—the good man with the love
for the good wife of some other man.
Carnegie’s “Empire of Business” (from George
Robertson and Co., Sydney) is a curious com-
pound of cant and commercial acumen, of blither
and keen business instinct. When the great
money-maker discusses the tariff question, or steel
manufacture, he talks with sound sense. When
he urges that every office-boy carries in his lunch-
Dag a millionaire’s cheque-book, it is silly nonsense
of the kind which Dr. Smiles, in his “Self-Help,”
made popular—mischievous nonsense, too, for
nothing is more destructive of good citizenship
than the fostering of wild hopes of money-making.
The book is, however, on the whole, a keenly
interesting one. Some of its chapters should be
studied by those interested in the iron-industry in
Australia.

MANY COMPLICATIONS LEFT BEHIND.
All Banished and Health Restored
by CLEMENTS TONIC.
The Case of Mr J. BARTLETT.
(BY A SYDNEY REPORTER.)
It is to the interest of this country that
all citizens should be so educated that they
may become a self-reliant and prosperous
- people, and to put this idea into practical
effect a reporter was commissioned to bring
before the community a mat er here dealt
with by Mr. James Bartlett, of No. 5
Patten’s Buildings, Kent-street, Sydney.
“In the time of a great epidemic.” said
Mr. Bartlett, “ when influenza was ram-
pant in Sydney, I was one of the many
who failed to elude that terrible disease.”
“ Were you in danger of meeting with
serious consequences? ” asked the wiiter.
“ There was every likelihood that I
would never get well again, as I was in a
frightfully low state for many months.
Matters went so badly with me that I
thought I was fighting a hopeless fight
against an overwhelming force, and as I
1 was laid up for weeks, and could not do a
stroke of work for seven months, I think I
had good cause to form that opinion. I got
so thin and ghastly white that everybody
thought I was going into consumption, be-
sides getting awfully weak into the bar-
gain. j The pains in my head were enough
to run away with one’s senses, being of that
heavy, depressing nature which makes the
victim feel as if there is only care and
sorrow in the world, It was not the in-
fluenza itself that I had to regret so much
as the effects it left behind, for my system
seemed so upside down that there was not
a sound part about me. My limbs w ere so
racked with aches that if I walked a few
yards I had to sit down and rest for the
purpose of easing the. pain, but another
reason why I could not go far without
taking a spell was because I used to turn
very giddy. Through that cause I nearly
fell down ifstairs several times, so
that I was almost afraid to move in case
of meeting with an accident. I wondered
what was the good of living in such misery,
for I had no energy, no pleasure, no any-
thing that was worth having. For weeks
and weeks I could not get any sleep, and
there was a feeling in my eyes as if they had
swelled up. A dark haze was visible at
every turn, my sight being so bad that I
could scarcely see, and if I wanted to get
anything for myself I had to grope about
as if it were dark. I could not hear very
distinctly either, owiug to a strange drum-
ming in my ears, so that I was not much
use to anybody, indeed, I was a nuisance
to myself, and would like to have been out
of the way of the persecuting pains which
came to the small of my back and almost
paralysed me whenever I stooped. This,
together with the thickness of my urine,
told me that my kidneys were disordered,
and I was afterwards told so by the doctors
whom I was treated by, with the additional
information that my liver was also dis-
eased.”
“ Bid they give you any medicine ? ”
“Of course they did, and I was never
behind hand in taking it, as my enforced
absence from work meant a great loss to
me, and I wanted to get better. But, some-
how, my condition remained stationary.
When I examined my tongue it was al-
ways furred and rough, while my mouth
had a' foul taste as if I had been drinking.
Not a morsel of food could I enjoy, but I
am not one to give in till I am fairly beaten,
and I used to make myself eat; but it
would have been better if I had spared my-
self the trouble, as everything seemed to
decay in the stomach, and give rise to ex-
cessive torture in the form of windy spasms.
My chest also felt as if a piece of lead had
l»dged there for the purpose of stopping my
breath, and there was no relief to be got
till. I vomited everything up again. The
weakness of my stomach was so advanced
that I could not keep my food down, so
that accounts for my feebleness and the loss
of flesh I sustained. There was also some-
thing wring with my heart, which palpi-
tated as if i had been indulging in some ex-
cessive exercise, and with my nerves all
gone I was completely wrecked from every
point of view. A twitching pain below my
neck irritated me, but for that matter I
was always in an irritable mood, even the
sound of voices raised in laughter being
sufficient to annoy me; but I was so
des ( erate and desp ndent that I did not
know what to do with myself. In any
short stretches of sleep I got there flashed
an awful picture upon my vision, aud when
I woke up I could not help thinking that
something I dreaded would surely happen,
although I recognised that death would
have been preferable to any more suffer-
ing.”
“ Why did you not try a change of
medicine ? ”
“If I had not done so I would not be
here to-day, as I was sinking very fast after
the long and torturing captivity in the
hands of my diseases, when a fellow-work-
man prompted me to try Clements Tonic.
It was not till I carried out his advice that
the influenza was beaten, and now, after a
long and convincing experience, I am sure
there is no medicine on earth like Clements
Tonic, for after taking a couple of bottles I
was feeling a new interest in existence.
The improvement was then so marked that
I ventured to hope that a possibility still
existed that I would yet recover, and by
closely pursuing my latest course of treat-
ment my heartfelt hopes were gratified. I
started on the right track with a slight
appetite, which came through using
Clements Tonic, and which that medicine
continued to increase. Then my liver be-
gan to work more regularly, a fact which
afforded great relief in several ways. For
instance, iny headaches slackened off, and
the sickly taste left my mouth, besides hav-
ing full use of my eyes in consequence of the
disappearance of the haziness before them.
The pains in my back and shoulders were
lighter than before, and I had no trouble in
breathing, as my chest was quite free from
oppression. A feeling of vigour crept
through my system through the medium of
Clements Tonic, and I was gaining in flesh
already, which showed that my food was
digesting well. I marvelled at the rapidity
with which my vomiting troubles were
stopped, while the action of Clements Tonic
on my nervous system could not be too
highly spoken of. When my liver and
kidneys had been relieved of all symptoms
of disease by Clements Tonic I hardly knew
how to suppress the gladness I felt, es-
pecially as no cause remained for anxiety
as to the state of my heart, which ceased
palpitating shortly after flatulence was
remedied.”
“ And now your health is perfect, I
believe ?”
“It could not be better, for I took
Clements Tonic till I was cured outright,
and for the past three years I have scaled
over 13 stone. lam thankful to be able to
give this information, which you can pub-
lish in any way it pleases you.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION'.
I, Jambs Bartlett, of 5 Patten’s Buildings, Kenfc-
street, Sydney, in the State of New South Wales,
Commonwealth of Australia, do solemnly and sin-
cerely declare that I have carefully read
the annexed document, consising of four
folios, and consecutively numbered from
one to four, and that it contains and is a true and
faithful account of my illness and cure by Clements
Tonic; and also contains my full permission to pub-
lish in any way my statements—which I give
voluntarily, without receiving any payment; and
I make this solemn declaration conscientiously be-
lieving the same to be true, and by virtue of the
provisions of the Oaths Act, 1900.
Subscribed and declared at Sydnty, this
twenty-sixth cay of March, one thousand nine
hundred and two, before me,
WM. P. FITZGERALD, J.P.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Garden of Weeds.
Take the cool path; my dear dreaming garden
Many cool paths discovers ;
It is pride bids me ask you to pardon
This personal fashion
Of grass green and ashen,
And wan weeds, your outcasts —my lovers.
In times somewhat sooner I planted
My splashes of color ;
What flower to each feeling was granted ?
Daffodils for the pleasure ?
The soft finger-pressure—
That first girl ? Their place now is duller.
There once, where the grass was at thickest,
Was a sunflower riot—
Gold masses, they marked where the quickest
Faint smile of all women
Dimmed my eyes as they dim ill
The gold blaze—the grass, see, so quiet.
That marigold ghost of old fancies
Is a fair hope mistaken ;
These thin, pale, dwarf daisies are glances,
In prismatic slumber,
For loves without number,
Each petal a folly forsaken.
Lifted lip !—is it here lam busy ?
You smile : Where my skill is :
STou miss the gold hearts of narcissi,
The splendour of roses,
Bright burgeoning closes,
The white flash of arrogant lilies ?
They are well—if love’s light does not blind you—
Your eyes may be keener :
Mine dimmed in the limelight—behind you,
That glaucous tuft waving
I think faultless, saving
Too pale; not my fault ’tis not greener.
M.L.
M. CORMAO.
Waiting.
Oh, the paper on my wall,
Where the roses lie and sprawl,
First a bud and then a spray,
Then six roses—pink on prey . . ,
And I count the flies that crawl
Up and down my bedroom wall.
And the carpet dimly green
With a peony between—
Every bloom has petals-eight,
Four are curled and four are straight
With a leaf to intervene,
Then two peonies, then green.
See them tread the Golden Stair,
White-robed maidens grave and fair;
She a-stoop, and she a-tum,
All their yearning eyes a-burn . . .
Never stir the maidens white,
Nor the doves spread wings for flight.
And the clock ticks on and on. . .
There’s another half-hour gone,
But it seems that evermore
That clock’s hand will point at four;
And it has to crawl to five,
When, they say, she will arrive.
Shut my eyes and she is here,
Tall and straight-limbed, wind-curled hair.
And her eyes are stars that woo,
And her cheeks are roses two,
And my summer and my south
Is her tender-kissing mouth.
VIO.
THE YOUNG ’UN.
L'Amante et L'Amant.
Dearest, the seasons come and disappear ;
The green’s succeeded by the yellow sere ;
In turn receding, in turn they re-occur,
And Summer garb gives place to Winter’s fur.
My life’s sweet Summer day will some day wane,
But not, like Nature’s, to appear again.
0, promise me, my knight, my love, my friend.
That you will come, be with me at the end,
\.nd in your arms enfold me, pillowed on you?
breast;
So let me, dying, love you. Love me! —then I
rest.
Dear heart, if Death should choose to take thee
first,
Let me be there to hold thy small sweet hand—
Thy gentle hand, a noble deed its worst;
That, clasping so, I may not be unmanned,
But gaze with steadfastness into thine eyes
A.nd watch thy mortal strength in its eclipse.
Nor let me doubt that God is truly wise,
But seek His earthly comfort in thy lips.
And, when at last exits thy final breath,
I’ll lock thee close and fly with thee—and Death.
H.V.N.
To Maud.
Weary of earth and its delusive shows,
Weary of thoughts that would not give me rest,
Weary of dark forebodings and depressed,
f craved death’s blank negation and repose—
Only to stretch the weary limbs, and close
The languid lids, and faintly feel that best
')f comforters cooling my burning breast
Vnd loosening from my heart its weight of woes,
low sweet it seemed ! When 10, a swift surprise :
Crossing my path as gently as a dove,
With something sacred in your large sad eyes,
{ou filled my soul with hope and joy and love
extending feelings and new sympathies,
. md lavishments of impulse from above.

EXTRACT FROM
“The Australian Mining Standard,” July 24/02
“Patent No. 11,514, Sydney.—lmprovements in Electric Belts. This invention is in-
genious, and exhibits means for passing electric currents of varying intensities through the
body of the wearer. The main improvements are three in number, and consist (1) of an
improved cell wherein the copper element entirely encloses the zinc element; (2) a
rheostat, or switch, whereby the intensity of the current may be varied according to the
requirements of the disease ; (3) flesh terminals or plates that are covered with wads of felt
and wash-leather, whereby the skin of the wearer is prevented from being blistered or other-
wise injured by the intensity of the current should the belt be worn for any length of time.”

SATURDAY, AUGUST 9, 1902.
Maoriland’s New Vogel Policy.
Years ago, in the days of Julius the Jew,
Maoriland had a monumental loan-drunk,
and after it, as usual, came the great break-
up. It was a dismal time, that break-up ;
Maoriland emerged from it with a fixed reso-
lution to go on no more loan orgies, and to
hold the very name of Julius the Jew in
abhorrence—financially, at all events. Fora
while the State kept its word to some
extent. It certainly never left off borrow-
ing, but where it spent £2,229,000 of cash
that didn’t belong to it in 1881, it spent only
399,000 in 1891, and that was, at all events,
a considerable sobering up. It proved to be
only a temporary sobering up, but Maoriland
itself is the last place that is likely to hear of
that fact. The Dick Seddon country exhibits
exactly the same phenomenon as do the
States this side of the Tasman Sea. It has
gone mad-drunk again, and is firmly con-
vinced that it is a completely reformed
character. It is borrowing just as
wildly as Vogel ever did, and it
still looks back on Vogelism as a
liquor that it left off very long ago, and
as one of the sins and shocking examples
of its garish youth. The falling away has
been comparatively sudden. Maoriland was
the soberest of the seven States of Austra-
lasia only a very few years ago; for the
year just closed it has been the most
utterly intoxicated of the crowd, Westralia
only excepted. It has even, in proportion
to population, beaten the Owe’Sullivan’s
remarkable record in New South Wales,
which is saying a good deal, for the
Owe’Sullivan’s record is something beyond
the average.
* * ♦ *
Maoriland, the State which forswore
Vogelism a dozen or more years ago, closed
its financial year on 31st March, 1902, with
a public debt of £52,966,447. Roughly
speaking, that is £66 10s. per head —an
amount that is exceeded, but not much
exceeded, by three out of the seven States
of Australasia. The growth of the debt in
recent years has been after this fashion :
Debt in 1889 £38,375,050
Added in 1890 292,900
„ 1891 and 1892 .. „ 45,118
„ 1893 544,772
„ 1894 568,575
„ 1895 560,549
„ 1896 2,663,816
„ 1897 1,315,838
~ 1898 596,806
„ 1899 1,974,582
„ 1900 936,446
„ 1901 1,716,793
„ 1892 3,375,202
Debt in 1902 £52,966,447
The outburst for the year just closed repre-
sented a loan expenditure of £4 4s. per
inhabitant, and even in these parts such a
record is something uncommon. That one
year’s work was equal to 10 years’ borrowings
at the average rate which prevailed betwee'n
1889 and 1895. And it was mostly foreign
borrowing. Money is abundant in Maori-
land just now, but the people, as a rule,
scorn the moderate rate of interest paid on
State loans. So the drain on the country
for interest to be sent abroad increases con-
tinuously.
• * * •
All the precautions which characterised
Maoriland finance of a few years ago seem to
have gone overboard in one insane act. For
a number of years the carefully financed
surplus was sufficient, not pnly to pay for
unproductive but necessary works, but it
also to some extent (sometimes to a consider-
able extent) paid for productive works. For
a year or two Maoriland was the only Austral-
asian State which paid part of the cost of
new railways out of revenue, and in these
days it was the bright and shining light
among the seven States, and an example
to its dissipated brethren. This year
Treasurer Joseph Ward doesn’t see
his way to carry any sum out of
revenue to the Public Works Fund,
and he expects to be in much the same
position next year. That means that prac-
tically all the public works expenditure—
productive and unproductive alike—is to be
charged to loans. The worst State on this
side of the water, even in its worst days,
never worked the loan swindle in quite so
wholesale, or quite so glaring, a fashion.
Out of the loans for 1902-3 £450,000 is for
new rolling-stock and repairs to railways,
£450,000 for roads, tracks and bridges, and
£50,000 for “ development of goldfields”;
also defences, public buildings, lighthouses,
“rates on native lands,” and immigration
expenses come out, of loans. There is some-
thing fresh and ingenuous about the Treas-
urer who i-educes freights and fares on one
hand, while on the other borrowing money
to repair his dilapidated railroads ! Yet
even by the aid of this huge fraud, Maori-
land railways don’t quite pay interest on
their cost, and it is expected that the returns
this year, solely through the reductions made
in fares and freight-charges, will be below
those of last year.
*•* • .
Treasurer Ward, who becomes more and
more of a power in Maoriland, has developed
into a boomer of an aggressive type. And
Seddon, as he gets further and further away
from the recollections and traditions of his
dead chief, Ballance, becomes less and less
of a statesman and more and more of a use-
less, roaring and impracticable Jingo. Their
insane pride in the prosperity which Bal-
lance’s policy brought to Maoriland promises
to be their undoing, and it may be the
undoing of their State also. They begin to
regard that prosperity as an inexhaustible
spring. They granted Old Age Pensions
without making any provisions for finding
the money. They reduced the postage rate.
They cut down taxation in many directions,
and diminished railway charges, and went
into the S’African war without supplying any
extra funds to go Boer-shooting with, and
burned the candle riotously at both ends, and
melted it in the middle at the same time.
Now the result is that, if Maoriland finance
was just about as honest as that of Victoria
or South Australia (which, considering the
shocking dishonesty of public finance on this
side, wouldn’t be much to boast of) Seddon’s
State would show a very large deficit instead
of its present alleged surplus of about
£240,000. A surplus which is made by
charging practically all the unproductive
works expenditure to loans is a lie and a
fraud of the worst description.
• * * »
Almost every one of the great socialistic
ideas with which the Ballance Government
commenced its career is now being used as
an excuse for borrowing large sums of money
in England. The repurchase of large estates
for closer settlement is done, to a consider-
able extent, with money borrowed from
England. The State Land. Bank is run with
money borrowed in England—not raised
locally, as in Victoria and South Australia.
Public works are mostly done with money
borrowed in England. The Bank of
New Zealand’s capital was mostly raised in
England. Now Joseph Ward has come
to repairing his old railways with money
borrowed in England. As regards some of
these borrowings it is held that they are no
burden on the people because the enter-
prises pay their way. But almost any enter-
prise can be made to pay its way in a country
that floats loans to the amount of £4 4s. per
inhabitant in a single year, though plenty of
enterprises would cease to pay as soon as
that artificial stimulus was withdrawn.
Whether the foreign loan money is spent on
productive works or on unproductive works,
the drain on the country at large in the way
of exporting interest remains the same.
Maoriland just now doesn’t feel the drain, for
ever since 1898 it has borrowed more than
enough to pay the interest with ; bub it must
feel it by-and-bye, for the easy borrowing
process can’t go on for ever. Maoriland can
stand the ill effects of a boom better than
any other Australasian State ; its fertility,
its splendid Protective system, and the
fact that its socialistic policy has led to a
more general diffusion of wealth than in
most countries, will all tend to break its fall
when the fall comes. As to the coming of
the fall there is little room for doubt. A
country can hardly be on the pinnacle of the
second highest loan boom in Australasia
without dropping off by-and-bye.
• * • *
This new resurrection of the policy of
Julius the Jew has even less excuse than
the average boom policy. It is almost
wholly the work of the last four years, and
mainly of the last two years. The big loan of
1896 was partly a sudden and special effort
to save the Bank of New Zealand from ex-
tinction, and mostly a raising of money to
found the State Land Bank ; it certainly
wasn’t part of a definite policy of passing the
bill for non-productive public works on to
posterity. When the new swindle really began
Maoriland was the most prosperous State in
Australasia—the one least in need of being
temporarily propped up by desperate 1.0. U.
devices. It was so rich that it could have
started its State Bank and its Land Resump-
tion policy out of its own resources. It had
already tried in a small way the self-reliant
policy of constructing its railways out of
revenue, and was making some headway.
It had sworn off Vogklism more definitely,
and with a more intense swear, than any
other State in these parts. But the memo-
ries of Ballance’s self-reliant policy were
growing dim with years. Seddon went
crazed in his old age, and dropped the
politics of his own country to go whirling
the Jingo axe and bellowing insanely for
Blood. Ward was a boomster by nature
and a speculator by training. And a de-
ranged public was so busy singing “Rule
Britannia” that it didn’t watch either of its
leaders ; and so the old Vogel disease came
back to Maoriland when nobody was looking.
If things go on at the present pace, Maoriland’s
great policy of State Socialism will become
so inextricably mixed up with the big foreign-
loan disease that no one will be able to tell
for certain which was which. The same
parasite grows on practically every policy in
this country. There has been Protection
and big loans, also Revenue Tariffism and
bigger loans, semi - Freetrade and very
large loans, State Socialism and steadily
increasing loans. There have been loans
without any policy, but it is so long since
any Australasian Government had a fixed
idea of any kind, without borrowing a large
sum of money to celebrate the fact, that the
date of the episode is practically forgotten.
The Perils of the Worker.
In the battle for a livelihood, the seaman and the
coal-miner hold the most dangerous posts. This
fact has been brought home to Australians during
the last few days by two great calamities: The
Quiraing, with 22 souls on board, has been
definitely given up as lost; and at Mt. Kembla
some 90 miners have been killed by an explosion
of gas and the subsequent formation of poisonous
fumes. It will be a public wrong if the greater of
these tragedies is allowed to push from memory
the lesser ; and if, in either, any degree of investi-
gation likely to lead to a disclosure of the truth is
spared. The point which is to be cleared in each
case is whether the tragedy was absolutely un-
avoidable, whether all the precautions that human
skill and foresight could suggest were taken to
avoid disaster.
* * * *
It is obvious that going to sea in ships will
always be attended with some degree of risk.
Circumstances which no degree of care and
prudence could have provided for will occasionally
arise, and a great sacrifice of life be made to the
violence of the elements or to some sorry jest of
Fate. A ship well-found, well-engined, well-
manned, loaded with proper regard for her carry- ’
ing capacity, and managed with all possible skill
and precaution, will sometimes founder, be
wrecked or burned. Let it be clearly ascertained
that in the case of the Quiraing there was in no
respect a neglect of any of the measures against
disaster which human skill can devise, and then
there is no more to be said. The unavoidable
happenings of Fate maybe mourned ; they cannot
be guarded against. If, however, there was
neglect or rashness in any particular, let that fact
be established, and any possible steps be
taken to avoid such happenings in the future.
The sailor’s life has quite enough of natural
risk, without human neglect adding to it
one iota. The Bulletin does not suggest that,
in regard to the Quirang, there was any neglect of
reasonable precaution, but asks for the fullest
investigation, to settle the matter beyond doubt.
* * * *
Similarly, in respect to the mournful calamity
at Mt. Kembla. It may be that all steps which
human reason and skill could suggest were taken
to secure the safe working of the mine ; and that
similar mines may be in future worked under
similar conditions, without further risk to human
lives than Fate ordains. The law of destiny over-
rules all things, and may not be questioned. But
outside of that, there is an offending which brings
down “sorrows outside of destiny.” In face of such
happenings.as that recently at Martinique, there
is no room for human questioning. We can
neither foresee nor foreguard them. In coal-
mining, as in other mining, there will occasionally
be a cruel freak of Fate, impossible to provide
against. But was the Kembla disaster of such a
nature, or was there contributory human negli-
gence ? That is the point on which there must
be strict inquiry.
• • » *
Mt. Kembla colliery was said to be “absolutely
free ” from “gas,” and for that reason was worked,
as most other New South Wales mines are worked,
with the aid of naked lights. But for a coal-pit
to be “ absolutely free ” from inflammable gas is a
state of affairs which is recognised to be somewhat
abnormal and liable at any time to be disturbed.
So precautions are taken (and the law directs
them to be taken) for the careful daily testing of
the atmosphere in a coal-mine. Theoretically
these precautions should be sufficient to guard
against the risk of any accumulation of dangerous
quantities of coal-gas. Practically, in the Mt.
Kembla case, they were not. Whether that
was due to any neglect of the procedure
enjoined by the law, or to an inherent defi-
ciency in the procedure, is the point at issue.
If there was neglect of the precautions prescribed
by the law, a very serious punishment should be
inflicted On the persons responsible.. If the pre-
cautions are in themselves insufficient to guard
against the possibility of another such disaster,
then the Legislature must set about reconsidering
the whole question of coal-mines regulation. Even
should it be necessary to compel the universal use
of the safety lamp, all human precaution must
be taken against another wholesale sacrifice of
life.
* * * *
Experience teaches that in this regard precau-
tion must be not only made, mqst stringent and
particular, but drawn up under the presumption
that the workers, whose safety is sought to be
guarded, will themselves in no way co-operate in
the efforts made on their behalf. So bitter is the
struggle for existence, so reckless does the fear of
hunger make men, that the miner and the seaman
will, without protest, incur preventable danger;
will co-operate with an employer in disregard-
ing precautions prescribed by law ; and even defy
both the law and the employers’ regulations,, to
take absolutely unnecessary risk. Coal-mining
and shipping regulations have therefore to be
framed as much to protect the men against them-
selves as to protect them against any danger
caused by the greed of the employer. The present
laws may be sufficient, and may be administered
wfth proper diligence and care. But it is the
business of the State Government to presume that
such is not the case, in view of these two disasters.
The Quiraing and Mt. Kembla arraign the ship-
ping and coal-mining laws and their administra-
tion. They must be treated —these laws as
systems accused by two great tragedies, and re-
quired to clear themselves, if they can. If they
cannot, there should be prompt punishment of any
human carelessness, if that be responsible;
prompt reform of the law, if it be at fault.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Two jewel experts who have examined the regalia to
be used at the Coronation of England’s Sovereign have
made the discovery that nearly one-half the “gems” in
the regalia are spurious. Nearly all the large stones have
been replaced by imitations. The discovery confirms the
statements made in various memoirs that Queen Vic-
toria’s two immediate predecessors, William IV. and
George IV., resorted to the expedient of selling or pawn-
ing the Crown jewels when they found themselves in
financial difficulties. It is said that King Edward refuses
to be crowned with paste jewels, and has ordered that
imitation stones in the regalia be replaced by real gems,
for whose temporary possession he will pay. The utmost
secrecy is maintained concerning the crowns the King
and Queen will wear at the Coronation ; no one has been
permitted even to see them. The English Crown regalia
was estimated to be worth many millions before the two
experts made the discovery. The regalia is kept care-
fully guarded in the Tower.— Daily Paper item.
The Lewis-Bird House Tax.
Tasmanian Premier Lewis’s house-tax, which he
calls an income-tax (probably the only one on
earth that is absolutely without exemptions) and
which he proposes instead of the ordinary
income-tax, on the ground that the latter
is “inquisitorial,” is nominally, Bd. in the £.
But by valuing the house worth less than
£lO a year at £5O, and the £lO house at £6O, and
the £3O house at £2lO, and the £4O house at £320,
and the £6O house at £540, and the £BO house at
£BOO, it really goes as high as 6s. Bd. in the £, or
even more. For instance, the person living
in a hovel worth £2 a year will pay
on £5O (50 times Bd. = £1 13s. 4d.), which
is 16s. Bd. in the £. The proposed impost
is really a most erratic kind of property-tax
Koliinoor and Cohenoor.
They found the crown was bogus and the sacred
gaud was brass,
And they ask each other sadly how the po.-tent
came to pass.
They found the ancient symbol was a sham, just
like the things
It, represents—the holy, heaven-created rights of
* kings,
The dignity that comes of being someone’s Eldest
Son,
The grease which constitutes a man God’s own
Anointed one ;
How can these dreams and vanities and pomps
seem real, alas !
When some fool inspects the crown, and finds the
crown itself is brass ?
They found the crown was bogus, and its precious
gems were glass,
Oh, the people may be dull and slow, like
Issachak, the ass,
Bearing weighty loads in silence; yet the time
must surely come
When it isn’t safe to tell them that the diadem
is “ brum.”
For the crown and orb and sceptre, and the long-
tailed robe of state
Are the outward signs and symbols of the inward
potentate,
And the dream begins to vanish and the show to
fade like grass
When the pageant is inspected and its top is < n'y
glass.
They found the crown was bogus and the fetish
was but gas,
Built on cant and pride and prejudice and dreams
of caste and class.
For ages and for ages men have fought and bled
and died,
And their souls and treasures wasted for that
emblem deified.
It has stood for all things kingly—and the tale
that comes to-day
Shows it bogus, eronk, dishonest in a truly king-
like way.
O dust and bones of ages, from Quebec to Khyber
Pass !
What you died for was a fat man topped-off with
a bit of glass !
JAMES EDMOND.


on one kind of property only. It seems to be
called an income-tax to prevent it falling
on the landlord so long as there is a tenant to put
it on. The occupier is supposed to pay it as a tax
on his income, said income being roughly guessed
at according to the kind of house he lives in,
whereas if it was called straight out a tax on the
house itself the burden might ultimately settle
down on the owner of the property—which is a
thing to be avoided in Tasmania. Why a man’s
income should be judged solely by the size
of his house instead of partly by the size of his dog
it is hard to say. A poor man with 12 children
needs a good-sized house, while a rich man with
none doesn’t, and the former can’t afford to be
specially penalised. The income-tax may be
inquisitorial, but any number of people would
rather give full information about their incomes
than run against Treasurer Bird’s house-tax. An
ordinary tax-payer named Smith may object to
tell Bird his income, but when Bird proposes to
get over that by telling Smith what Smith’s own
income is, he will probably object quite as much.
Anyhow, Great Britain has had an income-tax for
many years, and when Lewis and Bird say this
British institution is not a fair one they are
reflecting on the mother country in a way that is
worthy of pro-Boers of the deepest dye. Great
Britain also has a house-tax, but it ranges from
2d. to 9d. in the £ and every house under £2O
annual value is exempt. The villainous Lewis-
Bird idea of demanding £1 13s. 4d. per annum
from the poorest old widow who has a roof above
her head isn’t British anyhow. It isn’t Boer
either. It is Gehenna—which is the same as Sheol.

Coast Coffins.
“Shellback” to The Bulletin about a serious
shipping malpractice:
The custom of piling- up the decks of Western-bound
coasting vessels is increasing to such an extent in the
Eastern ports of Australia that soon both officers and
crews will be compelled to camp around the funnel for
want of other sleeping space.
Legal provision exists in all the States to prevent any
vessel from leaving port in an unseaworthy condition,
yet weekly, if not daily, ships clear out of our ports with
scarcely a vestige of deck-space visible. Horse and cattle
stalls fore and aft, fodder and sundries on hatches, in-
flammable and dangerous cargo, such as acids, &c.,
stowed in any spare corner and under boats where horses
cannot be housed. Small wonder, therefore, in the
frequency of gruesome reports from W.A. of the arrival
of such-and-such a ship with the loss of so many head of
stock. “ Mountainous seas that thundered on board at
frequent intervals,” and all that sort of thing, has become
monotonous in its iteration.
Some occult influence must be at work behind the
scenes, when so-called shipping inspectors allow this
dangerous practice to go on under their very noses. It
is useless for them to plead ignorance, in the face of what
is patent to any wharfside lounger ; and, indeed, this is
one of the abuses with which they are supposed specially
to concern themselves. Reform from within the ranks
of sailormen is, under present circumstances, impossible,
since the first word of protest would have to come from
the shipmaster. And his hands are tied, because with
him it is a question of “billet” v. “bullet.” So he
stands by and curses inwardly till his distance from port
allows him to unburden his soul—heartily backed up by
his subordinates. It is not fresh legislation that is
required, but the vigorous enforcement of the legal
provisions already in existence.

Australian Defence.
Mr. Brodrick is evidently not so cute a politician
as Mr. Chamberlain. The latter, having invited
the representatives of “ the colonies ” to come into
his parlor and discuss, in a friendly fashion,
certain matters, found that all but Mr. Seddon
knew that “ certain matters ” meant methods of
fettering and plans of bleeding, and politely re-
fused. Whereupon he convened an enthusiastic-
ally friendly meeting outside the web, at which
vague expressions of good-will and friendship
were the order of the day, and the harmony was
not disturbed by the sight of a single Imperial
lancet. But Mr. Brodrick was crude in his
methods. He came to the representatives of the
colonies with a proposal that they should consent
to the formation in the over-sea dependencies of
Britain, of reserves for the English army, to
be drawn upon for service whenever call came
from India, or Africa, or Europe. The repre-
sentatives said emphatically “No” and the Im-
perialistic idea received a rude shock. England
must now recognise that Australia and Canada
are only willing to give war-help, after judging
the merits of the war, and then in purely volun-
tary fashion. Not a soldier will be pledged
beforehand. Had Mr. Brodrick the political
instinct of Mr. Chamberlain, he would have
avoided evoking so straight a declaration and,
by not bringing forward proposals which would
not be accepted, have left the position in a state
of uncertainty. Imperialism was poorly served but
Australia much aided by Mr. Brodrick’s mistake.
If a war were to be entered upon by England next
year, in which Australia was unwilling to take part,
her reluctance could not be attacked on the plea of
any “implied understanding,” any “unexpressed
agreement.” Australia’s relations with the War
Ofßce have been made as clear and definite as
possible. The Bulletin is glad to be able in this
matter to praise the unexpected firmness of Mr.
Barton, whose attitude throughout the Colonial
Conference debates has been an agreeable surprise
to Australians. _ They have had in the past dis-
quieting experience of strong men going from
these shores and being attacked by a sudden weak-
ness m the back and knees as soon as London was
reached. In Edmund Barton’s case that ex-
perience has been reversed.
When Suppression is a Virtue.
The daily papers of Australia know how to
suppress when it suits their book. They can and
do absolutely ignore the existence of opinions and
persons antagonistic to their ideas of things. It
can only be concluded, therefore, that the remark-
able degree of publicity which is being given to
sectarian squabbles in the Australian daily press
(and more particularly the Sydney press) indicates
a wish that the old, disreputable, heathenish row
in the name of God between Orange and Green,
should be revived to the breaking of heads and the
confounding of progressive politics. The daily
papers may pretend that it is their duty,
as reporters to the public of all that is
interesting, to put on record the frothings
of sectarian bigots. But seeing liow com-
pletely they lose sight of their duty when it is
a question of smothering any expressions of
opinion distasteful to the commercial and adver-
tising classes, such an explanation of the publica-
tion of speeches, “ calculated to cause a breach of
the peace,” is very thin. Suppression would be a
virtue, in the cause of public peace and political
progress, when a Cardinal from his Cathedral or a
Boanerges from his Bethel soils his mouth with
the advocacy of sectarian bigotry and religious
persecution. But the daily papers, in Sydney
particularly, industriously publish every such
utterance j the more mischievous it is likely to
prove the more fully it is reported. The object
they have in view will probably be fully disclosed
when the next general election comes on.
The Allegedly Bogus Crown.
The news has drifted out this way that the British
crown was overhauled some time ago, with a view
to Edward Vll. ’s coronation, the result being
that about half the gems proved to be merely
paste. There were real gems there once, but
where they have gone to, or how long it is since
they went there, wherever the place may be, no
one knows. The late Queen V ictoria’s grand-
father was a sordid agricultural king with a
miser’s instinct; and one of her uncles was a gam-
bling and hard-drinking king who lived in a night-
mare of unpaid bills; and another uncle was a
silly king with a large illegitimate family and a
shabby umbrella; and both uncles had many
ladies to support. Possibly it was one of that
trio who stole round to the pawnshop with the
sacred emblem after dark; or possibly the Evil
Deed dates further back —or further forward.
What the full extent of the damage may be no
one knows ; even the Cohen-oor may have gone to
Cohen, and been replaced by a bogus glare like the
front lamp of the Crown-street tram-car, and
other historic gems may now be on Abraham’s
bosom. It is a deeply sorrowful tale. The Crown
is the emblem of Royal authority. Our waste
lands are said to belong to the Crown—and now it
seems it was a dishonest and bunkum crown. Our
Supply Bill is supposed to be money granted to
the Crown—and now it would appear that the
Crown can’t always be trusted with its own
valuables. It is nominally the Crown which re-
fuses, after serious moral consideration, to pardon
the convicted embezzler—and there is a dark
suspicion that the Crown had its own dishonorable
and fraudulent pawn-ticket in its tail-pocket when
it refused. British history is spotted all over with
the narrative of men who stood by the Crown to
the bitter end, and if the Crown wouldn't stand
by itself, but fled surreptitiously round the corner
and swapped itself for a bit of glass, then their
lives were wasted in a bad cause. The watchword
of the unadulterated loyalist has been that we
should revere the Throne and Person, and now it
would be a fair thing to have the Throne itself
inspected by competent plumbers to make sure
that the Person hasn’t made away with part of
that also. It is a time of suspicion and moral
upheaval, and no man knows for certain what
may happen next. The crown itself doesn’t
amount to much beyond the fact that it would
make a good salt-cellar if turned upside down.
It is a survival, like the sword of Othman, with
which the new Sultan is still girt, though the
sword is of little value nowadays, and the Sultan
needs badly to be girt with a big Krupp gun if he
is to hold his place among rulers. But the crown
is an emblem, and to hear that its purity and
genuineness have been aspersed is like unto a
rumor that the twelve apostles were adulterated
and the primitive church was a bogus article and
mixed with chicory. When stories like that get
about, the royal tradition is beginning to decay at
the top. The present rumor hints at low, under-
hand habits among the royal ancestors —larceny
as a bailee and such-like. It speaks of things
that are better forgotten—sordid debts, rapacious
and immoral females demanding money, kings
drinking more than was good or dignified, to nerve
themselves for the base and sinful deed, and hook-
nosed persons admitted secretly by the back door
to haggle over the heirlooms and paw the sacred
symbols. It calls up visions of unclean hands
laid on large, crumpled shirt-fronts in the royal
presence, as S9I.OMON and Isaacs declared they
couldn’t conscientiously give the price asked, or
anything like it. It is full of possibilities of
royalty being afterwards confidentially winked at
with impunity by obscene parties “ in the know.”
Even the House of Peers falls under suspicion,
for if the dealer wouldn’t give the price demanded
a title may have been thrown in to clinch the
bargain. There are possibilities everywhere, and
none of them are pleasant or dignified possi-
bilities.

Bird Tires of Drifting 1 .
Treasurer Bird, of Tasmania, sticks a partially-
honest beak into a tender spot:—
I do not think it at ail likely that our financial concerns
will adjust themselves; I mean that under the normal
operation of the existing Federal customs tariff and of the
post and telegraph service we cannot reasonably expect
any marked increase in our revenue from those sources
either during 1903 or any early succeeding year ; and,
consequently, we cannot hope that the deficiencies of the
present will be covered by surpluses in the near future.
Nor can we look for any such increase in our revenue
from existing State sources as would tend to bring about
a speedy balance in our revenue and expenditure
accounts. It is necessary, therefore, that we take some
action, unless, indeed, we are content to drift with an
annually increasing deficiency for some years to come.
There are some, who preach this gospel of drift as the best
policy during the first few years of Federation. “ Fund
the annual deficiency,” these statesmen say, “ and
trust to the Federal Parliament and a beneficent posterity
to relieve us of these burdens in years to come.” But to
bear no part of the burden ourselves ; to do nothing to
provide for the continually accumulating deficiency,
would be, I think, a most unstatesmanlike course to
adopt. The people of to-day should be required to bear
some fair share of the financial burdens which are the
price of the benefits they enjoy ; and therefore, now that
we are discovering how far our revenue under Federation
falls short of our expenditure, it is absolutely necessary
that we should at once make some effort towards parti-
ally, if not wholly, equalising our revenue and expendi-
ture accounts.
This sounds well and good. But any intelligent
person can’t help remembering that Mr. Bird has
long been a member of a Parliament that
easily comes first in thriftlessness and stands pre-
eminent among the federated Legislatures in the
matter of passing the bill on to posterity. The
Tas. Parliament, of which Bird has been more or
less the recognised financial authority and critic
for more years than are easily remembered, has
managed to spend a far greater proportion of loan
money on unproductive works than any other in
the Commonwealth. Its record shows about
£8,500,000 borrowed, and about £4,000,000 snent
on railways, telegraphs, and other productive
works. The other £4,500,000 has been splashed:
up in building and repairing culverts, roads,,
jetties that have long since been washed away, in
State aid to religion, immigration that failed,
loans to local bodies that were never honest
enough to tax themselves for repayment, defences
that are positively dangerous to the defenders,
salaries of officials, of road-menders, and
provender for hungry British Governors. The
cheerfulness with which the debt for all these
things has been passed on to posterity is the one
outstanding fact that will make Tas. finances-
famous in the time to come. Already the man
who is asked to pay taxes in order to meet the
interest of money spent on a culvert which had
rotted away before he was born is begin-
ning to say unpleasant and disrespectful things-
about his hereditary Dobsons and Fyshes and
Birds. And there is no ghost or vestige or sem-
blance of improvement in this direction in Bird’s
programme. His idea of putting the finances
straight is that, after practically all the necessary
but temporary and unproductive works have been
charged to loans, the revenue should be made to
cover the rest of the outgoings. The fact that the
old system is a silly and disgraceful swindle and
the high road to financial Tophet hasn’t struck '
Bird on the tail feathers yet. And it isn’t, at all
likely that it will do so till the State’s credit give 3
out. Bird simply resolves to sober-up and not
take more than 16 drinks a day.

The prospects of a strike of engine-drivers and
firemen on the Victorian railways are just now a
bright red. The drivers and firemen have a
strong association, and have indicated their inten-
tion of resisting to the end any attempt to make
the railway-men find the money squandered by
politicians on fireworks for the little Duke and
contingents to S’Africa. The quarrel of the public
servant with the Government on this score is a
just one. Why should the public servant always
have to make up the deficit—caused, as a rule, by
weak-kneed, kowtowing politicians stealing the
Eublic funds to glorify themselves and their am-
itious wives? The deficit in Victoria for last
year may run into about £500,000, and the
State finances will never be got into decent
condition until Australians realise (1) that
their country, being young and undeveloped,
cannot afford to spend tons of money on
fripperies for the benefit of a privileged class;
and (2) that the “poor farmer” is getting at the
rest of the community in a shameless way. The
“poor farmer” pays only the same tax per head as is
paid by the artisan and city clerk. But while the
artisan and the clerk receives little in return, the
“poor farmer” has his contributions handed back
to him with interest added. Government makes
the roads for the “poor farmer,” and takes him
water, for which he promises to pay, but never
does. The “ poor farmer’s ” grain and other pro-
duce is carried at a loss on the railways ; which
loss has to be made up to a large extent by the
suburban fares, which the artisan and the city
man have to pay.

At Northam (W.A.) police court, on June 4,
there was heard a remarkable case. A man was
charged with using insulting language to one
Agnes Dongle, a half-caste. Another man, a
returned contingenter, was also charged, under
section 55, with asserting himself to be a police
constable. Both pleaded “ not guilty,” and were
defended by Mr. Lyon. _ The cases were heard
together. The evidence given by three half-caste
girls (unpublishable in detail) was to the effect
that both defendants came to their camp and
behaved most indecently. The first man took one
of them away, and, returning, boasted that he had
criminally assaulted her. The other man repre-
sented himself as a policeman. The first man, it
was averred, then made an attempt, assisted by
the other, to criminally assault another of the
girls. The report of the case, from Northam
Advertiser, concludes: —
No evidence was called for the defence, and after Mr.
Lyon had addressed the Bench, the Resident Magistrate
(Mr. F. R. Barslee) fined the first man 10s., with 4s. 6d.
costs, or three days’ imprisonment; and the other, £l,
with 4s. 6d. costs, or seven days’ imprisonment.
There is on the business-paper of the House of
Representatives a notice by Mr. Mahon :
That, with a view to determine the expediency or
otherwise of amending section 51, sub-section 26, of the
Constitution Act by the omission of the words “other
than the aboriginal race of any State,” it is, in the opinion
of this House, desirable that a Royal Commission should
be appointed to investigate and report.—
(1) On the condition of the aboriginal inhabitants of
Western Australia north of the 30th parallel of latitude.
(2) On the system by which aboriginal natives are
assigned and indentured to white employers.
(3) On the administration of justice in the lower courts
of Western Australia in so far as the aboriginal inhabi-
tants of that State are, or have been, affected thereby.
Seeing that England has just concluded a long
and most expensive war against the Boers in
S’Africa on the pretext (in part) of Boer miscon-
duct in regard to the rule of the native races, the
Australian Parliament had better hurry along
with that Royal Commission, or an invading
army may be yet landed at Albany. The Bul-
letin does not remember anything quite so
scandalous in the maladministration of justice
alleged against the Boers, even by the most rabid
of the pro-Gore party, as this charging of men
straightly accused of criminal outrage with “in-
sulting behaviour.”

The old, lazy-going, easy-going, self-satisfying
sophistry once more (Sydney Telegraph, 10th
July):
Nobody, as far as we are aware, has found fault with
the Government for constructing railways or other public
works for which there was a genuine reproductive de-
mand in the country. And if there are such works wait-
ing to be carried out now, there is no reason why the
Minister should slacken his hand with regard to them.
In enterprises of that kind borrowed money can always
be invested with safety and profit.
The italicised clause is one of this country’s
pleasant opium dreams—one from which it will
have a very bad awakening b.y-and-bye. Some
reasons why it is a dream are these :
(1.) A work is reproductive if the people have the money
to pay for it —not otherwise. There are works which
would pay on their own merits. There are works which
wouldn’t pay on their own merits, but which would
pay in the boom created by the annual expendi-
ture of £2,000,000 of foreign loan money. There are
some more that would pay in the bigger boom created by
the yearly expenditure of £3,000,000 foreign loans ; some
more still that would pay in the further boom of
£4,000,000, and another lot yet that would pay with the
expenditure of £5,000,000 annually of foreign loans.
(2.) And if the Owe’Sullivan got the annual expendi-
ture of foreign loan money up to £20,000,000 for a year or
two the people would be so temporarily bloated with loan
wealth that probably a big opera house at Menindie or
'Walsrett would be a reproductive work for the time being
—that is to say, the people there would have enough of
Owe’Scjlli van’s loan money in their pockets to support an
•opera house.
(3.) So because a work may pay in the temporary bogus
affluence created by loans, it is held to be justifiable to
float loans to pay for that work.

The report that Milner is resigning the High
Commissionership in S’Africa is countenanced by
the sudden transfer of Gov. Lawley from West-
ralia to the Transvaal. Milner, before “ bar-
racking,” even unofficially, for the suspension of
the Cape Constitution, should have remembered
that, in serving a man like Chamberlain, too
much zeal may be worse than too little. When
the war was young, and there was some reason to
expect a decisive victory, it might seem proper to
apportion the spoils as seemed good to the con-
queror; but now, with only half a victory, no
“unconditional surrender,” no wholesale banish-
ments and confiscations—and with the wife of
Steyn receiving a presentation of 1000 sovereigns
from the ladies of Capetown (whose husbands and
brothers, according to the lying cables, whoop for
suspension)—Milner has surely become impos-
sible. He, the enthusiastic advocate of war to the
knife, now finds himself committed in writing to a
piece of illegal violence. A man who has declared
publicly that the Cape Colony Constitution ought
to be suspended cannot with any decency continue
as High Commissioner with the Constitution in
full blast in spite of him.

To give the lie to the repeated declarations of the
Vic. Employers’ Federation that all employers
disapprove of the Factories Act, several of them
•earnestly besought the Inquiry Commission to
recommend their being brought into the fold of
non-sweating trades forthwith. These were
“white” laundry-proprietors. They told a
moving tale of the persistent increase of the Chow
washee-man—there are 126 more of him than there
were four years ago, while the “white” linen-
scourers only increased by 103; also of the law-
breaking honorary magistrate who fines the
Buddhist Is. for working his employees 20 hours a
day, thus enabling that unclean sweater to
laugh at the law. But their strongest com-
plaint was against the alleged charitable
institutions working with a Govt, subsidy
without restrictions and paying little or no wages,
such as the Elizabeth Fry Retreat, the Abbots-
ford Convent, and the Salvarmy Home at Bruns-
wick. All these grapple with the case of the
fallen woman, her regeneration, and the expia-
tion of her “sin ”; and if the moral improvement
infused into her soul is proportionate to the
amount of work she does (gratis) then she ought
to be an angel in about three months.

Melb. Argus published an article lately on the
labor situation in S’Africa, which derives its
interest mainly from half a thousand articles the
Argus published during the Boer war guaran-
teeing the absolute purity of all the motives
at the back of the war on the British
side. The European miner was to be particularly
blessed in being freed from the horrors of
Krugerism, and given his vote and all the other
sacred privileges of British enlightenment. Now,
in sorrow and anger, the Argus draws attention
to the fact that the Rand Association offers ss. a
day for white workers, and white workers have
declared they’ll see the Rand Association blasted
first. Alack lit is too late in the day to blast the
Rand Association, the white workers having
wasted their strength and spilt their blood to
destroy their friend, Oom Paul, and set their
enemy, Cohen alias the R. A., in an impregnable
position. “ The circumstance that the men must
work with Kaffirs aggravates the position.” Says
the Argus, furthermore :
The Rand mine-owners are now searching Africa for
cheap colored labor, and it will be interesting to note
whether, with a great body of white men wanting work
on the field, their policy will pass without protest. It
will surprise few observers if a great deal of social suffer-
ing and trouble comes in quick succession to the racial
strife happily ended.
‘ Oh, what a difference in the morning ! ”
Polynesian Inspector Rannie furnishes particu-
lars to the Piiilp Govt, of condition of S.S.
Islanders in Bundaberg district, as follows :
Two are grocers, paying rentals of 12s. a week ; another
is a boardinghouse-keeper, and has £2OO in the Govt.
Savings Bank. Another sent last year £lOO worth of
goods to missionary friends in S.S. Islands. Another is
married to a white woman, and has four horses and a
dray, etc.
All the above alleged arguments against a whole-
sale deportation of Kanakas are also most power-
ful reasons why Australia should stop importing
colored competitors into her territory. Formerly
we were told the Kanaka could be imported with
impunity because he was restricted to plantation
work; now we are told we can’t possibly deport him
because he’s not engaged in mere plantation work,
but is a respectable citizen, paying rent, having a
balance in the bank, engaged in commercial life,
and (most marvellous reason of all) is married to a
white woman and is raising a family of half-castes.
The prospective stoppage of the great half-caste
industry in Bananaland is a grievous matter in the
eyes of the Piiilp Govt., and causes that same
Govt, to curse the Barton Cabinet unto seventy
times seven, and to talk ferociously of secession
and rebellion, with Philp for Jefferson Davis.
The Amending Wills Act, introduced into Yic,
Leg. Assembly by M.’sL. A. Bailes and Lawson,
is remindful of nothing so much as the futile
gentleman who went to great trouble and expense
to sweep up the sand in the Sahara with a hand-
brush and dust-pan. He raised a tremendous
dust—and that was all. Bailes’ bill merely pro-
vides that, no matter how a testator husband
devises his property, his widow shall be entitled
to at least one-third of the estate. It is some-
thing, but so little as to be hardly worth the
bother. What is wanted is an Act empowering
the Supreme Court to upset any will it thought
unfair and to award, out of a deceased husband’s
estate, reasonable means of support to widow and
minor children. Also, such an Act should restrict
thecharitable tendencies of departing husbandsina
drastic manner; it wouldn’t be going too far to
restrict charitable bequests to, at most, one-tenth
of the estate. Any man who regularly gave to a
hospital or a church one-tenth of his wages would
be regarded as sufficiently generous ; if more, he
would be reckoned lavish ; if he did it only by
robbing his wife and children, he would be con-
sidered a scoundrel. Yet the law allows a dead
man to carry out tricks and capers it sternly
represses in a live one. The Law’s respect for the
dead is a stupendous circumstance compared with
its regard for the living.

Yio. Inspector of Lunatic Asylums, in his annual
report, once more girds at their present farcical
system of management. He wants means to
remove consumptive patients from general
asylums, receiving-houses, special provision for
nervous diseases at hospitals, and improved
accommodation for curable patients.. Also, he
says, “ the present inducements are insufficient to
attract and retain officers with high training
and ability—especially matrons,” “ the Victorian
lunatic asylums are in many ways below the
modern standard,” and “the fact that the patho-
logical interests of the asylums are in the hands
of the Crown Law Department results in their
pathology remaining undeveloped.” Lunacy is
greatly on the increase in Victoria, which is not
surprising in a State that consigns the pathology
of lunacy to the Crown Law Department.
