War is already brewing between Minister Bent
and the Victorian railway employees. The short,
thick Thomas has Rip van Winkled all these last
20 years, and is quite unfit to deal with a large
body of men like the railway-workers. The loud
answer that turneth away peace is always in his
mouth, and he is grumpy, arrogant and offensive.
He threatens one day to “deal’’with the men
who object to his retrenchment policy, and the
next says sourly that he will take no notice of
“ yelling curs,” which means that he isn’t going to
deal with them. His suggestion that mutiny in
the railway service should be treated like the same
thing in the army, and the offender taken out and
shot, isn’t calculated to preserve order. Bent’s
temper was soured through stooping down to pick
cabbages in his youth and standing up to float
land banks in his middle age. He isn’t mentally
graceful in either attitude. Now, in his old age,
he is sitting in a Ministerial chair in a bear-like
frame of mind, without factor dignity, and trying
to run 2000 miles of railway on public-house
chucker-out principles.

The new Pinero play of “ Iris," staged at
Sydney Royal on Saturday, is a great work. As
a tragedy it even “lays oyer” “Mrs. Tanqueray.”
If it had struck Australia in a less stony day and
impecunious generation it would be a notable
financial success, for even Pinero never con-
structed a_ story on more artistic lines. His
heroine, Iris, is a rich and noble-hearted widow
of 25, forbidden by the will of a soured departed
to marry again under pain of losing her fortune,
blie is a universal friend and Lady Bountiful.
Also she wants to marry young Trenwith, who
has no money and no prospects, and who is about
to “go on the land ” in British Columbia, and has
only a tall figure and a dress suit and the prospect
of a log hut to offer as an equivalent for the for-
tune that would be lost through their marriage.
And middle-aged financier Maldonado wants to
marry her, and offers her money enough to make
the lost fortune of no account. They are both
good enough men, but Iris engages herself to Mal-
donado in sheer terror lest she may lose hold on
herself through her love for Trenwith, and go on
the land. And it is no sooner done than she repents
of her haste and breaks the engagement; and curses
grow up like tall trees in the financier’s hitherto
kindly heart. Then the trusted old family solicitor,
Gentleman One, goes off with everything, and
reduces poor Lady Bountiful and many of her
friends to poverty. So she engages herself to
Trenwith, having now little to lose, and that nice,
tall_ young man goes on the land. Iris promises
to live in brave and honest poverty on the £3 a
week that remains to her till the log hut is built
in British Columbia.
* * • *
Unhappily, she has never learned to live
on £3 a week, and she gets through all the capital
m a few months. Then she sinks down to utter
want, and Maldonado haunts her—a Mephis-
topheles with a cheque-book-till the end of it all
is a richly-furnished flat and a Past, and a broken
woman in rich raiment, with Maldonaldo’s cheque-
book like a mountain on her head. And at this
crisis, the good that is in Maldonaldo, but which
has been suffocated by his millions and his sense
of injury, comes to the top again. He repents of
his revenge, and again asks the fallen Lady
Bountiful to be his wife, and share his wealth, and
promises to do battle for her against the society
that has thrown her out. And, if the lady
closed with the offer, there might be no tragedy
after all. But, just then, the young man
who went on the land comes back with the
wealth that people who go on the land make in
dramas, but nowhere else ; and Iris throws over
her rich suitor again for the love of the tall young
ideal in the dress suit. She repents before him,
and tells him of all the woes that drove her to sin,
and he holds out a helping hand—to get his coat
and hat and go. And just then Maldonado, who
hears that he has been thrown over again for the
same rival, comes in, this time as aMephistopheles
without any cheque-book, and furiously bids Iris
to go also. The last seen of him he is an incarnate
fiend knocking the elegant flat to splinters. (He
begins with the cheapest articles, and the curtain
falls before he gets to the expensive ones.) The
last seen of poor Lady Bountiful she is going out
to end her penniless days in the streets. She is
a good woman by nature, whose life goes to
pieces between a cold, good man who means well,
and a hot, bad man who means well also—the
disaster being accelerated by Gentleman One.
And her end is probably that of a starving
female perishing on the mud banks down by the
Thames.
* * * *
Maldonado is one of Brough’s many
triumphs. He is a fine character finely played,
and this writer’s sympathies go with him He is
the nominal villain of the story, and as a curious
sample of the murderous philanthropist and bene-
volent Mepbistopheles he is human in every line.
His love turns to hate, while the love of the
allegedly better man only turns to skim milk.
At all events, he is the last man (before he
starts wrecking the flat) who offers the fallen
Iris his name and his fortune, and promises to
stand by her, and when he thinks himself thrown
aside a second time he at least smashes things
stoutly and earnestly, like one who has the blood
of warriors and bushrangers in his veins. Lovell,
as the man who took his hat and coat and left at
a crisis, supplies a good study of the pale, upright
prig. Mrs, Brough does Iris as a flesh and-blood
woman whose troubles come upon her through a
noble heart, an ill-regulated desire to do right,
and a pathetic inability to live on £3 a week, and
who loses her final chance through having too much
conscience in her immoral flat. At the same time
this lady shows a tendency nowadays to play
every part in a rather low-spirited fashion.
The whole _ Brough co. shows up well in
“Iris.” Mis3_ Temple and Leslie Victor and
some other highly deserving people may be
mentioned at greater length next week. Many
tears were shed over “Iris” on Saturday night,
and with anything like good management there
should be a hitter pulpit controversy over it, and
it should be severely spoken of by the church.
There is a moral in it somewhere; still the
solicitor gets away with the money, and Iris, if
she went quite straight in the first place, would
only inherit a log cabin in British Columbia ; and
the wages of sin are an elegantly furnished flat;
and it is indirectly the lady’s better nature which
leads to her becoming an outcast; and it is
indirectly Maldonado’s desire to undo the evil he
has done which leads him to become an embittered
furniture-breaker and to drive the heroine into the
street.

The Thornton Comedy Co. concludes a
successful season at Sydney Palace to-night
(Thursday), The co. goes on to Bathurst for three
nights to bring the light of its fun to the city of
drought and rack-renting, and thence to Brisbane.
** * *
“ San Toy ” will be staged for the first
time in Sydney at the Palace Theatre this Satur-
day by J. C. Williamson’s musical comedy co.
In the caste are some names almost new to Syd-
ney, and some old'favorites—Miss Lilian Digges,
who played the difficult name-part in the “Alice”
pantomime last year with much grace, among
them. “ San Toy ”is said to be a bright and airy
trifle calculated to make even a pessimist turn
from the contemplation of calamity to cheerful-
ness ; and there is great call for entertainment
of that sort in N.S.W. just now.
■* * * *
“ With Flying Colors,” at Sydney Lyceum,
is a play of more variety than might be expected,
touching on high life and low life, the British
sailor and the British convict (in his humorous
moments), with a glance at the British peasant in
his smock-frock uniform. The gang (Blandolt’s
plays usually prefer their villains in a gang) in-
cludes Harne Ireland, who prefers Albert Nor-
man to Arthur Styan, partly because he’sthe worse
villain and partly because he isn’t her husband.
Consequently she betrays her husband Arthur to
the law. _ Albert Norman, meanwhile, has dis-
guised himself as Lieutenant Andover by the
simple expedient of killing that individual after he
has returned home from the African war (or some-
thing) and is refreshing himself by playing a tune
called “Pom-Pom ”on the piano. The wrong Lieut,
is afterwards haunted by the tune “Pom-Pom.”
Albert Norman is trying to marry Frances Ross,
whose ridiculous father promised the equally
absurd but now deceased parent of Andover that
their children should be united. Frances Ross
and Baker remain true to each other through
much glittering scenery, though Baker is reduced
to enlisting as a common sailor through being
found out cheating at cards, the gang having
abstracted the document which proves that Baker
only did so in order to break his brother-in-law
from the card habit. Baker gets the document
delivered back in a sealed envelope with directions
not to open it till twelve o’clock ; but meanwhile
he has stoushed Albert Norman for insulting
Frances Ross, and is sentenced to three years in
Dartmoor. Stage justice has no chivalrous instincts,
and the British law severely reproaches Baker
for not being calm while Albert Norman
chased his best girl round the deck of
Her Majesty’s ship. During the trouble the
sealed envelope tumbles out near the footlights
and fades out of the drama. A few more people
die here or hereabouts. About this time Albert
Norman is discovered not to be the man who sang
“Pom-Pom ” at the piano, and in consequence an
irresponsible person informs Baker, who has
broken out of prison, that he is a free man.
Albert Norman, not being the officer he pre-
tended to be, Baker, though a common sailor, is
justified in smacking him hard, and the affair
settles itself into a mere ordinary scrap. Just
about the end Styan, who has also broken loose,
wrecks the furniture of Scene Five in his attempts
to murder Norman in fourteen different ways.
After which the good people clasp hands amongst
the mangled chairs, and somebody remarks that
it is now New Year’s morning, and that their
troubles have fled for ever.
• * • •
Bland Holt, as a bank messenger who
wants to be a journalist, does not go right through
the play, but makes a good deal of genuine
amusement in Act Three, when he gets himself
arrested as a deserted sailor in order to get hold
of paragraphs. It is a relief to find both him and
Mrs. Holt as ordinary low-life characters not par-
ticularly faithful to their master and mistress.
They have even married by Act Four and invested
in a baby, though Baker has got into prison, and
Miss Ross is very sorrowful in various fashionable
costumes. The drama has numerous good little
character sketches, but the main personages, who
are all up to their ordinary acting level, are of the
usual pattern.
#* * *
At Sydney Tivoli, Cinquevalli still amuses
with his nimble muscles and his gay little tricks
of humor; and those skilful musicians, the
Jacksons, are on a welcome return visit. Miss
Florrie Gallimore, billed as “London’s premier
soubrette,” made a first appearance last week and
proved to be a Marie-Lloyd-and -water type,
younger and not so broad as the cerulean Marie,
but astonishingly like her in voice, manner and
appearance, even to the long, bright teeth. Mark
Anthony and Tom Woottwell continue to be
heartily greeted by the audience.
** * *
Major Lenehan’s yarn, at Sydney Cen-
tenary Hall, about the S’African war was inter-
esting, despite the fact that he read it from a
manuscript book, as a dry Anglican parson
delivers his sermon. Also, he is no orator, and
the account itself was splashed over with crimson
streaks of “fine writing.” But it was interesting.
We have had war-correspondents before—men
who had gone out to see and describe; but this
one had gone out to kill and be killed ; and,
having saved his body alive, he came back to
tell why he went, and what it felt like to be
engaged in the slaying of one’s fellow creatures
There was no cant about him : As a boy he
had longed for a gun—a real gun that would do
real damage ; and when he was old the desire did
not depart from him. When the Boer war hap-
pened he got the opportunity to use that real gun,
and the interest of his story was to hear a man
who, two years ago, was a peaceful, inoffensive
attorney tell what it all felt like. It was hard, but
only moderately dangerous. There was no glory
about it—the actual fighting consisted mostly of
shooting at an apparently deserted hillside : and
there was no horror—only a long-continued lack
of proper sleeping accommodation and a monoto-
nous repetition of short rations. He told of his
connection with the historic Bushveldt Carbineers,
but had to cut it short on account of having wasted
valuable time over the battle of Diamond Hill,
and still more valuable minutes with a phono-
graphic reproduction of what Toby Barton and
G. Republican Dibbs said when lie was going
away. The phonograph and the battle of Diamond
Hill are mistakes. The people want to hear
about the Bushveldt Buccaneers. If Major
Lenehan sticks to that part of his subject, and
leaves Barton, Dibbs, and Diamond Hill out of
it, the public will roll up.
** * *
“ The Lady Slavey,” by the Sheridan co.,
opened to a crowded house at Sydney Criterion,
and the congested state of the theatre was, in the
main, deserved. Our own Harry Leston is dis-
covered in Act I. as the usual light-hearted Irish-
man, cheerfully enduring the trouble of not being
able to pay his rent or anything else. An Irish-
man’s hght-heartedness in bearing up under the
burdens he casts on other people is always much
admired on the stage. Leston’s creditors, who
consist mostly of nice girls in fashionable frocks,
voice their desire for coin in choruses ; and their
wrath is appeased by songs and dances contributed
by the impecunious one and his three daughters,
one of whom has taken a situation as her own
slavey, because the real Mary Janes have left.
Meanwhile, two bailiffs make their appearance, to
an appropriate burst of thunder and lightning
(which does not disturb the unpaid-for lake). They
consist mostly of John Sheridan, and intend taking
the ancestral furniture away in a wheelbarrow ;
but are placated with some more songs and dances.
Meanwhile, the cheerful Irishman endeavors to
repair the family fortunes by appropriating a
travelling millionaire as a son-in-law. His two
elder girls are considered suitable, as they have
learnt to play the pianola and the phonograph,
and have similar up-to-date accomplishments;
but the youngest, having gone into the slavey
business, is regarded as being out of it. A
g;reat Moral is conveyed when the mil-
lionaire, who is otherwise a nondescript sort
of personage, chooses the slavey (who conveys
another moral by looking much better in her frilly
apron and cap than in her long-tailed dress after-
wards). A variety lady, who owns a pet noble-
tman and calls him to her with a whistle, also tries
'for the millionaire, and looks so serious a rival
that the anxious parent is almost “resolved to
scratch his two entries at once.”
* • • •
Sheridan, as abailiff, who becomes a flunkey
and then a bogu3 millionaire, acts up to his
old form, and puts fun into his part of
the libretto whether it happens to be there or not.
Miss Celia Mavis, with a pretty figure and a good
voice, flutters cheerfully round in her taking
slavey costume, but doesn’t do any slavey work
while before the public. She is asked to make tea
once, but sings a song or two instead. Miss
Florrie Barns, as the Empire Lady, does some
mountainous high-kicking and joins Frank Cross-
ley in a ditty which tells humans to love each
other, because the ducks, geese, baa-lambs and
other kindred souls do the same. The dancing in
“ The Lady Slavey ” is a specially good feature,
some of it being novel as well.

The Woods-Williamson co. has moved on

Henry Lee's production of “ Cyrano de
Bergerac,” at Melb. Bijou, last Saturday, was
an admirable surprise even to those who ex-
pected to be struck by Rostand’s play, and had
heard good accounts from America of Lee’s
performance in the name part. What they
hardly expected was a handsome, well set-up
show, containing 46 different characters, with
never a stick in the whole brigade. “ Cyrano de
Bergerac,” being French and foreign in its title,
in its ancient historical interest and color, and in
the intricate discursiveness of its first act, is the
sort of play that Williamson and Musgrove care-
fully avoid, as a rule. They know their Australian
public’s objection to 46 names on the programme,
anyhow. But, if either of those experienced
magnates had given “Cyrano” a chance, in
which case it would have had the advantage of
more stage room than it gets at the Bijou, the all-
round efficiency of Lee’s company would probably
not have been equalled. This performance is a
welcome departure from the old Australian custom
of putting hopeless idiots into one-line parts.
* * * *
There are five acts to “ Cyrano de Ber-
gerac.” In the crowded Ist act, and first half of
the 2nd act the happenings are mostly outside of
the plot, rather than in it. You get pictures of
the Richelieu period in Paris. The interior of a
theatre that looks like a tavern, with a stage on
the left hand side, and a mild suggestion of the
great Cardinal in the stage-box—an incidental
duel for De Bergerac, the legendary hero
of 200 or 500 duels—a rush of hungry poets
in seedy black velvet suits (poets wore a
livery at that time) to the bun - shop of
one Ragueneau, who dabbled in ballades and
sonnets himself, and therefore allowed the profes-
sional bards to wolf his pastry gratis—a rush of
Gascon cadets to the same bun-shop. These
things lead up to the story of Nosey de Bergerac’s
self-sacrifice, as related by the present bard in the
foregoing rhyme. One character not mentioned
in that metrical synopsis of the play is a Count
who covets the heroine, but is circumvented by
the Nose. The central figures, apart from the
name-character, are the nice girl, the young man
who borrows the hero’s brains and marries the girl
just before he is sent away to get killed, and the
circumvented Count, who turns out to be not a
bad sort. But the name-part amounts to about
half of the play.
« * • •
Henry Lee, disguised in a moderately
awful nose, is a strong, impressive De Bergerac.
As far as he seems stagey, the staginess seems
natural to the man. Sometimes his enunciation
of the words is too rapid, but his voice and action
are very eloquent when he has special points to
make. Miss Janet Waldorf, hoarse, as usual,
makes the sketchy character of Roxane sufficiently
attractive. Norval M‘Gregor is capital in one
scene and satisfactory elsewhere. Also, Laurence
Hauray scores steadily as the Count. C. M.
Rodney (late of the ’alls) is a cheery pastrycook,
Ragueneau ; and Hans Phillips and John Cos-
grove make themselves useful when wanted.
Among 20 other people, there are some novices
who act their one-line apiece with so much intel-
ligence that it would be a pity to mention their
names and give ’em swelled head.
#* # *
“ Sweet Nell,” concerning which beautiful
picture of immorality in High Life The Bulletin
has said nice things on many occasions, came back
to Melb. the other night looking rather better
than when it went away. Since six months ago
the young man who nearly got executed has
changed places with another member of the
original cast, the change being an improvement.
Moreover Mrs. John Sandes figures as the casual
Duchess instead of a previous lady, and Mel-
bourne’s opinion of the royal Charles’s taste in
duchesses went up a lot when Mrs. Sandes took
the floor at the Princess’s for the first time. For
that matter Nellie Stewart looks somewhat love-
lier than before, if possible, but this last remark
is tempered by the suggestion that the divinity in
question struck the top note of loveliness at the
outset and has been hanging on to it ever since.
** * *
Feminine curiosity as to the Sex’s privi-
leges in wrong-doing was largely responsible for a
crowded Royal, at Melbourne, last Saturday, when
“The Worst Woman in London” filed her little
schedule of misdeeds. Her claims to beat the
record are based upon a straight-out murder of
her husband, an attempt to cremate her step-
daughter, and the conversion of Temple Har-
rison into a ramping lunatic —this latter is
her least forgivable offence. Compared with
the _ woman in America who was lately
convicted of poisoning two-score and ten of her
bosom friends and benefactors, the W.W. is a mild
offender. She scarcely excels Lady Audlev, who
also marries an old man, and wipes him out before
endeavoring to roast her step-daughter. Some
relief to The Sinful Woman’s sinfulness is supplied
by Bert Bailey, who backs “dead birds” to his
undoing, and marries Georgie Leighford ; also, a
ballet in Hyde Park, with stockings too unusual
for publication.
* * * *
Miss Duggan, as the heroine, is assisted in
her Worstness by shapely shoulders and arms, and
gowns long enough to goad any right-minded
woman to healthy envy. The average heroine
ought to rest content with these advantages.
Miss Duggan’s lapses into homicide, bur-
glary, and incendiarism are unaccountable.
The “Worst Woman” was strong in scenery
and effects. In the last scene but one
Miss D. and her faithful Willard fire a dwelling
which contains Ruth (the Ruths of melodrama are
mostly virtuous), and the two women argue
matters in an upstairs room while the staircase
burns furiously and flames peep in from under the
floor. And in the last scene a fireman hoses the
premises from adjoining housetops, whilst Ruth
travels over the roofs of London by telephone
wire. Then the hand of the lunatic lays hold of
the W.W. and Vengeance is His.

After Friday of this week “ A Circus
Girl ” will be numbered with the has-beens at Her
Majesty’s (Melb.), therefore a few nice allusions
to the ladies of the co., or some of them, are held
back for future occasions. “Robin Hood” is in
rehearsal for the 16th, and a revival of “ A Runa-
way Girl ” will fill the intervening space. This
will enable the recent band of amateur imitators
to come along and acquire a dim consciousness of
their pardonable defects, unless the promise of
“an interesting change of oast” means that
manager Williamson has drawn upon the recent
amateur co. for some new faces. He can hardly
have gone to them for new voices or I—s.
There lived a gifted Gascon in the misty days of
yore—
A poet, scholar, duellist, a gentleman-o’-war—
Who fought for barren honor, neither truckling to
a lord
Nor courting adulation for his ready wit and
sword;
Yet he worshipped one weak woman for her
beauty and her grace—
One vision clad in silken skirt and bodice tricked
with lace—
Though his passion was a secret that he never
dared disclose,
Being cruelly disfigured by a pantomimic nose.
The maiden sought the Gascon, and his pulses
throbbed for joy
Till she introduced the subject of another soldier
boy,
Explaining that the ’Dolphus whom she wanted
for her “ mash ”
Had the beauty of Apollo, plus an up-to-date
moustache,
His yearning gaze had told her he would offer her
his heart
When his timid tongue found courage to perform
a wooer’s Dart.
The Gascon heard the lady’s tale; his hopes
within him froze;
But be swore to make her happy, and —a tear ran
down his nose.
He carried out his promise—nay, was better than
his word,
Eor he spake the choicest speeches love-sick
Phyllis ever heard;
And he wrote delightful letters, which inflamed
the heaving breast
Of the young and artless creature unto whom they
were addressed.
And all the sweet, warm sentiments the Gascon
wrote or spoke
Were made to seem the products of the classic-
featured bloke;
And thus the girl’s clay idol in her estimation
rose
On airy pinions borrowed from the poet with the
Nose.
They wedded, and were parted by the ruthless
hand of Fate —
The bridegroom fell in battle, and the widow’s
grief was great.
She lived with Holy Sisters, who permitted her
to quote
Long extracts from those letters that her lost one
never wrote.
And so she went on mourning (though she always
raised a smile
When the Gascon came to see her) for a weary,
weary while.
But one day the patient author of her fondly-
treasured Lie
To the peaceful convent garden came to lay him.
down and die;
And the long-deluded widow knew the truth ere
he expired—
How his wit had wooed and won her for the man
her eyes admired—
How he’d freely lent the fancies of his love-
inspired brain
To the dunderheaded image of the masher who
was slain.
Then the woman kissed the poet, and he sank into
repose
With the light of heaven shining on his panto-
mimic nose.

greatly improved throughout, and having special features
which make them superior to all others. The agents for

Collins St., Melbourne. This enterprising firm is always

which includes the unparalleled Luhrig Concentrating

Very extensive alterations and improvements
have just been completed at the engineering works of
Mephen Ferguson, Melbourne. The principal item of
interest is a new Electric Welding plant, which is now in
use welding the steel-plates required in the manufacture
of the patent spiral riveted pipes which are in such large
demand for water-supply, mining and other purposes.
The new method of welding is giving very satisfactory
results, and is well worth seeing.*
* • • •
The antidote to cyanide poisoning, as supplied
by Ernest de Beer, 439 Bourke-street, Melbourne, has
been approved by the Victorian Mines Department as
being the only real antidote known. All who use
cyanide should keep one or two of these on hand. The
price is 10s. each.*
• * * •
The test crushing from Queen Margaret Central
(W.A.) has yielded 2620 z. from 200 tons. This is a very
poor return for seven years’ work. The chutes from the
Queen Margaret begin dipping into this property between
300 and 400 ft., and above this level the mine is practically
blank It will take at least £lO,OOO to put the co. on a
payable basis, and more than twice that amount if spent
in driblets, so that a long series of calls has to be faced.
Under such conditions scrip is merely a gambling counter
without any intrinsic value apart from market manipula-
tions.
* • • •
Latest advices from well-informed authorities
in London indicate the probability of a sharp advance in
the value of copper in the near future. Copper shares
which are now at a low figure might be worth watching.

UUMKDIM AMD WELLIMOIOM.
At the little Melbourne Gaiety, where the
aristocracy can enjoy all the distinction of a front
seat for one shilling, Dan Barry and co. are just
now plaving “Uncle Tom’s Cabin” with varia-
tions The Gaiety doesn’t offer much scope for
display of de white man’s inhumanity to his black
brudder, but Dan spreads out his effects as fax as
they will go—which is not far. The religious
influence of the narrative is brought very near to
the guilty souls who have paid a shilling for the
sermon; and Little Eva gets borne upwards by
cherubim who hover so adjacent to the orchestra
that the conductor seems to have entered the
Kingdom, as it were.
»* * *
The Florenz co., from Europe via Sydney
Tiv, put in their delayed appearance at Melb.
Opera House, last Saturday, and supplied an
acrobatic want in the musical programme. The
Florenzians seem to do what the Craggs did, with
the difference that they make some of the feats
more difficult and the general effect less
pleasing. All the same, they are a good
“turn.” The co. includes a massive lady
of prepossessing appearance, whose bare and
brawny shoulders are freely jumped upon by
her male eollaborateurs whilst a bounding Hebrew
professor lightens the proceedings with a Dervish
frivol. Eagan, in the plum-colored suit, is back
again, and the present scribe adores Eagan because
sweetly furious Henrietta Byron rushes in to see
him like a burst of joy. All this time Madame
Titus has been deepening a public conviction that
she is the one mimic in the world whose imitations
are vocally inimitable.
#* * *
Hawtrey has farewelled at Adelaide Royal,
in “A Message from Mars.” Also in the con-
ventionally-immoral “ Lady of Ostend.” Also in
an astonishing play by a lady writer who has dis-
covered the inherent cowardice of making one
plot turn on only two people who resemble each
other; she apparently calculates on getting 50 per
cent, more fun by making them three in number.
Clearly there are possibilities in the new idea, but
why draw the line at three? The adventures of
a whole cricket-teain who could not be distin-
guished from each other are hereby suggested as a
useful plot. Holloway has opened in “A Life’s
Revenge,” and Adelaide, which is quite un-
accustomed to seeing ladies fight with swords, is
rather shocked, and slightly fascinated. The
Tivoli has been doing good business with the
Sidney Drews and a strong co. into which Eagan
and Byron and the Willons have now been
dropped.

The Australian Gas-Light Co. appears to have no ambition
to increase its reserves, which have been practically
stationary for a long series of years. It doesn’t even
attempt to accumulate a fund for the repayment of its
debentures at maturity, and when £300,000 worth fell due
the other day they were renewed. The co., however,
makes a large saving in the deal. The interest is reduced
from 5 to 4 per cent, which means £3OOO a year less to
pay, and the new debentures have fetched on an average
£lOl 10s., so there is a considerable profit on the transac-.
tion apart from the interest saving.
* » * *
Information is still wanting as to how the
financial year that closed on 30th June really panned
out in N.S.W., Victoria, and South Australia, and the
returns for July (first month of new financial year) are
not all in yet. It is known, however, that N.S.W. had a
remarkably good month in July, revenue being £50,818
ahead of expenditure. It is also known that, despite all
the recent heavy borrowing, the loan account is over-
drawn £3,844,562, and as the amount yet to be received
on account of the last £3,000,000 loan won’t anything
like half square this, it is tolerably certain that the next
loan, despite assurances to the contrary, is very close at
hand. There is also talk of a Queensland loan of
£2,000,000; a Maoriland loan of £1,750,000 ; and loans
from S.A. and Westralia, amounts unknown. Victoria
has floated its loan of £1,000,000. Victoria got better
terms than any other State has done for a long while—in
fact, it made such a good bargain with the financiers who
agreed to underwrite the loan that the public wouldn’t
take it at the price, and most of it was left on the under-
writers’ hands. The natural result will be that the next
State which goes borrowing will get worse terms.

The Metropolitan Gas Co. (Melbourne):

Dec., 1901 56,872 9 „ „ 38,574 10,725 568,717
June, 1902 50,212 9 „ ~ 38,574 11,643 568,712
Several points stand out conspicuously in the report of
Melbourne’s very massive gas concern. The net profit is
the smallest recorded for a long while. The reserves
show a slight diminution—the first for a long while. Of
course, the latter item is not a serious one, for the large
amount provided out of profits for extensions and new
works must be taken into account. But the co. is bur-
dened with an immense debenture debt (£848,000 of de-
bentures have been issued), and a much more consistent
effort should be made to reduce this liability. Again,
the reserve of cash and liquid securities (£59,918) is fully
£lO,OOO less than it was six months ago. In addition to
the additions and extensions that were paid for out of
profits, there were others which were charged to capital,
and these have depleted the cash considerably. If the
co. has any hope or intention of ever pa.ying off, or reduc-
ing, its huge debenture debt, it needs to keep accumulat-
ing a reserve for the purpose, and apparently it can’t do
very much in that way while the dividend remains at 9
percent. The increase of the dividend last year from 8
to 9 per cent, looks like a bad mistake in every way'.

The Victorian Government has decided to have
nothing to do with the proposed private enterprise
railway to Mount Deddick. Nobody could ever discover
why any co., or alleged co., wanted a concession to build
a line from a small seaport, through a barren stretch of
country, to a so-called mineral field which had never
shown the least promise of paying for axle-grease, and
wiiere nothing was being done. Victoria stood to gain
nothing by the enterprise even if the raihvay was built,
which seemed impossible, and if the concession got into
the hands of somebody who did, by any chance, manage
to sell it to persons of weak intellect in England, it would
Erobably create ill-feeling against the State. So it is
etter to leave Deddick in its solitude.

The earnings are getting back pretty nearly to the old
level, and if they get much further back in that direction
the increase of dividend will look rather dubious policy.
The sum written off is in reduction of the book value of
the co’s office property, and it is always a safe thing to
keep that written down to bedrock. In several important
respects the balance-sheet of the Perpetual Trustees,
Executors and Agency Go. of Tasmania continues to be
a model of what a balance-sheet should not be. It lumps
commission and interest together, so it is impossible to
guess what rate of interest the assets yield. Also, it
describes £2787 of these assets as “Investments” and
£13,681 as “Sundry Debtors,” without any attempt at
supplying further particulars. These items represent
three-fifths of the total assets, and a whole lot more
information as to what they consist of would not be
amiss.
* * * *
S.M. Herald, in its financial column of 25th
July, worked off its usual elderly sneer about how “a
short cut to prosperity by means of note-issues seems
always to be a dream of those who are not
prepared to work for and attain it by legitimate
methods.” Then it proceeds to treat the proposal
that the banks should be required to hold a
portion of their cash reserve (the affair that doesn’t circu-
late) in State paper, as an attempt to create a great,
bloated, debauched circulation of depreciated paper.
Also, it points to Argentina as a horrible example of
where this sort of thing leads to—Argentina, which the
local Fat Man is always holding up as the bright example
that we should follow. And finally, it utters the solemn
warning that any rash experiments may interfere
with our borrowing powers. The borrowing business is
the short cut to prosperity always dreamed of by a stupid
old Herald that ‘ ‘ is not prepared to work for and attain
prosperity by legitimate means.” But the besotted paper
really regards borrowing as working hard and legitimately
to attain prosperity. The State paper money idea is at
least a matter of self-dependence. It consists in the
country lending money to itself. It doesn’t take any
more interest out of the country, or increase the
country’s foreign liabilities. And these are big advan-
tages in the case of the most-pawned community on
earth —which Australasia certainly is.

of a water famine is weakening many holders of Broken

The Broken Hill Water Supply Co. describes its reserves
as amounting to £53,287, but when the goodwill item
“Water rights, leases, Parliamentary Bills, &c., cost
£25,194” is deducted the net amount is as above.
Whether the net reserves are sufficient to cover deprecia-
tion of the (nominally) £164,302 worth of works is a
question that can’t be decided, except by experts. The
above big item still stands at the original cost, and
The Bulletin is still of opinion that it should be consider-
ably written down, and that the goodwill item should be
wholly written off. Some day Broken Hill must perish
utterly; it has already received, in its recent run of ill-
luck, a broad hint to that effect; and when the event
takes place (or when the co.’s concession. expires) the
B.H. Water Supply Co.’s plant and goodwill and all the
rest will be worth nothing at all. It must either accumu-
late funds with which to repay its capital, or else regard
itself as a “ wasting” concern, like a gold mine, whose
capital will be all gone after a certain time. The results
are practically the same either way, but the shares would
be a much steadier investment, and much less of a gamble
if an attempt was made to accumulate a sufficient fund
for the ultimate repayment of capital.

• • • •
Last Monday’s Sydney Telegraph again
solemnly warned the Federal Government that it must
borrow, even for non-productive works—that it must not
build any public works out of revenue—because, “ to
expend even £200,000 on public works out of revenue
might land the Commonwealth on the wrong side.” The
party which the Telegraph represents and barracks for
has brought about this state of things by persistent
cutting down of the tariff, and the Telegraph was never
weary of declaring that the original tariff was a monstrous
engine of extortion. It now demands loans on the
ground that it has succeeded in cutting down the tariff
till it is quite insufficient—and it is enthusiastically
backing the Senate in attempts at further reduction.
The honest course now is for the Federal Government to
charge necessary works to revenue, and, if revenue is
insufficient, to demand more tariff revenue, or direct
taxation. The account that is “on the wrong side”
can’t be put on the right side by loans.

The Commercial Building and Investment
Co. (Sydney) again fails to make ends meet. Still, it
comes so near to doing it that it seems a pity the board
didn’t forego part of its remuneration and turn the small
deficit into a profit, if only for the appearance of the
thing. The co. has a paid-up capital of £145,036, and its
recent record is as follows :
Loss on Loss on Pro- Total n. wrv .,
Business, perty Sales. Loss.
June, 1896.. £lBl5
Deficit.
„ 1897.. £3145 £3145 1330
„ 1898.. 4037 4037 5
„ 1899.. 1485 £l5OB 2993 8360
„ 1900.. 933 933 9293
„ 1901.. 186 186 9479
„ 1902.. 45 45 9524
The co.’s gross income is equal to £4 os. 7d. per cent.,
and the income, less working expenses, is equal to £2 7s. 2d.
per cent., on the paper value of the co.’s investments. If
the co. was trading on its own capita', therefore, it could
pay 2 per cent, dividends and have something over to
carry to reserve fund. Unfortunately it is trading to a
great extent on £148,000 of borrowed money. This cost
£4 10s. 2d. per cent, interest last year, and money bor-
rowed at £4 10s. 2d. and invested at £2 7s. 2d. net, is cal-
culated to hamper any institution. The Commercial
Building and Investment Co. shows a steady improvement
in its earnings, but practically no progress is being made
at reducing the heavy mortgage debt on its properties.
If the £14,000 allegedly callable capital could be got in
and applied in reduction of the liabilities the institution
would show a small profit which could be applied to the
writing down of the assets till things got on something
like a sound footing again. It would require a profit of
£5BOO, instead of the present loss of £45, to pay a 4 per
cent, dividend, so the sound footing is at present a good
distance off.

(Wellington, Maoriland) closed a very profitable year on

The Wellington Building and Investment Co. is a
splendidly profitable concern, but its balance-sheet is in
some ways strikingly reminiscent of many splendidly
profitable co.’s of the same kind that existed on this side
before 1893. It trades on £43,760 of its own funds
(capital £27,500 and reserves £16,260) and on £109,690 of
deposits. Ithas£l42,623invested in mortgages, which pro-
bably can’t be realised quickly at an emergency, and £9039
in “Investments” —the balance-sheet doesn’t give any
hint as to whether they are of a liquid character or not.
There is no mention of any reserve of State securities or
the like. While the deposits keep coming in it is profit-
able business to bury them to the last farthing in mort-
gages and other fixed investments (which, as a rule, are
the best-paying kind of investments). The trouble lies
in the possibility of a time coming when there will be
heavy withdrawals, and the fixed investments can’t be
realised in time to meet them. Of course, such a thing
looks just as impossible in Maoriland now as it did on
this side 13 years ago.
« * « #
The dead set which is being made against con-
tributing shares in mining co.s, in consequence of the
action of the Broken Hill South directors, is all right,
provided that the co. is given something to fall back on in
the hard times, sure to come to pretty well every mining
co. sooner or later. The co. that has to meet a slump in
metals or work through barren ore-faces with no calling
power and no reserve fund has, most times, to make
enormous sacrifices in order to pull through, when it has
the luck to pull through at all.
« • • *
B.H. Block 14, which has lately employed only
about 80 men, shuts up altogether, pending recovery in the
lead market. Last half year, the working account
showed a loss of £BOB2, although items for about £3OOO
should be taken from this to get the true “ strength ” of
the co. From the shareholders’ point of view the shutting-
up is best; the mine was simply being gutted of carbon-
ates, which are almost invaluable in building up a
smelting mixture. And in [the long run it is probably
best for the miners, too, because for the sake of a few tons
of ore the whole mine’s future was being risked. Meantime
the co. has adopted its recently-discussed scheme for rais-
ing a little more capital by preference shares.— The chair-
man of the North officially confirmed at the shareholders’
meeting an estimate recently made here that that co.
could resume work profitably when lead gets to £l2 ;
which, by the way, it seems in no hurry to do. A share-
holder asked if the co. would adopt the Huntington-
Heberlein desulphurising process, as the B.H. Prop, had
done ; and the chairman said they would if it was neces-
sary. So far, though, there is not the smallest proba-
bility that any co. which does not smelt its ore will
benefit by any desulphurising process. So the arrival of
these processes is not at all likely to make things any
better for the South, North, Junction, Block 10, or
British. Block 10 has engaged J. O. Armstrong, for-
merly mill-manager at the B.H. Prop., to design its
proposed new mill; and he ought to make as good a job
of it as anybody.
• • * •
Cobar Chesney : The Secretary writes —“You
have unwittingly made an error in your remarks on this
co. There was no syndicate to take up forfeited shares,
and there were no forfeited shares taken up. Under the
Act the forfeited shares on the 21st call were advertised
for sale by auction. At the request of several brokers,
who said they had not had time to communicate with
their principals, the date was postponed twice, and it
took place on June 14, after full advertisement. There
can be no preference in such a matter.”
• * * *
The King of Sunny Corner, Kemp Charleston,
has been dethroned. As lessee of Frank Gardner’s dere-
lict mine, he has been notified by N.S.W. Mines Depart-
ment that he must comply with labor Conditions within a
few days, or leases will be forfeited. As to the justice or
otherwise of this ultimatum, as affecting Mr. Charleston,
The Bulletin expresses no opinion. The case is typical
of many in N.S.W. Politicians, storekeepers, and miners
combine to get leases forfeited. If they succeed, the
advantage to them is mythical. In the Sunny Corner
case, miners and metallurgists assert that they can work
the mine at a profit. This assertion involves an impro-
bable condition—that the man who is put out of posses-
sion will allow the machinery and furnaces to remain.
But assuming that he were magnanimous enough to
make the plant a present to the jumpers what, then,
would be their position ? To bring the mine to the pro-
ducing stage, the workers would need at least £2OOO or
£3OOO to buy fuel and other supplies, and then would
come the “ realisation” business which would paralyse
them. They, in their ignorance, imagine that so much
matte, containing so much copper, lead, silver and gold,
represents so much money. It may do so, but—mot for
them. An army of agents, middlemen and refiners have
to be reckoned with first and what they would leave
would not provide sustenance for a bandicoot.
Sunny Corner may l»e a good mine still, but it
will never be worked successfully unless it is tackled on
a mammoth scale and equipped with most modern ap-
pliances. And so it is with many other properties.

Mount David (N.S.W.) provided a big surprise
last week —a crushing of 125 tons for 1350 z. It was never
reported that they were breaking good stone or that the
crushing-mill was working. The surprise did not have
the desired effect —the only change being that the quota-
tion-makers altered their figures from b._ 7d., s. Bd., to b.
7d., s. 2s. Mt. David’s is a oase of “give a dog a bad
name.”
* • » *
Baker’s Creek (N.S.W.) has fallen from its high
estate. No div. last half-year, as over £4OOO had to be
taken from gold proceeds to provide for exploratory
work and renewals of plant. Reports do not read too
well. Directors have resolved upon “ a vigorous policy
of exploration during the coming half-year, in the hope
that new bodies of# ore may be revealed.” It is only a
hope, and the mining hope is the most deceptive. The
position clearly demonstrates neglectful management in
the past, in that development-work has not been kept
sufficiently well advanced, and no provision was made
for it by establishing a reserve fund in times of prosperity.
* ♦ * *
Coincident with the publication of a statement
that John Warren had completed his examination of the
Smith’s Creek tin mine, shares fell from 355. to 20s. Un-
sophisticated outsiders may assume that this signifies that
insiders have got the tip that the report will not be too
favorable. But the position may be reversed. It may be
that insiders have a hint that the report will be satis-
factory and have started a bear movement to frighten
timid holders and thus prepare the way for an exciting
bull market.
* * * *
Analysis of voting at Wallaroo and Moonta
meeting shows that directors saved themselves by their
own votes. Dissatisfied shareholders proposed that three
directors should be removed, and in support of this
drastic course several allegations were made, the most
serious being that the directors subordinated the interests
of the co. to those of another co. in which they were in-
terested, and with which an agreement had been made
for the disposal of valuable by-products. Directors were
distinctly on their trial as to their fitness to manage co.’s
affairs, and their action in voting as they did, was,
to put it mildly, in questionable taste. The voting
showed : For the motion, 190 persons, 49,144 shares ;
against, 222 persons, 83,611 shares. The negative
vote was made up thus : Directors (6), 13,380 shares;
directors, as trustees (10), 13,073 shares ; directors’
relations (17), 12,385 shares; foreign powers of at-
torney controlled by directors (24), 10,405 shares—totals
57 persons, 49,243 shares, leaving a balance of 165 persons,
34,368 shares ; and of the 165 it is recorded “ these
include partners and employees of directors.” At the
meeting show of hands was : for motion, 29 ; against, 32
—the latter including all the directors (except chairman)
and wives, sons and employees. If this white-washing is
satisfactory to the directors they must have great hides.
* * • *
Greymouth Lagoons dredge (one of the largest
in M.L.) was started last week, and in (say) three weeks
time shareholders should have some idea as to whether or
not they will get any of their money back. On prelimi-
nary prospecting results there ought to be no doubt as to
success. Sydney is largely interested in the venture. •
* * * *
Long Tunnel Extended Co., Walhalla (Vio.',
decided to erect a 20-head battery. It is never too late to
do a sensible thing. Co. has paid over £lOO,OOO to
another co. for crushing, more than half of which would
have been added to divs. had it had a battery of its
own. Last half-year co. won 93630z5., and paid
£12,000 in divs., besides creating a reserve of
£6OOO. Chairman said that the mine was the premier
producer in Victoria, but he was wrong, as, during the
same period, South New Moon, Bendigo, won 11,2900 z.
and paid £33,600 div., in addition to paying a large
amount for machinery. The profit of the Walhalla mine
on 28dwt. ore was under 50 per cent, of the production,
while the Bendigo mine, on much poorer ore, showed a
profit of nearly 75 per cent. This comparison is made for
the information of “Victorian,” who challenged the
accuracy of a recent Bulletin par. as to Walhalla ex-
travagance.

The Prince of Wales (the little man who
yawned drearily through Australia a year ago) is
to wear the most remarkable headgear of the
Coronation” :
* « re , se ? lb ’® s an ordinary coronet, but its apex bears a
tuft of feathers tipped with gold. These are the tail
feathers of the fenwah, which is the rarest member of
the family of the bird of paradise. As the feriwah is not
only uncommon, but also frequents the haunts of tigers
its capture is a matter of the greatest danger and di/h-
-, ..Moreover, it must be caught alive and the feather
plucked from the tail of the living bird, as instantly after
death the plumage loses its lustre. The Prince of Wales’s
feathers took twenty years to collect, cost the lives of a
dozen hunters, and are worth £lO,OOO.
cruelt y nian an d beast, all these deaths
and this great expenditure to put a plume in the
hat. of the dukehng. What a monstrous super-
stition does monarchy appear in some of its
phases 1

Gov. Chermside, of Queensland, after in-
sisting that his salary should stand in with
the charwoman’s and the Civil Service clerks’ for
retrenchment reduction, has given another proof
of manly spirit by decreeing that the names of his
guests at Government House are not to be printed
m the papers. Previous Australian Governors
ha.ve given special facilities to reporters to ascer-
tain the names of their guests ; and women (and
their husbands) fight and intrigue for invitations
to garden-parties and musical evenings at Govern-
ment House, not necessarily out of any respect to
their hosts, not from any desire “to do honor to
DON'T YOU KNOW.
HEAVY PARENT: “I Suppose you know she is my only daughter?"
LIGHT SUITOR: “ Yaas, yaas, but really, you know, that’s more of a recommendation than otherwise.”
the Throne,” but so that by the publication of their
names in the papers they can triumph over the
people who were not asked. Thus ai’ound
“ Government House ” have centred most of
the squalid squabbles, mean suburban cat-fights
and bitter feminine jealousies of “society.” When
the names of a Governor’s guests are not put in
the papers, a lot of that will be stopped. And,
reasonably considered, the advertising of a man’s
guests is snobbery of a very vulgar kind. If John
Smith asks Bill Jones to dinner it is a matter
between them, the other guests and their Creator
only; and the fact of John Smith being a Gover-
nor makes no real difference. If Smith tells the
papers he’s had Jones to dinner, it’s impertinence;
if Jones does so, it’s crawl. The non-crawling
section of the community will applaud Gov.
Ghermside’s action.

Editor Bulletin.— Be the hideous blunder
in the Ginninderra shooting affair. Is not the
goring of rewards to the police a mistake ?
Why should the police be rewarded for simply
doing what they are paid to do? We don’t give a
reward to a tailor for having made a well-fitting
pair of trousers. The reward—if any—should be
only for those outside the “force” —“civilians,”
as they are ignorantly termed by some of the
Sydney dailies. The desire for gain leads to pre-
cipitate action, with frequently disastrous results.
Again, the practice of increasing rewards, as time
goes on, frequently defeats its own end by induc-
lng would-be captors to hang back, and thus
giving criminals more time to escape. Of course
there could be no objection to rewarding con-
stables for specially hazardous services (as soldiers
are rewarded for special acts of valor). A
‘decoration,” carrying with it a small pension,
should be sufficient incentive, if any be needed, to
policemen to do their duty.— Civis.
• * * *
Concerning the N.S.W. police :
The shocking shooting affair in the yueanbeyan district
sets people wondering how the constable is tested and
trained before he is turned out upon them with authority
in his nod and a revolver in his pocket. Of every nine
applicants eight are weeded out (the number varies ; this
is about the average). Most of the applicants are the
right, age—anything between 21 and 30. More fail in the
height and weight test—minimum forfoot-nien, sft. 10in.,
list. 101 b., 39. Vin. at the chest; for mounteds, sft. Bin.,
list., 38in. Then there is a general sizing-up, in which
mechanical tests are discarded j the preference
is given to swimmers, riders, bicycle experts,
young fellows with some knowledge of stock,
and those who, though only fair in these things,
seem to be especially alert. Those who stay in go on to
the depot, where papers are set in arithmetic and dicta-
tion (the mounteds have stiffer exam.) ; there is the
riding-test also for the mounteds. Those who fail in these
latter tests may come again. After that, police-reports
as to character, then the doctor, and for those provision-
ally accepted, the depot, where there is physical drill,
sword exercise, rifle and revolver practice, drill, and lec-
tures (at present by Supt. Cotter) in police duties and
responsibilities. The foot-men, as a rule*then go into the
metrop. district, and serve for a while with non-coms. ;
the mounteds go out with their new boots and their
revolvers to the back of the mulga, and sometimes never
get in again.

As proved in the dreadful Queanbevan
shooting blunder, tattoo-marks aren’t always of
reliable assistance in identification cases. Many of
these tattoos are done by Japs, and other Orien-
tals, who keep to certain hackneyed sets of pat-
terns. Tattooed rings round fingers are very
common among seafaring folks, and the dancing
girl holding a wreath over her head is a very
common and popular design. It is fairly safe to
bet that out of every 200 tattooed sailors at least
two will be found with, in every detail, minutely-
corresponding marks. Some years ago at Chateau
Tahbilk vineyard, in the Vic. Goulburn Valley,
were two newchum sailors who had run away from
the same ship, were both of the same nationality,
and bore on left arm, middle finger of right hand,
and chest exactly the same tattoo marks ! Both
had them pricked in, at different periods, by the
same Japanese tattooer in San Francisco.

* * * *
Hardly a day passes without half-a-dozen
papers expressing their sorrow that “through
inadvertence the name of Mr. Smith was not
mentioned among those who sent wreaths to the
funeral of Mr. Jones,” or regretting that, “by an
oversight, the name of Mr. Smallbeer, M.L.A.,
was omitted from the list of those who attended
the reception held by the Consul for Greenland. ”
They even apologise for not mentioning that some
obscure person was on the platform at a church
function, or was included in the deputation that
saw the Mayor about getting a supply of disin-
fectant for the Johnston-st. drain. What kind of
people are the Smallbeers who thus harry the
press when their names are omitted from the tail
of some twopenny proceeding, and why does the
press apologise to them? The apology isn’t in-
teresting reading. “We deeply grieve for
having failed to mention that among those who
wept at the grave of Mr. White was Mr. Brown,”
or “by inadvertence Alderman Boggs was not in-
cluded in the list of citizens who looked on while
the body of John Smith was being got from under-
neath the tram ! ” What a wooden joke it all
amounts to!
• * • *
“F.R.” to The Bulletin :
A little sketch last week represented N.S.W. saying to
Beauchamp: “You were not a good governor.” That
may have been the opinion of the N.S W. daily papers,
but was it the case ? Beauchamp earned the bitter hatred
of the “Government-house push”—the rich snobs and
the extra smart, loud women, who used to have the
free run of the premises in Carrington’s time, who
drove the Jerseys away, and who were allowed to slap
the “jolly” Hampden on the back. These and John
Norton he fell foul of. But for practical, sympathetic,
honest effort to make the best use of his position I think
no governor of N.S.W. ever equalled Beauchamp. And
now he shows his practical affection for the community
(which hustled him out of Government House so as to be
in a better position to diddle Victoria on the Gov.-Gen.
question) by acting as the genuine friend of every Austra-
lian artist and writer who goes to London. Beauchamp
wasn’t fool enough to meddle actively in politics ; but the
only constitutional duty that fell to him (the refusal of
Mr. George Reid’s impudent request for a dissolution in
1900) he carried out firmly. He did, moreover, endeavor
to encourage art and letters and good citizenship, grudg-
ing no expense and no trouble. But Beauchamp was too
good a democrat for N.S.W. ; that was how he earned the
dislike of the snobs and of the moneyed classes.

had the “jumps” badly.

* * * *
When the dead were being carried out at
Mt. Kembla a survivor was heard to sav:
“There’s friends or relatives picking out other
bodies, and claiming them, but there’s no one
here to claim poor Joe’s.” Joe had been his mate,
and they had “batched” together in a little hut
near the mines. “Look,” he continued, “this
would have been pay-night. Joe had to leave hy
this evening’s train for Sydney. To-morrow
morning his wedding was to take place. Next
week he was to return to make a home for himself
and bride ; but Joe is going to be with us for ever
and ever, and he ain’t going to have no wife.”
Another miner, who perished, left a wife who lost
her father and three brothers in the explosion at
Bulli, a neighboring mine, a few years ago ; while
one home has been deprived of five of its male
members. Some of the wheelers, and others
working near them, owed their lives to their
horses, which took them safely through the dark-
ness to the daylight tunnel. In one instance a
wheeler abandoned his horse and bolted. Two
days later the horse walked out of the mine, but
on reaching daylight it seemed to become frenzied,
and capered about eccentrically. Finally it trotted
away across the mountain.
* * » *
“ Banco ” writes to The Bulletin;—
■Re Kembla explosion. It is not so much quantity
of coal gas in a mine that causes trouble as the quantity
of dry coal dust. I was born on the southern coalfields
and have had some years practical experience in three of
the collieries. Some years after the Bulli disaster my
brother and I were driving a main heading in the old
mine. One day we fired a shot to lift a stone (a fault in
the coal seam). This caused the coal dust to fire, with
the result that the heading became for a second one mass
of flame. A second catastrophe was averted by the simple
use of water to lay the dust upon the roadways. Then any
explosion was harmless beyond the watered flat a fewyards
back. On one occasion the manager of Bulli colliery experi-
mented upon coal-dust, and found it to be as explosive as
gunpowder. Colliery explosions may be prevented by
mines being kept free of gas, by means of a powerful
draught of air sweeping through every part of them ; and
by the liberal use of water upon the roads. Gas is often
allowed to accumulate in old works which are not reached
by the draught; the trouble comes when a person enters
one of these with a naked light.

The futility of human plans ! At the trial
of the Kenniff brothers for the alleged murder of
manager Dahlke and Constable Doyle, one wit-
ness, a brother officer, deposed that on March 29
(the day on which he was last seen) Doyle came to
him and said that he was going out to arrest Jim
and Pat Kenniff on a charge of horse-stealing, and
that he would be back in time to catch the mail.
He seems to have caught the last mail—the very
last one.

An Evangelist-Torrey story, told through
the nose by the Yankee gent to illustrate the
power of the Word :
At one of my N’Yark meetings a prahminent citizen
said he was ready to ac-cept sa-alvation, and prahmised
to ac-cept it right there. He afterwards kahmplained to
me that he felt none of the e-lation and jye that he had
been led to ex-pect he would feel at the moment of kahn-
version. “ Never mind that, my friend,” 1 said, “ the
feeling will come along ahl right; keep on believing.”
The new Kahnvert left the meeting-haowse right then,
looking mighty glum. But the jye was sure to ar-rive,
as I had said. Sure enough, a while after, a friend found
him leaning for support against a lamp-post up street.
He said he was so chockful of holy jye and de-vine
ecstasy that he r’ally could naht stand. This is a pahsi-
tive fact! (Great applause.)

Evangelist Torrey (pronounced Tory) and
his singing mate, Alexander, claim to have roped
in 2000 “converts” in Hobart. The variety show
staged by these pushing Yanks drew like a blister
for 10 consecutive nights, and, undoubtedly, their
efforts have given the almost deserted churches a
much-needed lift. A favorite song, in which the
audience joins enthusiastically, is something about
an “old-time religion” that “was good enough
for Moses and is good enough for me.” The refrain
goes:
’Tis the old-time religion,
The old-time religion,
It’s good enough for Torrey (say)
It’s good enongh for me.
Torrey, it appears, is salaried by some religious
organisation in the land of wooden hams, but
Alexander, it is understood, has to depend on
what he can catch. The last day’s collections of
the Hobart season came his way and totalled CO
sovs. He returned effusive thanks; then the
audience, without a smile, sang “’Tis the old-
time religion ! ”

The leading Adelaide lawyers are having
stirring times. Symon and Downer dropped
Federal disputes to return home and fight against
each other in a long lunacy case, and now in a
long divorce case. Symon took a morning off to
go to a mining co. quarrel, where he was told by his
Senate President that he was not speaking the
truth, and knew it ; to which he replied in kind.
Gordon is conducting controversies with two dis-
tinct crowds who protest against sweeping state-
ments in his recent speeches, and Nesbit has
quarrelled with his client in aforesaid divorce-
case, and thrown up his brief in the middle of it.
•» * * »
The doings of Paris Nesbit, the brilliant
K.C., are again attracting general attention in
Adelaide. After two days of a sensational divorce
case, in which he appeared for the rev. co-respon-
dent, he went away and swore informations
against a should-be witness on his own side, with
the father and a solicitor, for conspiracy to keep
the youth out of the way when wanted. He also
started to prosecute for “conspiracy” several of
the witnesses, who had not yet appeared, on the
other side ! He announced that he did this against
his clients’ wishes, but in pursuance of his own
duty as a King’s Counsel. After which it hardly
came as a surprise, when the Court met again, to
find Nesbit abandoning his brief, which Downer
took up. Then Symon mentioned to a shocked
Court the awful “contempt” of intimidating
witnesses, for which he would ask for a committal
unless “ the parties concerned ” amended their
ways. The Supreme Court is an immovable body
to buck up against, but Nesbit is just the “irre-
sistible force ” to take it on.

“Frank M.” : Francis Bacon (Goodooga,
not Verulam), fighting the squatters’ battle, un-
intentionally tells us why the A.W.TJ. was
formed. “When I was squatting about Deuili-
quin the shearers used to pay 4d. per lb. for flour ;
same for sugar; 3s. for tea; and 4s. 7d. a week
each for extras.” Now, how much did those
commodities cost the stations ? There has been a
lot of mean work about station rations and station
charges. The taint of lagdorn —“beasts’ feed for
beasts’ work,” and “ eight ten, two and a-quarter
—is about it yet.

There was much growling amongst the
jurors, lawyers and others engaged in the Edgar
inquest at Armidale (N.S.W.), because the
coroner, personally and protractedly, wrote out
the depositions. The old gentleman has “coro-
nated ” for upwards of half-a-century; nis
pace was not a “ cracker,” and he had no objection
to “ wasting the time of the court.”

R.C. papers in N.S.W.
express some consternation at
the fact that the proportion of
“mixed marriages” is rapid-
ly increasing, and the E.C.
girl and the It.C. boy show
a growing disinclination to
consider theological issues
in arranging their life-
, plans. Naturally, from a
' sectarian point of view,
this is alarming, as it
shows a weakening of the
antagonisms which are
the breath of life to
“churches”; but to the
community it is gratifying.
It suggests that the time is
not far distant, in this coun-
try at least, when the Dill-
Macky will lie down in peace
with the O’Donovan and
religious strife be known no more.
Parson Perry having re-united in holy
matrimony the two rival Sydney art societies, and
made honest man arid wife of them again, “ the
wedding-breakfast” was held at the Oafe Francais
last Thursday. Mr. Lister-Lister being host.
There were many protestations of perfect harmony
—and a good deal of “ chucking-off ”at the Other
cat by some members. Mr. Arthur Collingndge
suggested that the united society should apply
for a charter to constitute itself “the Royal
Academy of Australia” —and then citizen Col-
lingridge aforesaid proceeded to vigorously
sing the “Marseillaise.” Mr. Julian Ashton and
Mr. Souter urged the other fellows not to be such
asses again with such eloquence that a riot ought
to have followed, but Mr. Lister-Lister poured oil
on the troubled waters. His voice is all for peace,
and under his guidance, the united society may
yet achieve perfect harmony, and perhaps give to
the world a great symbolical picture, in which
Lister-Lister will paint the sad sea-shore, and
Collingridge put in the atmosphere, and Ashton
an unrobed lady, and Leist some clothes on the
lady, and Sid Long a nice green-blue moon, and
Souter and Garlick fill up with a few weirdities of
the animal world.
. • * •
The men who lifted £3OOO worth of opal
from the White Cliffs mail, while it was under
armed escort, last January, did not leave many
tracks behind; and at the Police Court hearing
there was no direct evidence of the robbery
against any of the three who are charged with it.
The police have had their eyes turned only one way
all the time; but until the stolen gem, or what
they think is the stolen gem, was put on the mar-
ket they were helpless. There is evidence now
that Burgess sent opal across from Broken Hill
to White Cliffs to sell; M‘Gregor carried it;
Stanbury dealt with it at the other end. These
are the three accused. M‘Gregor appears
to licivo already made a statement that
he understood from Burgess that the stuff
was “crook” and that there was plenty more to
go across ; there are mysteriously-worded letters
between Burgess and Stanbury ; there is positive
identification of some of the stone sold by Stan-
bury as stone that was included in the stolen
mail; there is evidence of Burgess riding up one
other night to the changing-camp where the rob-
bery took place and asking how long the coach
had gone; and there are many other little bits
that may mean much, or nothing. There isn’t a
more popular man in the mulga country than
Burgess. Stanbury was for many years town
clerk of Wilcannia. M‘Gregor, who was not
defended in the police-court, is of another stamp
—a little old man well known in racing stables.
* * * *
News of the latest midnight murder-trial
comes from South Australia. After a long day,
the C.J. “started to sum up at 7.30 and finished
about 8.30. He concluded the reading of the
evidence at 11.10, when the jury retired.” The
jury:—it was at Pt. Pirie—took no risks (South
Australians don’t take hanging risks when they
can help it), and shortly after midnight said
“ Not guilty.”
During the past five years there has been no
hanging in South Australia, and there were. only
three in the seven years before that. No aborigine
has gone through the scaffold in that State for 12
years. (The “scaffold” in S.A., by the way, is
the dead-end of an upper-storey passage in the
gaol, provided with a trap-door. You walk along
the corridor, stand facing the wall, alone if you
prefer it, the attendants being in the room along-
side, and in a second or two you are in the lower
storey.) Also, last year, no prisoner underwent
separate or solitary confinement in S.A. gaols,
except as gaol punishment; and in this connec-
tion, strangely enough, more females than males
suffered. There has been no flogging for years.
¥ * * *
The woman who carries her purse in her
hand isn’t always such a fool as she seems. A
couple of “snatchers,” who had followed such a
one from an adjacent bank to the big cheap-sale
emporium, grabbed her purse and did a record
“steeple” down the street and over two fences
before being caught. The lady’s apparent want
of interest in the matter was explained when the
purse was opened. It contained 3|d. Having
seen the two safely in custody, she extracted some
notes from her bosom and went inside to sample
the bargain-counter.
The latest girl-story (from Melbourne)
rests on better evidence than usual, inasmuch as
the victim is suffering from being shot three times,
from nervous shock, bruises, gravel-rash, loss of
hair, from having sand forced down her throat,
and other damage. As usual, her best boy is the
person accused; true affection has a tendency in
some quarters to break out in assault and battery.
The story goes that the pair had sat together for
an hour, during which he poured forth his
tale of affection. Afterwards he knocked her
down, pounded her on the face and head
with a cheap pin-fire revolver, shot at her three
times with the inefficient instrument grazing her
each time, filled her mouth with sand, helped her
up, tore her clothes and her hair, and pounded her
some more with the revolver. When he left off,
and the girl got to her feet again, he is said to
have remarked pleasantly, as one dispassionately
considering the case, that he didn’t “know how
she stood it ” and offered to take her to town
and wash the blood off her. Even after
all this she managed to get home and
pluckily dressed her wounds herself,_ and next
morning, when her shocking condition led to
inquiry, she refused to give particulars, and did
her best to avoid the advertisement usually
pertaining to such cases. The persistent hanging
round of an excited young man, and the inter-
cepting of a letter, in which he promised not to
pound his best girl any more, led to the arrest of
a young man who was “quite calm.” The calm-
ness of all parties to the case appears to be much
above the average.

Twenty-one lady-teachers left Sydney for
S’Africa last week. Eighteen others joined them
in Melbourne. They are going to take charge of
the new schools set up in the recent republics—
Australia sent her warlike sons
To battle for the Right.
They took away the Boers’ guns
And finished up the fight.
And now, while bells of peace are rung,
And ended is the Loot,
Her daughters go to teach the young
Idea how to shoot!

“ What ! ’’ said the other “ Are you going to pass
base coin ? Hold your tongue.” He did.

Deputy-Master of the Mint in London. Sydney-
Mint owes allegiance primarily to London, much
as Gov. Rawson does, though the people of
N.S.W. must keep up both.
