A WOMAN’S LETTER.
~ _ , Sydney, August 5, 1902.
My Dear Arini.—
A fine garden-scene, staged with musical
accompaniments, by their State Exes, when they
At Homed ” Sydney people in sections on two
consecutive days. The 17 acres of “ Cranbrook ”
background, lawn and hill, and nook, and curly
walk, and rooky crescent-way ;of tree and flower
and vine, overlooking blue sea and grey-green
coastlines, are immense aids to the vice-regal stage-
manager. Bland Holt would have managed the
wind of the first matinee better. It was a day
of 70 in the shade, with a horrid north-westerly,
that blew white sand into eyes and fruit-salads
u P set the tables, and set flapping the canvas
of refreshment-tents. It unfurled hair, but
wouldn t tolerate unfurled sunshades, and com-
pelled guests to orientate themselves as un-
animously as even the new rector of St. John’s,
JDarlinghurst, might desire.
i the second day was perfect, and when the
band hymned out the Get-Ye-Goneof the National
Anthem, the sinking sun made a golden wave-
way across the bay to the edge of the shore below
the Cranbrook ” wall—a path that seemed to
invite their weary Exes, with their swollen right
hands to come away and walk into the sunset’s
rim and peace. Not to walk on their hands, of
course.
In the half-light of the “Cranbrook” hall th®
Rawson trio stood to receive, attended by Captain
Holman, who held his new regulation William of
Germany cap in his hand, andlooked at it intently,
? S j- 1 ? were a beloved photograph, when anyone
he didn’t know was announced. Lady Rawson in
black, embroidered in cream silk, with primroses
in her toque and white feather boa ; her daughter
in pink cloth and black hat and pretty etceteras
m the of collar, and roses and lace. First
day’s “At Home” was attended principally by
persons of whom the second day’s gathering says
it never heard. Second day’s gathering was
attended by people of whom the first-dayers say
they have heard altogether too much. Official,
ministerial, and consular folk gilded the first day’s
assembly. The Wises—also the Sees, Kidds,
Perrys, O’Sullivans and a lot of ordinary M.’sP.
and their wives. A young local musician squired
the State Premier’s wife. In the daily-paper list
of guests he was credited with a wife (which the
poor thing hasn’t got), and he has been busy ever
since repelling congratulatory wires and words.
France’s vice-consul, aristocratically lean, diplo-
matically expressionless, looked after the yearnings
of ladies whose husbands are the Heads of Some-
thing. „
Dressing on both days was representative of
winter and summer—transparent sleeves and
tailor-mades and furs. One who wore a
tight-fitting coat of golden-brown fur, barred with
darker stripes, made the mistake of having a grey
skirt instead of carrying out the warm tortoise-
shell-cat etfect with a brown one. There was a
remarkable hat down on the tennis-court, where a
large cluster of small tables was set with the
kind of food that spoils your dinner. What was
under the hat was eating old-rose ice-cream, and
now and then the spoon would catch in a trail of
tiger-lilies that hung from what looked like a
Portuguese man-o’-war sailing on, the brim of the
hat. There was a lovely pale-green gown, em-
broidered with raised chiffon flowers, but it was too
early Spring-like for the robust, Summer-colored
matron who wore it. Everybody was delighted
with everything, but Society is badly in need of two
words to take the place of “charming” and
“smart.” The Rawsons were “charming”; so
was the view, and the sea, and the day, and the
motor-car that- puffed in the dust below, and the
band ; even the coffee was “ charming ” ; and their
friends were either “smart” or not “smart.” I
had some fowls, Indian-game breed, upstanding,
stylish-looking birds, recommended to me the
other day by a society man as “ very smart
fowls to have about a place.”
the strangers within “Cranbrook”
gates was Franz Wilczek, the Austrian-American
violinist, who is here on a vacation. He had the
intention of filling some of the empty time with
concerts, but his friend, Gerardy’s, failure to
make a financial success of his Australian tour
has warned the violinist not to take risks where
music is at neap tide. Wilczek is a beautifully-
tailored, short-haired young man with a deep
cleft in his chin and an abiding love for fox-
terriers and birds. A while ago I saw in an
American paper a cartoonlet of him escap-
ing from a burning hotel with a cage of
birds in his hand. He had remembered
them, but forgotten his violins. A New York
paper says that, apart from being one of the
world’s great violinists, he is the fad of the smart
set along the Atlantic coast. Now, isn’t it strange
to call a man a “fad,” as if he were ping-pong or
a motor. One thing impressed Mr. Wilczek at
the “ Cranbrook ” “At Home,” and that was the
number of men there. Half the crowd was male.
At a function of the sweilest kind in New York,
there would probably be not one man, or not more
than three.
. Fashion of shading hands is said to be going
into limbo. This ought to be good news for
Governors and Mayors. At the Rawson recep-
tion, one woman was seriously reproached by an-
other for shaking hands with the A.D.C.’s.
•Their uniforms,” she said, “should have pro-
tected them.” The new play, “With
Colors,” at the Lyceum, is a fine lesson on the
etiquette due to uniformed men.
Iris blossomed and beamed (I believe she is
a rainbow as well as a flower) to a crowded house
for the first time in Australia, last Saturday.
Pinero s Iris is a widow (charm 1). She is beauti-
ful (charm 2). She has the grace of a cat, and the
sensuousness of one; and she has the soul of a
sparrow Several men love her according to their
kind, and Pinero has made a great play of its kind
on her rainbow moods. For choice accessories and
environment Iris had a taste, but no more mind to
toil or spin than the lilies; she was that kind of
flower which likes to get through the world without
hard labor. Ins contains a really beautiful study
m flats (no reflection intended on the intellects of
the men m love with her- 1 mean the furnishing
of flats) and epigrams; also a twentieth-century
Romeoand Ju iet”scene in the villaon LakeComo.
Mr. Lovell, as the lover, is long-legged, his trousers
turned up his coat collar lifted, looking like a
secretary-bird stepping about with a kind of high-
shouldered grace. Mr. Lovell’s elocution suggests
a. lack of palate, and he nearly wrecks the last
tragic scene with Iris when he drops his head,
witn its incipent bald spot, on a cushion fair over
against the audience. Of course, men go bald at
25, but the young stage-lover shouldn’t dare to do
so. Iris is just the kind of woman who would see
a man’s whole life’s love go down in a day if there
were cake-crumbs on his mo. at the psychological
moment.
Frederick Maldonado, Spain in his blood, is
the more lovable of the two lovers. He is the
kind of savage that would, awhile back, club the
lady of his choice and drag her to his nuptial cave
by the hair ; nowadays he uses a cheque-book, and
the traction is quite as sure and more seemly.
“Iris” is beautifully staged, particularly the
villa on the lake of Como —the forget-me-not blue
of lake and sky, the white houses in the green
dusk of the hillsides ; the suggestion of sleep and
heat without the villa, and of passion within. It’s
a Swinburnian atmosphere.
Iris doesn’t give Mrs. Brough any remarkable
dress chances. She gets poor in a most becoming
gown of grey cloth with touches of pink, and she
has a blue evening dress with a white underdress,
but the black embroidery on it narrows her
shoulders. She has also a very sweet, fashionably-
made, black crSpe de chine in which to bitterly
regret the past. I’ve seen persons going through
a potsherd period in worse clothes.
There’s a trifle of really cunning business in the
first act, when the excited, love-stricken Iris impul-
sively scribbles the fatal note to Maldonado. She
rushes it into the envelope, seizes a jonquil out of
a vase, and, with the wet stalk, feverishly damps
the flap and seals it down. I tried it this morning,
hurry and all, but didn’t get up any thrill, as I
was only writing to the gas-man to oome and stop
the leak.
Gov. Rawson has been speaking at a Salvation
Army function, and amongst the wooden beads
which he strung was this : “ Only the sick know
what it is to feel that there is a kind man or
woman sitting at one’s bedside reading the gospel.
Bible-reading and prayer at the bedside come
home to the sick.” Now who manufactured that
for His Ex. ? I feel sure he’s too honest to go in
for brummagem himself, and wouldn’t be sur-
prised if it were Capt. Holman. Fancy someone
with a voice like a nutmeg-grater reading those
portions of the Bible which deal with Predestina-
tion to a convalescent! Think of the voice of the
average Salvationist and of his elocution. Think
of having the Bible read to you when you want to
sleep. If the Gov. practises what he preaches I
can understand why Mr. Rowe isn’t getting well
in a hurry. Capt. Watt had better stop intoning
Job at him.
“The Queen’s Coronation dress has arrived
from Paris,” writes the Soldene, “in charge of
four French modistes. The dress is of cloth of
gold, veiled with cream silk muslin, and traced
with Indian embroidery sent from India by Lady
Curzon •, embroidery on tulle, of roses, shamrocks
and thistles, marvellously worked in gold and
silver thread. The train of royal purple velvet is
twenty yards long.” Five feet five of queen—
twenty yards of tail! On the Queen’s accession,
a score of English writers told us that she
was using her personal influence to induce local
royalties and peeresses to get their Coronation
gowns made in England, and that she herself
would certainly set the example. Now we hear
the dress, body and bones and tail, comes from
Paris (and India).
Local musical critics and musicians are trying
to make up by the warmth of their praise for the
coldness of the public to ’cellist Gerardy. The
gublic somewhat shamefacedly talks about there
eing no money in the place, that our exports
have all ceased, that we are going to be dependent
for beef and mutton on M.L. and the Argentine,
and that now (even to-day) there is a Federal
vessel coming into Woolloomooloo wharf with
2500 sheep baa-ing on deck and the same number
frozen below, and that the outlook is, etc. Then
it runs and books seats for “San Toy.” Any-
how, if Gerardy has lost heavily by his Australian
tour he has gained something : he is engaged to a
nice girl, Australian, handsome, musical and well-
dowered, and daughter of one of the most popular
and charming women in our country.
On her return from Brisbane, Mr. J. C.
Williamson will allow Miss Amy Castles to give
a concert in Sydney Town Hall in aid of the
sufferers by the Mt. Kembla mine disaster. Amy
has been “doing” the minor towns of the
Commonwealth.
Melba’s outcoming is thus told by the British
Australasian (whose somewhat gorgeous story
gets considerable contradiction from an authorita-
tive quarter): “Four cabins will be knocked
into one for her use, and her own grand piano
will be fixed on board. She will travel with her
own chef, two secretaries, and her usual retinue
(what on earth is that?). A supply of wine is
also being shipped to Australia for her use, and
her father, Mr. David Mitchell, is having a
superb team of four horses specially trained for
her use.” This horse item sounds as though she
was going to do something in a circus.
Kindly Captain Cargill, who used to command
old R.M.S. Australia, which ran between San
Fran, and Auckland, died last week at Neutral
Bay, Sydney, at the age of 61. After a long in-
terlude of life on land, he returned to sea work
entering the employ of the Pacific Island Co.’
Only the week before last he was appointed one
of the assessors to the Court of Marine Inquiry.
His wife, well-known and esteemed for her philan-
thropic work, is one of the daughters of the late
Captain Moodie.
Our old friend, Louis “ Torpedo ” Brennan is
again an inventor. Not a slaughter-engine this
time, but a leg-saver, a contrivance which is a
special kind of steel rail to assist in mounting
upstairs. The handle is grasped and a button at
its base pressed by the thumb. The handle at
once proceeds upwards, parallel to the hand-rail,
mu in £f a very great help in making the ascent.
Ihe degree of pressure brought to bear by the
thumb on the button of the handle regulates the
pace. You can rush up or go slowlv. On being
released the handle returns of its own accord to
the foot of the stairs. When the contrivance is in
use perhaps the two-storeyed house won’t be such a
horror to the “general” and housemaid.
My Brisbane correspondent writes
“ One remarkable feature of Brisbane Hospital
reports this year is that “The Lady Bowen”
(maternity), “The Children’s,” and the “Lady
Lamington,” managed and controlled by women
doctors and nurses, and financed by women, can
purchase additional space, show substantial bank-
balances, and yet keep the institutions thoroughly
equipped with up-to-date appliances.
“Queensland’s Governor apparently dislikes
ceremony of any kind, and spends a good deal of
time in commanding or imploring the public to sit
down. However, the public is a oeremony-loving
one and bobs up serenely every time His _ Ex.
appears on the scene. It sits down when it is
told, of course, but the lesson never lasts long, and
he will have a busy time drilling his people in the
way-at-ease he wishes them to go. Lucky for the
buoyant Governor his aide was born old and acts
as ballast when Sir Herbert’s youthful heart
Brompts him to any injudicious enthusiasm.
aptain Harman doesn’t dance, doesn’t golf,
doesn’t play polo, isn’t gregarious, either where
women or men are concerned, and is blas4 to a
degree ; and yet this hardened cynic has the pink-
and-white coloring which, out here at any rate,
only belongs to the very youthful. He is the sort
of man you'd call ‘ sonny ’ on his appearance, and
* your reverence ’ on his conversation.
My Melbourne correspondent writes:—
“Once more there is a postponement of the
Vice-regal shivoo. The cards for the Coronation
evening spree were out for Friday (Bth), but, a
police carnival interfering, the affair was post-
poned until Monday evening (11th). It is puzzling
Melbourne to know why a police jollification
should interfere with Lord Tennyson’s entertain-
ment. Do his guests need so much watching ?
“The vogue of the draped dress is augured by
all the prophetesses of the chiffon world, yet the
one worn by Mdlle. Dolores at her first concert
scarcely commended itself. But the dear, delight-
ful Dolores is over-stout for classic drapery, and
the sun-set red material, with its sparkling of
silver stars, that swathed her figure and was
looped up on the left side over a fall of wide
white silk frills, was scarcely suitable. It
was a variegated gown, with ancient bead-fringe
dangling over the fat part of the arm, a lace
drape hanging despond ingly from the corsage,
and some severe red and pink camellias fastened
in at the side. A full square yoke and thick collar
rather gave a bunchy effect to the body, and the
precious throat appeared (though without cause)
to be muffled up. The afternoon harvest of
flowers reaped by the artiste was a particularly
fine one.
“ The sweetness of Dolores’ smile lingers long
in the mind, when the ear has lost the flow of her
notes. Who can challenge this superb artist for
radiant good humor? In repose, the fresh, round
face has some of the qualities of the pictured
Madonnas, but when it lights up with smiles, an
expression of rare roguishness and overflowing
happiness is uppermost. Her platform ease is a
study in B. Natural. She rests squarely on her
feet, lets her arms fall naturally, and, waiting for
the song cue, stands a picture of quiet ease, sim-
plicity, and almost lazy grace. Dolores is the
antipodes to the ‘ intense ’ artiste.
“Lady Tennyson is Said to have drawn a long
face over the extensive working-quarters of the
‘big glass house on the hill.’ She is consulting the
oracle of economy, as to the means of best keeping
within the narrow margin of allowances. She can
learn a thing or two from the manner of the Ston-
ington reg me, where only half the house is kept
in daily use.
“The Tennysons have started on a rigorous
course of duties. In spite of the outting winds,
sharp showers, and bleakness of Saturday, their
Exes, put in an appearance at the Caulfield Races.
The Gov. Clarkes were there, too, and a few blue-
nosed, Polar-furred, dull Sassiety flu'tterers faced
the music for their sakes.
“Nellie Stewart’s best advt. of her recovery to
health, after a lung-trouble had laid her up for
repairs, was made by her gorgeous appearance in
town on Thursday and Friday. An exquisite grey
outfit was the garb of Thursday, a symphony in
smoke-grey, uninterrupted by any other color.
For ornament, it depended on a very hip-expand-
ing cut, with wide French hems circling the waist
and continuing steadily to the skirt-edge. The
bodice was all deep hems, in modified Russian
style. A grey felt hat of plateau build was poised
rather precipitately over the forehead. Nellie
Stewart carried nothing in her hands, letting her
brief train take care of itself. It is only one
woman in a hundred who can carry empty hands
with any degree of grace. The Stewart is one.
“Flowers and applause accentuated the warmth
of ‘Sweet Nell’s’ welcome back to Melb. (August
2nd). Nellie Stewart, a shade thinner in the face
than when here last, now impersonates a sweeter,
less boisterous Gwynne than of yore. Sentiment,
rather than swagger, flavors the ‘ Orange Girl’s ’
latest phase. Nellie Stewart’s new spinnet cos-
tume, like turquoises showered on pink-lily petals,
is almost as artistic (especially when the magic of
the limelight merges its pinks and blues into a
rosy blush, shot with azure) as its predecessor,
which suggested sunrise on a strand of opal and
pearl.
“Charles 11., according to chronicles of his
time, ‘invented the pastime of sauntering.’
Judging by stage samples of his Court, he popu-
larised the queerest brand of laughter ever heard.
Harcourt Beattie, as the Merry Monarch, tinkles
off like a delirious mandoline. Albert Gran’s
bogey man version of Jeffreys splutters and
guffaws himself nearly into epileptic hysterics.
Lovelace hee-haws, and even ‘Sweet Nell’ takes
a joke noisily. Maybe, sotto voce glee came into
vogue later.
“Recent pictures of King Ned recall many
features and much of the expression that Henry
VIII. wears in that famous painting in the
National Gallery, where his royal Nibs bends in
courtly appeal over the fair, tranquil hand of
Anne Boleyn. The Falstaffian chest is the same;
the heavy features, the rather engulfed eyes, the
fleshy cheeks, the brown beard and hair, the whole
expression suggests resemblances to Henry the
Amorous which haunt one.
“Rumors of a unique-looking cancelling of the
marriage vows reach society ears. The pair in
question parted on the day, some 13 years ago,
the vows of eternal love, honor and obedience
were breathed. Since then, the woman, who is
very lovely and well-dowered, lived in the most
exclusive and circumspect single-maiden state.
The man left Australia and passed his existence
according to his fancy. At last the shackles have
been torn free, and certain R.C. religious scruples
set at rest. The freed beauty resumes her old
name, but takes on the prefix * Mrs.’ The sanc-
tion of the Pope was gained before a Court of
Justice loosened the bonds.
“Many incongruities about that ‘Circus Girl’
show vex the playgoer’s soul. One is the idiotic
sight of the lost, stolen or strayed convent girl
(Carrie Moore) squatting on a work-a-day trunk in
the middle of a busy Paris thoroughfare. She
evinces no surprise at her stranded position, and
when her sweetheart heaves in sight (he is the only
man in Paris that she knows) still she evinces no
honest surprise. And when a cuddlesome duet
ensues, the convent chick carries her part of the
hug off with a oonfidenoe, a proficiency, and a
girl-of-the-world air that send all preconceived
notions about convents into a cocked hat. There
is no sincerity in the art of Carrie Moore. She is
amazingly clever in some ways, but the part of the
ingenue she will never present with any truth.
A covert, knowing, audacious smile lurks about
her mouth all through every performance, and as
a Convent Girl she is not even an amusing
caricature.
“The troubles of the Women’s Hospital Cat-
mittee are as yet in their infancy. The resigna-
tions of Mrs. Gresswell and Mrs. Nicholson are
viewed with alarm by those in the know. Those
two matrons were intelligent and humane. The
most genial of all the Catmittee was Mrs. F.
Lang, and that cheery dame resigned months ago,
when the dominant party began to show its claws.
“ The evils of amateur mumming begin to come
to a head. The other day an aspiring Irving, who
is deserting his commercial concerns for mimic
stagecraft, figured in the insolvency lists. Proud
man can’t be dressed in a little brief stage beauty
without paying for it.
“ Melbourne’s wealthiest bachelor has just re-
turned from Harbourville with the news of his
engagement to one of its charmers.
“Rev. Walter Fellows, recently dead, spent
most of his life as incumbent of Toorak’s most
fashionable temple. Mr. Fellows, a handsome,
stout, upstanding cleric, was a survival of the type
of reverend who saw no harm in a game of whist,
a good dinner and the agreeable things of life,
generally, so long as he manfully did his duty,
according to his own conception thereof. Two of
Mr. Fellows’ pretty daughters made excellent
marriages; one is Mrs. Millear, of ‘ Edgarley,’
near Ararat, the other is Mrs. Bailey.
“ There was much writing on shirt-fronts at a
dinner party in a Collins-street cafe the other
night, when certain inter-State sports feasted with
the Mayor and found Sir Sam merry of tongue.
The linen-marking was introduced by a South
Australian, and, before the dinner ended, every
bit of starched ‘ dicky ’ was closely covered with
hieroglyphic characters and sketches. One shirt
front, a perfect gallery of autographs, is to be
framed and preserved as a quaint curio in linen

“An Adelaide Demosthenes in petticoats is in
Melbourne agitating her sisters to establish a
‘political economy’ centre, from which several
branches may emanate in extension fashion. This
vigilant person wants womankind to prepare to
use the suffrage wisely and well, by an under-
standing of matters political. But it is a mere
fragment of the feminine community that takes
the suffrage seriously or as a step to better things.

service and ba,nk-clerk swains. The glory of the
returned contingenter has long since passed away.

what of a silly. There was a conspicuous instance
of it on the Block t’other day, when a big-
shouldered soldier and a rather narrow-chested
dandy walked bn either side of a sprightly ‘ bit-
of-muslim’ This consequential Miss had all eyes
for the narrow youth, and left the * hero ’ unre-
garded by her side.
“Lieut. A. Christie, one of those recommended
by Lord Kitchener for war honors, was a G.P.O.
clerk with a genius for engineering picnics. He
had sociable ideas, and was for ever fruitlessly
endeavouring to amalgamate the he-clerks and
she-elerks in a common camaraderie. When he
shouldered his musket for the war the fair postal
girls gave him a^cheery send-off.

WATSON’S WHISKY.
PERSONAL ITEMS.
Only one N.S.W. Governor (Sir Robert
Duff) has died in office.
Anglican Archdeacon Bushtrotter is organ-
ising a missionary visit to the Q. far-western
districts. What’s in a name ?
In a legal confab at Sydney, last week,
Josiah Symon was mooted as the alternative to
Barton as Chief Justice of the Commonwealth.
T. B. Davison, who died lately at Maldon
(Vic.), claimed to have brought out and erected
the first mining plant used on Bendigo goldfield.
“ Sydney,” says an American who is dis-
gusted at the want of public spirit amongst its
rich people, “contains the most impecunious lot
of millionaires I ever struck.”
Rev. Matthew Walker, gaoled for perjury,
may be the starter of a new industry for women
in the baekblocks. His wife has been appointed
to do his clerical duties during his enforced
absence.
Vic. Leg. Assembly now contains three
Williams, three Camerons, two Billsons, two
Harris, and two Masons, but no Jones, and only
one Smith. Formerly, there were four Smiths—
viz. “Majah,” R. M., L. L., and Tom.
Mr. H. Noyes is the name of the Vic.
electrical engineer who is advising Minister Bent
re the conversion of Vic. suburban railways
traotion power. After all, G. H. Reid was not
absolutely original even as the great “ No-Yes.”
The hospitable squatter Frank Jenkins,
known well to most Murrumbidgee whalers, died
at 83. Sixty-seven years ago his father and him-
self, then 16, built the station home of Buckin-
bong, near Narrandera, and Frank lived there till
his death.
Morehead, the one-time brilliant “ 8.D.”
and general devil-raiser of Q. Leg. Assembly, later
Premier, later unlucky banker, later still dodder-
ing political canvasser, was the other day unsuc-
cessfully nominated for Chairman of Committees
m Leg. Council.
The “fighting man ” of Queensland Assem-
bly is beyond doubt Minister for Railways Leahy.
“ Janny ” carries a fair-sized political stone in the
heel of his Parliamentary stocking, and the trail
of his coat is all over the floor. What a time he
would have at a Donnybrook !
The lie heroic to the last. On Sunday,
June 22, the very day before the King’s collapse,
the Press Association wired to Windsor as to the
persistent rumors about his ill-health. And the
official reply from the private secretary was
“ Not a word of truth in reports.”
Mrs. Henry Lawson is the latest to bear
testimony to the good heart of Earl Beauchamp,
who has constituted himself protector-in-general
of Australians in England. During a spell of
trouble, the N.S.W. ex-Governor’s sympathy
with the Lawsons was practical and generous.

The voluntary surrender of portions of
their salaries by certain Queensland officials must
make other officials seek a cool place for thought.
How does “Sam” Griffith feel? Sam’s Govern-
ment helped Lilley out of the Chief Justice’s
boots and put Sam’s feet into them at a huge
increase on Lilley’s salary. What says Sam to
the Ghermsides and others ?

of his going to England, is the same politician
who denied N.S.W.’s intention to raise a fresh

Obit. : Michael Victor Cunningham, land-
owner, of Tyaak, near Bradford (Vic.), aged 89.
Deceased fought as a lieut. in the war between
U.S.A. and. Mexico in the early 40.’s, reached
Port Philip in ’5l, and was pioneer on the Ovens,
Reedy Creek and Bowman’s Forest diggings,
being the finder of the last-named.
Pastor Bevan, of the Independent Church,
Collins-st., Melb. has a collection of Australian
books—works written in or on Australia —which
is much envied by bibliophiles. Gone are the
days when anything like a complete collection of
Australian books could be acquired short of the
expenditure of thousands of pounds.
Mr. George, the new Westralian Minister
of Railways, opened well at a recent meeting of
railway-hands, by addressing the audience as
“ I ellow-employees.” He had got half through
his first sentence when a voice from the back of
the hall remarked: “ Feller-employees ! Yes, an’
’e makes 30 quid a-week, an’ we get 30 blanky
bob! ”
The Bulletin regrets to hear that Trea-
surer Bird, of Tasmania, is in very poor health.
Heart trouble is said to be the chief cause, vari-
cose veins and general weakness some of the
effects. During his two hours’ financial statement
in las. Assembly the other night Mr. Bird stood
on one leg, resting the other on a knee-high
padded chair.

Mary Clancy, a native of Ireland, who had
resided 60 years in Victoria, died in Ballarat
Benevolent Asylum, t’other day. The supt. of
the institution says he possesses documentary
evidence that she was over 107 years old. The
trouble in such cases is to make sure that the de-
ceased is the person mentioned in the document.
Present writer. feels at his prime at the age of 82—-
at least the birth-certificate with his name on it
says he is 82. The fact that it is his father’s cer-
tificate is a detail.

of “A Boer of To-day,”

Lands Dept. He had
been to S’Africa as lieu-

exceeded his Govt, leave.
Hence retrenchment with-

sins was always fighting

when he threatened
once again to make

climbed down, and was
glad to be rid of su,ch a

several months leave on
full pay. Cossins fought
with the Boers against the
Swazis over 20 years ago;
was made almost totally deaf
through the report of a brother Boer’s rifle, and
got badly belted on the head with a knobkerry.
“Melb.”: Died recently, Mat Mitchell, one
of the best known commercial men here for over 40
years. He was almost the last of a body of
masterful men who lived for business and enjoy-
ment, and got all that life has to give out of both.
Such were our mercantiles of the old era,
and you need only conjure up names like Dalgety,
Degraves, Goldsbrough, Grice, Moore, Nankivell,
Service, Sumner. To be sure, “they cut out the
blubber” under a system which they devised
themselves for their own advantage. Their suc-
cessors of to-day are nervous men, fearful even to
touch a glass of wine.
THE REAL GOVERNOR-GENERAL.
LordKetchener, of Mahdi’s-head notoriety,
seems to be an incurable vandal. Cabled that he
has presented to the Royal Engineers’ Museum statues
of Kruger, Botha, and Cronje, from Pretoria, and a
statue of Steyn from Bloemfontein.
This robbery of the Boers’ statuary can’t be
excused on the ground of any military necessity,
and is, whatever light it is looked at in, sheer
barbarism.
The veteran N.S.W. Auditor-General Ren-
nie, whom the Cabinet is so anxious to muzzle, is a
Presbyterian of the old dour type. He was one
of the founders of St. Stephen’s Church, in
Phillip-st., Sydney, but seceded from its congre-
gation when the unholy “ Kust of Whustles ” was
installed, and went over to St. George’s, where
the solemn gloom of undiluted Calvinism is not
disturbed by music.
Said that Mr. F. B. Freehill, who com-
bines the roles of solicitor, Consul for Spain,
military officer, and high-priest of the Irish party in
N.S.W'., has been asked by the military authori-
ties to give an “explanation ” of his presence at the
recent Sydney Irish Homeßulemeeting. If this be
correct, Major-General Hutton is evidently very
anxious indeed for trouble. Volunteer and par-
tially-paid officers have hitherto not been debarred
from politics in Australia.
The late Detective Keatinge had good and
bad luck pretty evenly distributed in his time.
For years hp was the big gun of the Service, and
was despatched near and far through N.S.W. In
his best days he was speculative and imaginative.
He attended spiritualistic seances, and on serious
cases regularly employed mediums of that cult.
In hunts after murderers he was a terror a quarter
of a century ago. Towards the end of his career
he devoted himself chiefly to the Chinese.
By the death of C. L. Dobbin, aged 67,
Victoria loses a painstaking P.M. He once re-
marked to present writer that the accused who
stood in the worst position in a British court was
the man charged with exposure ; and that, for his
own part, he would rather be tried for murder,
the former allegation being most difficult of dis-
proof, though the circumstances were often quite
innocent. This was before charges of assault
against men by flighty females had become so rife.

The man of Geil, now nasal-snuffling in the
Good Cause in Sydney, doesn’t even take the
trouble to make his scriptural allusions fitting.
T’other day he wanted to urge men to “ enthrone
purity and cast out impurity,” and told the
scriptural story of how Adonijah (representing

“ F.” : Henry Lawson discovers after all
these years that Lord Beauchamp didn’t send that
“birthstains message” to N.S.W. What non-
sense ! Beauchamp wasn’t at all disinclined to
discuss the matter here and he never denied
authorship. View that he took was that what was
written by Kipling as a compliment to Sydney,
accepted at the time without protest as a compli-
ment, couldn’t very well be turned into an insult
by his quoting it. Undoubtedly Beauchamp
meant well and for the .life of me I could never see
any reason for all the fuss about the “ birthstains.”
Will someone take Charles Hardie Buzacott
by the ear and lead him back to the old dart?
When he left the composing-room of S.M. Herald
and started his little Peak Downs paper nearly 40
years ago, he was a democrat to his soleless boots.
He got to Rockhampton, into Parliament, into
the Ministry, into Brisbane Courier, every step
an intellectual descent, until he landed M.L.C.
He is back again in Rockhampton now with
another little sheet, but he edits it from the poli-
tical M.L.C. abyss. “God did not make men
equal,” he chatters in a late issue, and by that
sorry phrase thinks he wipes out trades-unionism
and democracy in all its forms. It seems too late
in the day to reason with Charles Hardie Buza-
cott.
* The lately-lamented Governor-General was
always a tired man, and whenever he tried to
shine as an energetic official he became knocked
out, and had to “bunk” for recuperation. One
Australian artist who had to deal with Hopetoun
found him a shocking sitter—so languid that he
couldn’t hold up his head long enough to give the
man behind the brush half a chance. Indigestion
was then the dominant weakness, and at length,
after the third attempt, when the vice-regal sitter
broke away, and said he would really have to be
excused, but he positively couldn’t sit any longer
that day, the artist got mad, and U3ed most un-
diplomatic expressions, including a remark to the
effect that his Who’s-this needn’t bother to come
there again. That shock put his lordship to bed for
a week. “As a sitter,” says the artist, “His Ex.
hadn’t the natural ability of an old hen.”
Melba’s relatives write anxiously to Mel-
bourne papers explaining that London paragraphs
about the proposed ostentatiousness and magnifi-
cence of the return of the famous Australienne are
not at all near the mark. Melba has no intention
of trying to eclipse the royal visitation of the
Yorks, and is coming in modest array, with but
two attendants. The denial of the assertion that
Madame was bringing a shipment of wine to
entertain with is particularly warm. These
explanations look as if devised to modify great
expectations. In short, it is to be understood
that Nellie is coming to sing, not to shout !
Melb. demise: Albert Read, aged 90 years.
Deceased was the living embodiment of the melo-
dramatic stage-lawyer—white hair, chubby red
cheeks, clean-shaven, exquisitely charming man-
ners, dressed immaculately in traditional black
broad-cloth; had a stoop —(half obeisance and half
familiarity). He was a great and successful crimi-
nal advocate, and a fitting complement as attor-
ney to such counsel as Aspinall and Ireland, and
he was associated with most of the celebrated
old-time Vic. cases, including the principal
gold robberies ; and “ many a burglar ” Mr. Read
“ restored to his friends and his relations.” The
deceased was the defending attorney in the Haw-
thorn murder case, which created a stir some years
ago. The murderer shot his brother at Brighton,
and was brought to justice by some extraordinarily
clever black-tracking. At the first trial the pre-
siding Judge occupied three hours in his charge to
the jury, which disagreed. At the second a
merciless summing-up of 20 minutes by the then
Chief-Justice Stawell resulted in a verdict of
guilty after an hour’s deliberation.
Concerning our coming visitor, Mme.
Melba
For the next three or four months Australia will have
to endure newspaper statements of all the silly things
that silly people have said about Melba, likewise a num-
ber of things they didn’t say. For instance, the remark
made by an American musical critic when somebody im-
plored the prima-donna to seek shelter from the falling
snow what time she was waiting outside the theatre for
her carriage after a concert. Said the weak-minded
journalist in question, “ Ah, you see, Melba’s is a God-
given voice, and God will not let a little cold spoil it.”
What nauseating stuff ! Fact is that the development
of Melba’s naturally excellent physique was the result of
her healthy tom-boy girlhood. At Lilydale (Vic.), where
her father long ago built a country home for the town-
bred family, Melba is remembered as a girl who got
about in all weathers, hatless sometimes, and never
“ wrapped up.” In hardening her chest and throat the
lively Nellie unconsciously fitted herself for a brilliant
musical career. For the rest she was a great worker as
soon as she had made tip her mind to become what she
now is. When she was in Paris studying under Mar-
chesi, the robust young woman from Australia would
devour musical education from a book whilst she swal-
lowed her breakfast.
“ H.P.” sends a Victorian news-clipping
with an addendum :
PATHETIC SUICIDE.
Unable to Pay His Debt.
Harrow, Saturday.
A respected resident named William Burrill com-
mitted suicide on the 24th inst., by shooting himself
with an old muzzle-loading gun, the charge lodging in the
brain and causing instant death.
At the magisterial inqui'-y held by Mr. W. B. Edgar, it
was elicited that financial troubles were evidently the
cause of the rash act, and a very sad aspect of the tragedy
was presented by the production of a letter written the
night before, which after setting out fully particulars of
his debts, with, a list of creditors, went on to say that he
was ashamed of meeting, day after day, people among
whom he had spent half his life, and recognised that the
only way of being able to pay them was by taking his
life, as the sale of his property and effects, at present,
would more than discharge his liabilities, and time might
render this impossible. The deceased was a widower, 73
years of age, 35 years of which he had spent in Harrow.
A verdict of death from a gun-shot wound inflicted by
himself was returned.
“Poor old Billy Burrill!” remarks our cor-
respondent ; “ writer happened to know him.”
For years, he yvas almost blind, yet he refused charity,
but worked at his trade (carpenter and odd-job man) even
when he had to feel for his tools, and must needs have
fixed spots for each, that he might know where to put his
hands on them. Increasing years added to his difficul-
ties in the way of making enough to pay his way. He
then got behind, and saw but one way of paying his
debts—and took it. It is fashionable to erect memorials
to celebrate worthy men and deeds. Here is a fit subject,
surely. What a lesson for young Australia. “He died
to pay his debts ! ”

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors.— 7n the ease of ethnological and
natural-history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific value.
“ Werrimee ” : Some hard things are being
said over the decision of the A.W.U. to increase
the shearing rates to 255. per 100. The squatters
argue that, as the men have hitherto be ensatisfied
with2os., thedemand for a2sper cent, rise just now,
during the worst time ever experienced, and on
top of heavy sheep losses, is simply monstrous, and
looks like an attempt to jump on the squatter when
he’s down. The real reason of the demand is this:
(1), the number of sheep being greatly reduced,
there is less inducement for shearers to go out
back than formerly ; (2), feed being nil, and water
almost so, travelling per horse is impossible, and
per foot difficult and dangerous ; (3), a serviceable
bike that will carry a heavy man and swag over
long rough roads, means a big cheque to a shearer
just now, and the chance then of a breakdown 30
miles from water ; (4), the co3t of living, even
along the lines, has increased enormously
since last ' year, and in places that depend
for supplies _on teams and camels, the
price of stores.is almost prohibitive. Under such
conditions, with small sheds and bad shearing,
the men will have little enough to show at the
end of the season even at the higher rates. Out
Winton way 30s. has been paid for years, good
seasons and bad ; and 30s. all over N.S.W. at the
present time would be more in accordance with a
proper adjustment of things than a continuance of
the usual rate's.
SOMETHING ABORIGINAL.
THE BARMAID (to the station-hand): “And what's yours?"
THE DOG-S TIFFENER: “ Oh, something aboriginal. Gin or Boomerang.”
“ D.H.R.” : A novel experiment in sheep-
feeding. Mr. J. P. J. Bell of Flemington, near
Walgett, N.S.W., last year cultivated some 50
acres of sorghum, on which he has been feeding
5000 ewes and lambs since last December, and it is
likely to last for some time yet. The success of
Mr. Bell clearly proves the value of bores to the
way-back settler.
• * * •
The few squatters who have fat stock for
sale just now are reaping a rich harvest. A mob
of bullocks from Barnes’ Tabulam (N.S.W.)
estate realised up to £2O per head at Homebush
recently ; and a mob of cows from same place
brought £l2 per head. The station-price of latter
in fair seasons ranges from two to four pounds.
* * * *
Hand-feeding on the majority of inland
stations has already cost some shillings per sheep;
for example, one place has spent £50,000 in pre-
serving 180,000 sheep. If the drought continues
the tucker-bill will exceed the value, and what
then ? It is a fallacy that store ewes will become
worth over £1 per head, because they can be im-
ported at that price.
• * * *
A little bush picture :
Travelling, last week, from Condobolin to Landsdale, I
struck Jim Merriel’s camp at Minalong Tank, Melrose.
There was the customary fenced-in tank and tent. The
fence was decorated with innumerable sheepskins, in all
stages of decomposition, and there were also two piles of
dead wool. Half-a-dozen sheep, rescued from the tank,
had been fed for a week, and were making feeble attempts
to profess to be alive. Jim himself was hanging round,
with his hands covered in blood from recent skinning
operations. The only things about with much life were
thousands of crows in the trees, eyeing the feast below.
Told by a traveller for station-supplies,
writing from Farina (S.A.), that, on a single
waterhole on Cooper’s Creek, there are the bones
of no less than 30,000 cattle and horses, the bleach-
ing records of a dozen droughts.
* * • •
While cutting a 40-acre crop near Coon-
amble (N.S.W.) lately, a reaper-and-binder
chopped up between 400 and 500 rabbits.
• • • *
Carriers in Queensland are a luckless race
just now. It costs 255. a week to feed a horse in
Ilughenden—as the Hughenden-Riehmond line
extension engineers recently ascertained. To go
from Warwick to Goondiwindi costs a carrier £2l
in fodder alone while the carriage of an average
load runs about £24 ... £3 left for the unfortunate
driver. Many a carrier winds up his trip on a
deficit and has then to solve the problem of how
to live on a minus quantity.
• i • •
The latest dog lie—in Richmond River
Express :
A farmer who had sold a flock of sheep to a dealer lent
him a dog to drive them a distance of 30 miles, desiring
him to give the dog a meal at the journey’s end, and tell
it to go home. The drover found the dog so useful that
he resolved to steal it, and, instead of sending it back,
locked it up. The animal grew sulky, and at last effected
its escape. Evidently deeming the drover had no more
right to detain the sheep than he had to detain itself, the
dog went into the field, collected all the sheep that had
belonged to his master, and drove the whole flock home
again.

An abori-
ginal idyl:
The sky had
clouded over,
black. Glim-
mers of livid
lightning came
and went upon
the gloom, and
thunder
growled omin-
ously in the
distance. Some-
thing was com-
ing.
Mother said it
was going to be
the rain of her
life. We hoys
were laughing
and runn i n g
round her, as if
she were a hay-
stack. She was
looking by
turns at the
miserable
“ crop ” and at
the heavens,
when Dad came
along.
“ Why don’t
yer stand on the
fence?” said
he, sarcasti-
cally.
“ Because you
ain’t the stuff to
make a fence
that ’ud carry
me ! ” retorted
mother.
Dad snorted,
walked over to
the line of tubs
and buckets
ranged care-
fully under the
spouting, and
snatched up a
shabby bell-
topper.
“ What’s this?"
he asked, grim-
ly-
“ Your ’at,”
faltered Tim,
guiltily.
“So I believe,”
returned Dad,
edging closer.
“ But what did
you put it there
for ? Quick 1"
Dad snapped Jimmy by the neck, but mother chipped
in. “ ’Ere, y’ old pig ! let go o’ that boy, or I’ll drive this
into yer face ” —holding her clenched fist under Dad’s
nose. Dad pushed it away, and mother hugged him,
tripped him up, and down they went together, mother
on top. Mather can wrestle like a man—a big one.
When she had Dad safely pinned under her, she dis-
coursed.
“You’ve been sneerin’and chuCkin’-off a lot, haven’t
you ? ” she said. “ Well, now, if you think you can get
up, chuck-off some more ! ” —and mother grinned.
“Let’s up 1 ” growled Dad.
“ When you admit that it’s goin’ to rain, as I said.”
“ I won’t! ”
“ You won’t ? ” Mother lifted his head by one whisker,
and bumped the ground hard with his skull. “ Now
will yer ? ”
“ Orright! I will—an’ I 'ope yer gets washed horff the
blanky selection ! ” hissed Dad.
Mother gave him a final jab on the nose, and then let
him up.
And presently the rain did come down, like rivers—the
first rain, to speak of, that we boys had ever seen.
So Mother was right. Even Dad had to acknowledge
that, when, in climbing the ladder to fix up the spouting,
his foot slipped off the wet rung, and he flopped into 50
gallons of water !
Mother laughed, and said it was Dad’s first bath since
they’d been married.

“Hippo ” to “ Albo” :
If I found a sapling with a fork sft. up, and bored holes
in both limbs, as you suggest (B. 26/7’02), I should look
for the holes in after years at the same distance from the
ground, and also the fork, ’which would not be 10ft. up,
but sft. as before. If trees carried their limbs up as they
grew, what a lot of young spindle-shanked trees there
would be ! Heard a parson once relate a story (moral,
alas ! forgotten) of a boy who marked his height on a sap-
ling, and, a year after, was horrified to find the mark
above his head—concluding he had shrunk. Had I been
that boy, I’d have been horrified, too. I suppose “ Albo ”
would have merely concluded that the tree grew faster
than he did.

THE SITTER : “ What ah awful thing it would be, Mr. Madder,
were you to become blind!"
MR. MADDER : “ Not so bad as you think —critics are well paid.”
“Trotter” : The once-prosperous town of
Goondiwindi (Q.) is fast assuming a “deserted
village ” aspect. The stations around will soon be
completely depleted of stock ; and the poor selec-
tor on his 100-acre patch is a woeful spectacle,
struggling to keep starving beasts alive on boiled
prickly-pear. Drastic tick-regulations prevent
the carrier from crossing the border ; and he also
is in a sad way with his starving horses, many
having died from eating the “pear,” the fibre of
which forms a hard ball in the stomach. Cattlo
thrive for a time on pear, but very soon go off.
The storekeepers keep the few cookies going with
a Micawber-1 ike trust.

vomiting after eating strychnine-treated baits.

“Ashton”: To “Hypo” (8., 9/17/’O2).
At Winks selection, Surat Road (Q.), a hole was,
some years ago, bored in a pine tree and a peg
inserted to fasten horses to. The peg is now
almost out of reach. Again: On a selection
near here an ironbark tree serves as a post in a line
of fence, erected some 10 years back. The tree
has grown, hut has not carried the rails up with
it. Also, I have seen a wire-fence all askew
through trees carrying the wire up with their
growth. Many station-managers specify about
fencing that, where wires run through trees, the
tops must be cut off—balah excepted, which, if
cut, rots and falls out. There 1
* * * 9
This, from the report of Dr. Trenow,
Health Officer at Croydon (Q.), applies to pretty
well every out-back town in Australia :
Germ-laden dirt ot every description is deposited on
our roofs by wind-storms, and hawks also drop bones and
garbage. Householders should therefore arrange that no
rain-water be allowed to enter the tanks until the roofs
be washed clean. All drinking water also should be
boiled before using. Many of the domestic water-tanks
have not been cleaned out for years, and contain dead
frogs and a thick, offensive slime of decomposing, organic
matter.
Yet if you suggest boiled drinking-water, the
residents will say, “Oh, we are all right: we
drink nothing but rain-water .” Most people
seem to think that if water is clear and tasteless
it must be wholesome.

“C.C.” : “ F.M.” (B. 28/6/’O2)
states that tutu-poisoned cattle may be
saved by a fast run up or down hill.
Well, when on a station in Canterbury
(M.L.) some years ago, a mob of 15
milch cows had been eating tutu (pro-
nounced “ toot ”), and, although they
galloped a considerable distance down-
hill to the homestead, careered madly
round for a time, and then rushed off
in different directions, clearing all
fences as they went, 14 of them were
afterwards found dead, some several
miles away. The only one saved was
an old cow which was caught, freely
bled, and suspended for some hours.
Tutu-eating doesn’t hurt horses.
* * *
“ F.M.” : The N.S.W. stock-
route question is cropping up again ;
hut it is not at all sure that Ministry or
Parliament will find brains and courage
enough to tackle it. The reservation
of these stock-routes was one of the big
and creditable things of the men who
are gone. A mile wide, they run from
the Murray to the Queensland boun-
dary, and a lot of money has been spent
on them in tanks and wells. But to
what purpose, when they are leased to
the squatter through whose holdings
they pass, and fed down first when-
ever drought threatens ? Fence them
off, charge a reasonable price for their use, and
much might be saved and gained in various
ways. As things are now, the big idea is robbed
of all use.

“Artemis" : He holes
supposed by “ Prospector ” (B.
19/7/’O2) to have been sunk by
blacks in search of wombat.
Any such holes I have seen
were too narrow to have been
sunk by men. They lead into
large wombat-burrows and are
generally found in stony hill
country. In this district (Up-
per Murray) there have been
no blacks for about 30 years,
yet I recently saw a freshly-
sunk hole. In my opinion
the wombats make them for
air-shafts. As no loose dirt
is ever seen on top it would
seem that the animal works
upwards from the burrow, the
mouth of which is often mucli
below the shaft-opening. And,
anyhow, no blackfellow would
sink holes 23ft. deep after
wombats when he could catch
them just as well by waiting
at the mouth of the burrow.
“Lyn” : Honey-hunting
in the bush once, we came
across an immense bees’-nest
on the outside of a stump.
Large white flakes of comb,
loaded with honey, covered
the whole of one side of the
stump, and were unprotected from the weather
except by trees growing near about. We covered
the nest with a net, sawed the stump off, and
carried the whole away bodily.

Editor Bulletin, — Apropos late par re
Paspalum Dilitatum —Golden Crown grass—re-
commending the planting of this grass in the dry
western districts. We have had this grass planted
for two years, and find it will not thrive without
irrigation, but even with irrigation it is not to be
compared with lucerne or alfalfa in the dry dis-
tricts. In the moist districts the grass will
become a valuable grazing fodder. —H.F.L.

Some 150 half-castes, descendants of the
last of the Tas. aborigines, are just now big with
grievance against local Lands Minister Mulcahy.
Alleged that the Minister has leased Chappie
Island, the chief breeding-place of the succulent
mutton-bird, to a Tas. squatter for grazing pur-
poses, and that the sheep and cattle will destroy
the “rookeries,” and scare the birding industry
away for ever.
“Benjamin Bridge” writes from Glen
Innes
I have noticed my name connected with “ The Ragged
Thirteen.” 1 wish to correct “6x8,” and to do justice
to myself and my fe low-men. In the first place, why
didn’t “6xB” sign his name ? As low as he would make
me out to be, lam not ashamed to sign mine. It is not
true that I was with “ The Ragged Thirteen.” You
Stated that I was supposed to have joined those men
going through the Northern Territory. I never did. And
they were supposed to be union shearers carrying on
cattle-duffing, horse-stealing, and kangaroo-shooting, who
would never dare to show their noses any further inland
than Win ton in Queensland. That also is false. Those
men were not union shearers, but mining prospectors
making their way to Kimberley (W.A.) long before my
time in that part. I wish to speak a word in favor of the
union shearers, who, like myself, have often had a lot
thrown on their shoulders which is not true. Referring
again to “The Ragged Thirteen,” who were making their
way to Kimberley diggings, they ran short of food, and,
some time before I saw those parts, arrived at a station in
the Northern Territory and inquired of the Chinese cook
whether there were provisions in the store or not. He
informed them that three waggon loads had just arrived.
When they asked the squatter for food he said that he
would neither sell nor give food. To which the men
replied, “We have been some time now without food,
and we cannot go any longer,” and they helped them-
selves to what they wanted. They got the name of
“ The Ragged Thirteen ”in this way: When the squatter
asked them their names they said, “ You can call us ‘ The
Ragged Thirteen ’ (they were 13 in number). The name
has stuck to them ever since. I never was with “ The
Ragged Thirteen ” at any time. The good testimonials
that I have to show (they secured my release, and satisfied
his Honor Judge Heydon) should'be enough to satisfy
“6xB” and the general public.

When dugong were plentiful in Moreton and
Wide Bays (Q.) a lucrative business used to be
done in dugong oil and flesli. The latter was
cured like bieon, and was much ahead of cheap
pig-bacon, though having aslightly fishy flavor.

“P.” : I never saw the mirage so st rongly
on theN.S.W. plains as during a recent tour in
the North-West. From Narrabri to Moree great
lagoons, glassy lakes, and gliding streams seem to
surround you. The apparently limitless parched
and fissured black-soil plains, as level as a billiard
table, spread out from the view-point bearing not
one single verdant blade, yet, towards the horizon,
you ever see the alluring water.
* * * *
“Rypot ” : Re mirages. As a locomotive
stoker, I often see a mirage—or something akin.
The sun, shining on the glistening steel ahead,
sometimes makes one big blurr of the two lines of
rails, and if a bird happens to be standing on the
line (magpies often are) it looks like an elephant.
Then as we approach the mirage slowly fades
away. The big blur gradually grows less until the
two rails are again seen, and the “elephant”
shrinks successively to a bullock, calf, dog, and
bird. We never run over a magpie, or a goat—
they are too cunning.
* * * *
“Di. W.” : lie bird - mimicry. On a
mountain-climbing excursion once we came across
three lyre birds —a young one and its parents.
The male bird at once took flight, and the young
one, not able to fly well, perched on a low limb
close to us. Then the mother bird, to divert our
attention, started running up and down in front
of us and mimicking the calls of eaglehawk,
plover, curlew, magpie, and parrot so faithfully
that anyone not seeing her would fancy the bush
alive with birds.
* • • •
“Mutton-Bird”: Once camped under a
solitary tree, occupied by a bell-bird. Presently
his clear note rang our, then came an apparently
distant echo. Couldn’t locate it for a time—not
even a blade of grass to shelter any other per-
former. Watched the bird closely, and found that
the “ echo ” came from him ielf. While echoing
lie kept his hill slightly open and used the throat
only.

“ W.J.” : If “ L.M.” (B. 5/7/’O2) is a
woman, there’s no more to be said, because the

“Prossy” : In the cedar-brush, Copeland
district, N.S.W., there is a dark-green colored
vine with a yam-like root which is very palatable
boiled or roasted. The blacks are fond of it, and
I have found hundreds of vines that had been
worked out by the blacks in past years.
• * * *
“Pyalla”: Near Warialda (N.S.W.) I
lately witnessed a parade of brolgns. For perfect
marching and drill evolutions no trained soldiery
could compare to it. Their marching in line was
superb, but most wonderful of all was the perfect
circles they described when in single file. No
wonder the blacks are proud of saying that the
brolgas learned it from their corroboree dance.
And what on earth will the brolga not eat ? I
have seen him on the stubble fields, catching,
plucking and _ eating birds; and recently, on
Tycannah Station, a flock of them were apparently
feasting on rotten sheep carcases.
* * * *
“J.A.”: Came, t’other day,'to a tank
with about 3ft. of water and found a dozen
emus in it bobbing heads and necks under water
as if bathing. Found, however, that they were
fishing for bony bream ; catching them fast, too,
and swallowing them whole, though I could not
get close enough to see which went down first,
head or tail. Never heard of the emu as a fisher-
fowl before.

“Woomba”: Lately saw a new mode of
starting a bullock that had gone down crossing a
bridge and wouldn’t rise. “Bullocky” got two
sticks and got his offsider to pull the beast’s tail
out taut. He then placed one stick under the
tail and the other above, and rubbed them up and
down the tail smartly. The friction this produced
on a tender spot did the trick, and in less than
lOsecs. that bullock was on his feet, with a look of
surprise in his eyes.

the “go.” hadn’t scoffed the mice I can’t con-
ceive.

“Lar”: When I was running a small
steamer between Brisbane and Cairns, about L 7 ,
years ago, sometimes, owing to delay of boats, the
township would be in a painfully grogless state.
On these occasions a long string of buggies would
be lined up, waiting, to meet the boat; and when
we fetched up at the wharf the people used to
rush the ship, dry up the bar as a preliminary
canter, then present their bills-of-Tading to the
skipper, get their receipts, and, instead of waiting
for the stuff to be discharged, collar the liquor
themselves, load up their buggies, open a case for
the road, and go home happy.

/‘Casa B.” : To “ Credo ” (8., 17/7702).
That the crow (foes eat the milky grain any farmer
in Western Vic. can testify. Prior to the general
use of seed drills, it was common to tar seed-wheat
to save it from the crows—and even the drills do
not bury the seed deep enough to baffle them.
“Crede ” may be scientifically right about the
■exhaustion of the seed’s albumen, but the crow
regardeth not science. He gets to biz. as soon as
the wheat is sown. He will also pull up the young
wheat-shoots. I have often shot and inquested
the black marauders, finding the soft wheat in
their crops. Magpies and sparrows also offend in
this way.
ASHELLING PEAS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
a l the world is rjrecn and bright
Outride the latticed pane ;
The fields are decked with gold and white,
Ami Spring has come again.
But though the world be fair without.
With flow' rs and leaving trees,
’Tis pleasanter to be about
Where Nell's a-shelling peas.
Her eyes are blue as cloudless skies,
And dimples deck her cheeks ; -
Whilst soft lights loiter in her eyes
Whene’er she smiles or speaks.
So all the sunlit morning-tide
I dully at mine ease,
To loaf at slender Nelly’s side
When Nell’s a-shelling peas.
This bard , who sits a-watching Nell,
With fingers white and slim.
Owns up that, as she breaks each shell.
She also “breaks up” him ;
And could devoutly drop upon
Submissive, bended knees
To worship Nell with apron on —•
A saint a-shelling peas.
The tuckcd-up muslin sieves disclose
Her round arms white and bare—
’Tis only “shelling peas” that shows
Those dainty dimples there.
Old earth owns many sights to see
That captivate and please ;
The most bewitching sight for me
Is Nell a-shelling peas.

Edison declares that he has solved the
automobile problem with a new storage battery,
which, even in its present imperfect state, makes
it possible to run 100 miles without re-charging.
The inventor declares that he can improve the
appliance till it will be possible to run at 100
miles an hour for a number of hours, according
to the size of the battery. At a trial the new
battery drove the car at 62 miles per hour, and,
on the return journey, the driver got enthusiastic
and “ let her go ” at the rate of 83 miles,
Here’s a chance for the Owe’Sullivan
Ministry to get the Mitchell Library properly
housed for nothing. Carnegie, the American
millionaire of the bloodstained Pittsburg works,
offered, in a recent after-dinner speech in London,
to pay for the erection of a public library in any
city in the English-speaking world that would
provide a site and guarantee the maintenance of
the institution. The Owe’Sullivan has no politi-
cal objection to,tainted money, or lie would not
have asked for Rhodes’ gold. Why not beg of
Carnegie ? His money doesn’t stink more than
Rhodes’.
An interesting Venetian scene, with the
Campanile of St. Mary’s in the background, was
slap-dashed on the wall (24ft. by 12ft.) over the
entrance-door at Hawthorn (Melb.) Town rial! a
few days before the cable, announced that the his-
toric bell-tower had collapsed. Peter Hansen
(who painted Waxelleucy Madden’s portrait,
a while ago) was responsible for this new depar-
ture ip town-hall decoration. The Hawthorn
aldermen decided to do up their hall, and called
tenders for the job. Peter Hansen, whose excel-
lent artistic 'credentials might not have carried
any weight but for his being a resident in the
suburb, managed to get the contract to do up the
civic barn in the Renaissance style, and the result
was some very effective work. The neighborhood
now feels proud of itself, and the local councillors
have started to grow their hair long and talk
about frescoes, friezes, and panels. Hansen is an
expert decorative artist. In Egypt, he did a lot
of fresco work for the Khedive and pashas, and
he was also commissioned, years ago, to design
the decorations of the Royal Opera House at
Buda-Pesth.

MOTOR-CAE SERVICES.
So rapidly has the question of supplying
lapid and regular communication between
the city and suburbs oome to the front that
the o&ble tramways and our disgracefully
alow railways are in imminent danger of
being left altogether behind by a proposed
new motor-oar service, whioh made a trial
trip yesterday, when a 12-horee-power
Coventry Daimler ran out from the Stock
Exchange to Essendon and Braybrook and
back.'

“ THE AUSTRALIA ” is loftily situated ; from
its dome, the Pacific Ocean can be seen, and the
whole of Sydney.
To its favorable and airy position, “THE
AUSTRALIA ” adds every comfort and appoint-
ment. It is fireproof from tower to basement;
and an efficient staff patrols the building day and
night. It possesses the most elegant of drawing-
rooms, handsomely appointed dining-rooms, cosy
bar lounges and winter gardens. The system
and attendance of the hotel are unsurpassable.
J. URL SMITH (Late of Menzies’), Manager,
Gastlereagh-st., Sydney.
THE SONG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
H H sweet! oh sad! its echo rang ;
U It wailed a half-forgotten woe—
The song—our song—the song I sang
When we were happy long ago.
It followed as I passed along
The dreary, grey suburban street;
To-day it thrilled insistent, strong—
In town to tune of passing feet.
Its very echo pierced my soul!
It chained me, trembling, to the spot.
I flung the singer, blind, a do'e—
So blind with tears I saw him not.
Oh sad! oh sweet! its echo rang—
It yearned—as yearns a soul in pain!
Once more I bore the parting pang—
Farewell! Farewell! Farewell again.
DOROTHY M‘C.
The Inky Way.
“These incidents show the efforts which are being
made by modern journalists to keep the public posted.
. . , 1 “
h uld not any Australian newspaper-office blush
through its stone or stucco while such stuff was going
over the machines? It is the S.M. Herald slavering the
Daily Mail and the D.T. of London for their enterprise
u.„o>AM n t, i. ~ f oc
in the b African peace news. But where was the Herald
and its enterprise at that juncture? Where the great
Australian newspaper combine, stronger in capital and
richer in annual revenue than any London proprietary?
All the “ great daily” enterprise Australia knows is in
the way of seeing how little can be given to the public in
return for the hundreds of thousands hauled in every
year. Even in our own home affairs the meagreness of
the record has in late years become scandalous. One of
the biggest stock famines, and the biggest attempt at
artificial stock-feeding ever seen in the world, has not
had one column of special expert description in any Aus-
tralian journal. But it is recorded with much solemnity
how schoolmaster Smith was testimonialised on leaving
Cow Flat, and what the handicaps were in the Dungog
ping-pong tournament. "
* * • *
Sydney Morning Herald is trying some type experi-
ments, or using up old founts, and nowadays appears in a
sadly ragged and woebegone condition, like a Granny
who has been out oh the tiles all night and has lost ail
her hairpins and has forgotten to wash her face. The
appearance of the organ of respectable dullness is even
worse than that of the “ illustrated ” D.T. However, the
S.M.H. tries no type experiments with the advertise-
ments ; they are the important part of the paper.
* * « *
S.M. Herald gets reckless in its old age ; t’other day
it actually committed itself to this : “ When seasons are
good, sheep and cattle are plentiful and prime; then
meat is good and cheap. When there is a succession of
had seasons, as now, sheep are scarce and meat is dear.’
The boldness, the ferocity of it, by Jingo ! ’
*»* ’ *
Horsham (Vic.) Times, July 22:—“Owing to pressure
on our space we are compelled to hold over our cable
news.” The space-pressure is chiefly due to a 3-col. re-
port of a shire council meeting. The Horsham editor a
trained bush journalist, knows what his readers want
and lets ’em have it, no matter if he loses a million over
his cable service.
Tolstoi is better. And the newspapers all round the
world, which had been waiting to weep over his death,
pigeon-hole their copy again with regret— all except S.A.
Advertiser, which had put its stuff into type, and wasn’t
going to “ dis ” it, and so said : “ The recovery of Count
Tolstoi is an event of interest to the whole world. His
death would have meant”—and then the regulation
column. The “boneyard” is a regular institution on
every daily paper. When Parliament is out of session
and work is slack, staff members are-put on to writing lives
of the still-living big saints and sinners of the world.
These are pigeon-holed for future use when the great men
die. This system explains why there is usually so much
“ woodenness ”in the matter. A pumped-out man is set
to work in cold blood, not under the stimulus of the feel-
ing which the report of a great man’s death gives. Henry
Parkes was put in the “ boneyard ” when, by a cab acci-
dent, he broke his leg : and the account then written was,
with a little addition and change, used by most of the
dailies when he died, years after, even though an entirely
different estimate of the man had then formed in most
minds.
* » * »
The censorship in S’Africa was just about as inept as
the rest of the military organisation. Bennet Burleigh’s
intimation to London D.T. as to the peace proclama-
tion contained the two words “ Whitsuntide greetings.”
The office-boy immediately looked up (he gospel for the
day and discovered this sentence—" Peace I leave with
you . . . Let not your heart be troubled, neither let it be
afraid.” Wallace (the Daily Mail man) got in earlier with
a considerably more transparent, fake—“ s r endors (Boers)
finally accepted Alf.’s (Milner’s), offer Paxfontein gold
farm—terms satisfactory.” . . . The censor let the “ mining
message ” through.
• * * »
Sydney E. News is very careful indeed as totheN.S.W.
hangman’s feelings. In re the Shepherd-street tragedy,
a letter (perhaps a hoax) was picked up in Balmain pur-
porting to come from one of the suspects, which con-

• * * »
At the exhibition in connection with the librarians’ con-
ference in March, 1902, was shown the first vol. of the
Port Phillip Gazette, labelled—“The first newspaper
printed in Vic.” An Argus correspondent, correcting
this, says rightly that the first newspaper -printed in Vic.
was No. 10 (Mar. 5,1838) of the Melbourne Advertiser,
There were only 19 numbers altogether ; the first nine
were hand-written. Fact was the owner, Johnny
Fawkner, had omitted to get a printer’s license (perhaps
he thought that his pub. license covered everything), and
got out of the difficulty by issuing a written paper. After
Fawkner’s Advertiser came the Port Phillip Gazette
(Oct. 27, 1838), published by George Arden (editor) and
Thomas Strode (printer); Strode had been overseer on
S.M. Herald. They had no funds, but plenty of pluck,
Fawkner revived the Advertiser, with which was in-
corporated the Patriot, on Feb. 16, 1839, with J. G.
Smith as editor ; it was the organ of the Van Diemen's
Land party.
* * » *
One morning recently, very early, the staff of a Mel-
bourne daily were the victims of a little misunderstand-
ing. Just as the first edition was being sent to press an
urgent ’phone message reached the office from one of the
reporters, breathlessly demanding that a cab should be
sent to his suburb instanter to meet him at the local
police-station. “ He’s on to a scoop,” remarked the sub,
with certainty, and “ collared ’’ the roundsman, sent
messengers to bring three other reporters out of their
beds, and ordered the comps, to wait around. An hour
passed and no news. The police didn’t know anything,
and the whole business was wrapped in darkest mystery.
About 4 a.m. the sub. wrathfully shut down, heaping
curses upon the man whose copy hadn’t arrived.
Just then the roundsman appeared and reported with
much grin that he had been out to the residence of his
excited colleague and got the strength of the whole
“ sensation.” It was the arrival of a “ little stranger,’*
and the reporter wanted the cab to bring a doctor.
• * * •
When the late Chief Justice Higinbotham sur-
rendered the editorship of Melb Argus, in ’59, the pro-
prietors bought him a gold watch as a souvenir ; but
honest George, for some reason, declined to accept it.
The watch was hung up in the office cupboard, and no-
body knew what to do with it. In ’63, however, a man
with genius enough to solve the knotty problem came
along. This was J. O. King, the new manager. He said :
“The case is as plain as a pikestaff. I am manager;
what does nob belong to anybody else in this office belongs
to me.” So he took the watch. King’s name will live in
history on account of another autocratic measure which
is stfll in force. Up to the time of his advent two guineas
was the sum paid for Argus leaders. He knocked off the
shillings, reducing the pav to £2.

Re the corrections which legislators are permitted to
make in their Hansard speeches. A few years ago an en-
raged Member cried “ Its a damned lie ! ’’ across the floor
of N.S. W. Assembly. The S.M. Herald reporter “ took ”
the expression and, suspecting that it might be questioned
in the cool light of next day, secured a Hansard proof
before leaving the House. The expression was published
and the Member indignantly denied ever having used such
language, showing a copy of Hansard in support of his
denial. But to the utter discomfiture of the Member the
uncorrected proof of the Parliamentary records was still
in the reporter’s possession.
When Edward Wilson visited Melbourne in 1863-4
to make fresh arrangements in the Argus office, he
noiselessly entered the editor’s room at 9 o clock one
Sunday evening l and found that overburdened man just
putting the finishing touches to a drawing of a large and
umbrageous oak tree on his blotting-pad. 8
proprietor sought an interview with the sub-editor.
That important personage was sound asleep in his chair,
his head buried m a heap of newspapers flanked by an
empty quart pot. The saturnine Edward left without
speaking a word to a soul, the only other person about
(the head printer) having fled for his life on seeing the
apparition of the proprietor on the stairway. But wasn (
there trouble next day ?

* * * *
James Smith, the veteran Melbourne journalist, writes,
at over 80 years of age, with as much facility as in the
days of his earlier work on the Argus staff. It is said
that he can turn out an Age leader as quickly, and with as
much information at his finger-tips, as writers of half Ins
years, and that the freshness and verve of his descriptive
articles often change the nil admirari attitude of the edi-
torial mind into one of warm appreciation. He is, besides,
a constant contributor to the Harbinger of Light, the
organ of occultism in Melbourne, now well over its -30th
year of publication—his reviews, scraps of verse, and
occasional articles showing no sign of weariness or failing
powers. James Smith is, in fact, a living protest against
a theory oft-repeated in The Bulletin’s Red Page, that
men past 40, and women past 80, have done their best work
as writers, and that little more can be expected from them.

There was a significant incident in connection with the
recent peace news. Melbourne Herald, rightly antici-
pating that Mabriland, the prospective colonial military
depot, would be favored with first news, proposed to
Acting-Premier Joseph Ward to wire them directly he
heard of the proclamation. Always willing to make
friends, the compound Radical agreed, and the Herald
made a scoop.
Henry Fletcher to The Bulletin on
“ Loudon ” :
What hgs induced “ L.M.” to limn in rosy scroll the city
I know so well—to make a mirage of a nightmare ? And,
lest Australians, tempted by the picture, should pilgrim-
ate en masse, I tell what I know, in soberness, as one
born and bred in that dark and fearsonie city of the
Thames.
The color-scheme of London is soot. To a clouded sky
is added a canopy of soft coal-smoke some hundred feet
thick, that drapes the city with a pall, year-in, y'ear-ou*.
Rain and fog bring this smoke down in small, black, evil-
smelling flakes, making a permanent fresco in soot on ali
exterior objects. Statues, public buildings, house fronts,
even the bark of trees, all are grimed to blackness. No
light clothing can he worn without defilement ; and those
who wisely breathe through the nose find in the handker-
chief the iiorrors partly saved from the lungs. Londoners
do not know what rain is—fresh, sweet, Heaven-smelling
rain. They give that name to a dirty slush they guard
against by the constant carriage of an um rella.
Then there are many days of pea-soup fog, making a
night-darkness and a stink all its own ; and there are the
dry months, when the east wind blows, without ceasing,
a razor-edged blast i hat slays wholesale the old, the
young, and the weakly.
The streets of London, for the greater part, may be
likened to those of Waterloo, in Sydney, or of
Collingwood, in Melbourne, only they are narrower and
more begrimed with muck. The roadways 9 days in 10,
are a slush of treacly black mud, and the house-fronts, as
in our own mean streets, of a prison-like uniformity. The
cottage is not known in true London. Always the
terrace-house, mile after mile, street after street. Two
storeys, or three, or four—squalid or solid ; but all
unclean, unlovely. The most fashionable squares arid
terraces have the stables right under the back windows;
and a lane at rear called “ a Mews,” where sordid Poverty
sits and shivers.
Human misery, indeed, is the keynote of public life ;
in no London crowd or gathering do you escape the sight
of pallid faces, of dirty, broken clothes, sickly children,
and drink-sodden men and women. Such foul clothing
as is commonly worn by the poor of London is not found
on any other people, save, perhaps, the most degraded
Polish Jews.
But there is light even in the blackness of London. The
gilded public-house signboards are in perpetual evidence,
along house-copings, sides and front. These drinking-
places are at every corner and at many intervals between !
“ Oharrington, Head and Oo.’s Entire,” “ Truman, Han-
bury Buxton and Oo.’s Entire,” and so on—these are the
gods to whom London pays tribute in much porter and
four-ale. At night these temples are a blaze of light-
gleaming invitingly through the fog and rain; and,
crowding there, Gin gives the one joy of the day to the
multitude.
From lack of sunshine, and the imbibing of much soot,
the average Cockney is pallid, undersized and narrow-
chested. Two years of good food and fresh air in the
army or navy transform him and remove the curse.
Most Londoners spend a quarter of their waking lives in
going to and from their work, and their chief talk on the
journey, when they are not coughing or spitting, is of the
awful, uncertain, horrible weather.
There are a few beauty-spots in London—of a beauty
accidental and enhanced by rarity. The Thames Em-
bankment at night, the West End parks on a spring day,
when the sun chances to penetrate the smoke ; the river
scenery, even with the fog, or perhaps because of the fog,
with its countless sailing barges and little steamers
looming suddenly out of mysterious darkness on the
yellow lapping tide, and, so passing, a series of vignettes
like lantern-slides. But beauty, as a cult, or as a common-
place, is foreign to this city, and this people, who in their
form-blindness and color-blindness cling to their grime
and smoke with stubborn fanaticism.
L.M. writes, doubtless, of a few West End streets and
parks, seen with imaginative eyes ; but that is not London
—nor a one-percentage thereof. True London is a vast
horde of over four million people, for the most part
badly-clothed, ill-fed, and with only cne day’s poor wages
between them and pauperism—a people to whom ugliness
ana dirt have no voice ; while, as for the well-to-do, the
GROUNDS FOR SUSPICION.
BAEREIGH ( the pub.-keeper who has never been
known to turn it on free): " ’Ave a drink,
Bill?”
BUFFLER BILL: “ W'y, ain't it good?"
accustomed sight of misery has made them so callous that
they can laugh as they walk the streets while half-clad
famine jostles them unheeded. This is London.
From Reynolds’ Newspaper :
The growing physical incapacity of Londoners is shown
in the fact that, apart from the enormous amount of
private attention from medical men, no less a sum than
£1,000,000 was spent on hospital work. This is a startling
and disgraceful fact, due, in great measure, to the rash
and early marriages by which superfluous children are
begotten before the parents are in a position to properly
provide for them. Think of the appalling fact that dur-
ing 1900 just 2,090,000 persons were treated in the London
hospitals ! The decay of the race has turned the metro-
polis into one vast lazar-house where the very air is
tainted with human corruption. Thus one-half the
population of London are either diseased or criminal.
Ifow long can a nation endure under such conditions ?
London is Tory, because it is a city of invalids.

The English of the educated Babu seems
likely to be rivalled by the educated Kaffir. From
a Kaffir railway-official rebuked for his drinking
habits:
Having promu'gated my conduct of drinking presump-
tuously, I beg to tell me nominally the person informed
you. When you addressed your inspection I perceived
dishonest intermeddling otherwise I am not a controver-
sial acumen. Remember you are forced to tell me, the
