Enterprise will tell. Mr. A. Saunders, of
815 George-street, Sydney, has made a substantial
addition to his already extensive jewellery show-
rooms, where visitors may wander through a gor-
geous wilderness of clocks of all designs,
watches in unlimited variety, and whole
stacks of silver and electro plate.
Sprinkled over the white glitter are
cases scintillating with precious stones,
where the blue of the sapphire alter-
nates with the guilty red of the ruby,
and diamond lights mingle with the
refulgence of the opal. Silver swans
with jewelled eyes float past with velvet
pincushions between their wings ; vases
of tinted glass rise from fronds of silver ;
biscuit barrels of polished oak, bound
with silver, throw a darker gleam
between, and everywhere there is an
atmosphere of gorgeous, scintillating
luxury. All the thousand and one
beautiful things, which make thfe plain,
ordinary woman a dream of delight, are
here in the wildest profusion,;and at
bedrock prices. Australia is a country
where the class of people who can
wear jewellery running into thousands
of pounds is so limited as to be prac-
tically non existent. Even that class
which can accumulate a few hundred
pounds’ worth is frightfully limited, and
it is for the great mass, whose means only allow
them to have a few nice silver table articles, and
a bracelet or two, with a few pretty brooches and a
muff chain for the wife, and a neat diamond ring
for the husband, that Mr. Saunders has laid
himself out to cater. His show-rooms are full of
beautiful and useful articles, and although the
wealthiest can find there all that he desires, the
great mass of the goods have been created for the
benefit of the man of Rothschild tastes and plain
J. Smith finances.

BRISBANE.
POLITICAL
POINTS
The Duke of York’s “ghost” calls the
diary of the Imperial tour “ The Web of Empire.”
Will you walk into my parlour?’ said the
spider to the fly.”
There are now two rival female political
organisations in N.S.W. separated by a gulf of
bitter hatred. When women actually get to work
as voters in N.S.W. there will be much good
claw-work.
Under Premier Philp’s retrenchment
scheme the public servant with £450 a year loses
10 per cent, and the one above that 15 per cent.
So the man with £450 gets £405, and the man
with £460 gets £391.
N.S.W. Cabinet solidarity is not what it
used to be. Woman’s suffrage is so big a question
that Premier See challenges the Leg. Assembly
to put up a hostile vote, and so little a question
that Attorney-General Wise openly ridicules it,
and hands over its advocacy to Suttor.
Barton is the No-Yes politician from the
Imperial standpoint. Note his title and his atti-
tude on the Brodriclc proposal to make Australia
a barrack-yard for J. Bull:
On Edmund England strove to press
A title. Edmund answered “ Yes ! ”
But when she asked the quid pro quo,
As promptly Edmund answered “ No 1 ”
Dry Dog Reid privately stated, a few
weeks ago, that there are three Victorian seats
held by Protectionists M‘Coll, Isaacs, and
Phillips—which he hopes to gain for Freetrade at
next general elections ; and that with those seats
in the hands of Vic. Freetraders he can become
Federal Prime Minister in next Parliament.
Hence the recent stump campaign.
The reappearance of the Iron Bonus Bill
in the House of Reps, evoked one opinion, to the
effect that there was no hurry about establishing
a new industry for a year or two, anyhow. Why,
cert’nly ! In that year or two, on present appear-
ances, the money Australia will have borrowed
for relief works will amount to more than it will
manage to repay in 100 years. But there is no
hurry !

Concerning the Catholic, Episcopalian, Scotchby-
terian, Hardshell Baptist, and other pothers, re the
kind of oath that shall be sworn, let him swear on

E. W. Owe’Sullivan reckons that he has
the reversion of the N.S.W. Premiership when
John See retires to gloat over his title. But the
Labor party (with Wise in its eye) is not quite so
sweet with the Owe’S. now as of yore, which dis-
counts his.chance somewhat. Further, John See
is credited, in some quarters, with an intention to
recommend that J. H. Carruthers be sent for to
form a Coalition Ministry when he gives up the
reins. There is a pretty general impression that
Mr. See does intend to give up office shortly.
Attorney-General Gordon told S.A. Leg.
Council that it was rumored there was a lot
of loafing at the Islington railway workshops.
The men were indignant, and demanded a
thorough inquiry by independent experts.
Gordon calmly remarked that all he desired
had been accomplished by stirring the work-
men up, and he refused to let his statements
be inquired into. Here is another proof of the
“slump” in Labor politics in S.A., for Gordon
was once a member of the Kingston Govt, and a
pretended Radical.
Cabled that Canada imports 20,000 English
harvesters to gather this year’s wheat crop. The
protected Dominion employs so many of its
workers in its own manufactures that when one of
the great “natural industries” booms a little
extra it actually has to import labor—and not
from the handy country alongside, the protected
U.S. A., but from Freetrade England.
Adelaide’s ex-Mayor Tucker moves in the
S.A, Assembly to cut the salary of M’s.P. to £lOO.
He is now a high-class Tory, although on three
occasions he contested city or suburban seats as
an extreme Radical. Then he changed his views
and his objective and -ran off to a country seat in
National Ass. uniform. It was Tucker who
turned King O’Malley out of S.A. Parliament.
A cable in which Sydney Freetrade papers
give away, very injudiciously, Freetrade election
tactics in England (Leeds bye-election):—
Electioneering placards contrasted a big Liberal and a
little Protectionist loaf, although the price of bread
remains practically unaffected by the corn duties.
How often has that trick been played with success
in Sydney ? And even now they are trying to
play it on the tariff issue.
“ Civis ” writes :
Tub Bulletin repeats the inaccurate statement of the
Argus that Crouch’s question in the Reps, as to the
election of a Governor-General was “ received with
laughter.” On the contrary, there were cheers all round
the Chamber when the idea was put forward, and the
Tory paper’s suggestion of jeers is its only cover to what
it recognises as a strong current in a strongly democratic
Parliament. Crouch, by the way, believes in the Imperial
connection, but stands up strongly for Australian rights.
The continual buttering of Labor-leader
Browne by the Q. Boodle papers—seemingly taking
their cue from Premier Philp’s laudatory speeches
at the close of last Parliament—in conjunction
with their constant abuse of Browne’s party, looks
like an organised attempt to shake the confidence
of his supporters. But the attempt will fail.
“Billy” Browne has the confidence of his fol-
lowers to an extent rarely enjoyed by a party-
leader.
After Isaacs, K.G., had spent days racking
his brain over what seemed an informality in the
swearing-in of Acting Gov.-Gen. Tennyson, and
when the public were worked up to the point of
expectancy, Deakin drowned the promised sensa-
tion by stating that the swearing-in was in accord-
ance with a private despatch from Chamberlain.
But ,why was the despatch private ?—and why
didn’t Deakin reveal it before Isaacs sweated and
the public held its breath ?
Treasurer Joseph Ward says that the
securities of Maoriland command a better price in
London than those of any Australian State, and
S.M. Herald believes the statement to be true.
Therefore it seems that the British capitalist is
just yearning to lend money to the State which
has carried socialistic ideas further than any other
in the British empire—yet the Herald is always
urging this country to beware of socialistic
legislation for fear of scaring away capital.
The new batch of Consolidated Bills
whicli kept N.S.W. Assembly up all night
lately may or may not be what they are
alleged to be, and members are not afforded
time or opportunity to ascertain whether
the Bills correspond with the originals.
Mr. Dangar, in the Leg. Council, pointed
that difficulty out when a batch of them
was thrown on the Council table nearly
two years ago. There is simply a certificate
from a barrister that they do correspond,
and both Houses are expected to accept
that as final. In the Assembly on this oc-
casion, lawyer Levy asserted strenuously
that, in some respects, the consolidation
was open to serious criticism, but by the
free use of the gag he was prevented from
even pointing out what were the alleged
faults.
S.M. Herald, in its financial column
of 25th July, alluded to the United States
as “ the most prosperous community in the
world.” Surely the S.M. Herald had been
drinking that day ! Isn’t the U.S. blighted
by the curse of enormous Protection ?
Isn’t it afflicted with a Government paper
currency—the amount in actual circulation
is £170,000,000, equal to over £2 per in-
habitant, or times the bank-note circula-
tion of Australia, and there are banknotes
in the U.S. in addition? Doesn’t it nefa-
riously subsidise new industries, and dis-
courage the investment of foreign capital
in the country so as to make room for
local capital ? Doesn’t it shut out imports
in the most rigid fashion ? And by doing
all these things, that the Herald says
Australia shouldn’t do, it has come to be
“the most prosperous community in the
world ! ”
The Tasmanian Parliament contains
no labor members ; consequently the island
is unaffiicted with labor-legislation. Yet,
nowhere are the daily newspapers so
savagely anti-labor, and in no State was
such bitterly prejudiced evidence given
by employers before Vic. Factories Commis-
sion. Tasmanians still most solemnly argue
against interference with the liberty of the sub-
ject, though, since Transportation ceased, they
have experienced less interference with liberty
than any of their over-sea neighbors. Queer!
At least one section of Maoriland Farmers’
Union has given up the idea of wrecking Seddon,
and is now modestly agitating for the distribution
over the country of prize bulls at Government
expense. This is as it should be. A Union of
Farmers which cannot keep' its treasury in
sufficiently decent order to' be able to pay its
secretary the 20 guineas voted him had better
give up trying to smash a fighter like old man
Seddon. Begging for cheap bulls is much more
in its line.
Another lovely result of the Kyabram
retrenchment, movement in Vic. is an edict of the
Education Department that in future every young-
ster shall have only Bsq. ft. of floor space
instead Of 10. This, though, makes no real
difference to the kiddies, whom the cheese-
paring policy of successive Govts, has for
years past jammed in about 4sq. ft. of floor
space during school hours. In some country
schools half the children are habitually taught out
of doors, in all weathers, because there isn’t room
inside, and each shiftless Government says it
hasn’t any money to build more schools. As for
the district that wants a new, up-to-date school
passing the hat round locally, they don’t do that
sort of thing in Australia.
Premier Lewis's proposal to
abolish Tasmania’s Upper House, and
put 10 members elected on the Council
franchise and 20 members chosen on
the Assembly franchise in one Cham-
ber, is discussed by the island press in
a hushed tone —as if there were a
dead body in the room. The gene-
ral decision seems to be that, if
sufficient care is taken “toconserve
the recognised rights of property,”
as one paper puts it, the proposal
may do. And, certainly, the rights
of Property should be conserved.
A really useful reform would be to
abolish manhood and womanhood
suffrage, and have one House
elected solely by property-owners
—one property-owner one vote,
and only property-owners eligible
as members. Every citizen and
citizeness is a Property-owner by
virtue of his or her share in the
public estate, railways and other
assets. Every adult in Tasmania
owns nearly 100 acres of public
estate as his or her share, and if a
vote was allowed on that account
then Property would be duly re-
presented, and the prejudices of the island re-
spected, and Tasmania would have a decent con-
stitution at the same time.
Denny, M.P. (S.A.), delivered himself
thus in a criticism of the in-and-out running of
the Jenkins Ministry: “The Govt, have been
twitted with their notable ‘ reversals of form,’ and
had the same tiling happened on the racecourse
they would have been called before the stewards
and disqualified for life.” Folk are asking who is
the real Premier of S.A. Jenkins Iscariot fills the
office and draws the pay, but the Ministerial dance
is according to the tune played by the Tory
panjandrum, Opposition-leader Darling.
Most times nobody shudders at a State’s
deficit; pretty well everyone makes some remark
about our magnificent resources and lets the thing
slide. But just now in S.A. that State’s deficit is
being held up as an Awful Thing, and the State’s
condition is represented as being perilous, and it
is hoarsely whispered that notlring but a certain
railway, or a dock, or something of that sort will
save it. Also, of course, the State having a
deficit already, can’t think of doing the work
itself; but private enterprise ! It’s a dry
bone that the wolf can’t get a morsel from.
The. importunity of Chamberlain to have
the Commonwealth raise its Governor-General’s
salary shows that in one Imperial idea Britain has
gone back on itself. In 1846, Earl Grey, in a
despatch to N.S.W., then practically the Com-
monwealth territory, proposed that England
should provide the whole of the Governor’s pay.
Several members of the then Leg. Council adopted
the proposal, but a majority rejected it. Earl
Grey feared that if Australia paid its Governor it
would own him and rule him, and held that Im-
perial interests demanded that he should be owned
and ruled by England.
Noted, as instancing the enormous abuse
of the franking system in Tasmania, that in 1901
no fewer than 1,371,000 letters were franked—
standing for one-seventh of the whole letter-
carrying business of the State. The franked tele-
grams, if paid for, would have brought the State
£50,000. In Tasmania all letters and wires sent
by, or addressed to, M’s.P. go free, and many
members resort quite openly to every possible
dodge of the game. They frank all their own
correspondence, and all their families’. Last year
a housemaid, applying to Mrs. X. for a billet, got
a reply by post franked by Mrs. X.’s husband, a
Leg. Councillor.
In view of the proposed Queensland income
tax, the usual agitated scare-monger (who is some-
times employed in the office and sometimes not)
writes to Brisbane Courier saying that “one
English firm alone withdrew £3,000,000 last year
from Victoria because of the income-tax.” If this
is true, then Victoria is £3,000,000 less in debt
than it was before, and the English firm has appa-
rently taken its money back to its own country
(where the income-tax is Is. 2d. in the £). But
what English firm has =63,000,000 invested in Vic-
toria? And how has £3,000,000 been withdrawn,
without the money leaving the country? The
banking returns have not exhibited any serious
decline in the local stock of cash.
A reverend gentleman at an Orange pow-
wow in N.S.W. having 'straightly alleged an
instance of “a political appointment based on
R.C. influence,” Arthur Griffith, M.L.A., asked
for the exact facts so that he could get a Parlia-
mentary committee to investigate. Then the
pastor replied that
there was not the least probability of Parliament appoint-
ing- any such committee, but suggesting that a select
committee should be moved for, to investigate the whole
question of sectarianism in the public service.
It is a very poor grievance indeed that dare not
allege one specific instance. The parson if he
can’t produce his facts should hold his tongue
instead of suggesting a fishing-expedition.
The Tory press is very solid in its decision
that Premier Irvine,- of Victoria, was wrong in
putting his reduction of members proposals and
his Council Reform proposals in one Bill, because
by trying to get Council Reform (which is worth
a million or two) he runs the risk of losing the
reduction of members (which is worth at least 2d.).
It points out that if he had sent up two separate
measures the Council would have gladly passed
the Reduction of Members Bill and indignantly
thrown out the Reform proposals. Now it may
pass both of them, or it may reject both, and the
Tory press is grieved in its inmost soul that
Irvine should have hazarded 2d. —a good, sure 2d.
—in reaching out for a million or two.
“ Government Rounds ” writes from Mel-
bourne :
Though Vic. Premier Irvine proudly boasted when
forming his team that he was going to be boss himself,
Treasurer Shiels hasn’t taken long, to put himself on the
“ equal-in-all-thin gs” footing that he had with Allan
M‘Lean. Irvine never ventures on doing anything of the
smallest moment without consulting that Sir Oracle, and his
Ministry of “ strong men ” are in the same box. Their
whole time outside Parliament is spent in Cabinet
meetings. As the Govt, virtually has no policy outside
its Reform Bill and the lopping off of minor public service -
salaries, it should have fewer Cabinets than most of its
predecessors. The “Strong”- Ministers M’Kenzie,
Cameron, Murray, Robert Reid, and Irvine himself have
so far shown themselves incapable of deciding even the
most trivial departmental matters without recourse to
nominally the Cabinet, but in reality Shiels. And what
that man of mystery says goes.

How delightfully ingenuous is Premier
Balfour! Announcing to the Commons that a
Royal Commission had been appointed to inquire
into the conduct of the S’African war, he stated
that it would only deal with the—
“ Operations until the occupation of Pretoria. The
Government did not consider it necessary to inquire into
what had transpired in South Africa subsequent to the
occupation of Pretoria.
Didn’t consider it necessary ! Bill Sikes, coming
home furtively from a little expedition, challenges
the world on his actions—up to the time he left
Sunday-school! That the Boer war was conducted
as humanely as such an enterprise could be, under
Lord Roberts, is generally admitted. It is the
events after his departure from S’Africa that hint
of the unspeakable and demand investigation.
Concerning the Napoleonic methods of
S.A. Premier Jenkins :
S.A. Register, with a series of articles by a wrner sign-
ing himself “Financier,” has been sooling on the Govt, to
massacre the Civil Service in the interests of economy.
“Financier’s” particular brand may be gauged by his
reply to a query as to why he didn’t suggest the abolition
of the Leg. Council, with its expensive staff, maintenance,
printing, etc. He considers the Council the only check
on hasty legislation, . . . saves the country untold wealth,
etc., etc. The Govt, has obediently rushed in and with
great violence sacked a few bloated servants at salaries
mostly ranging from £lOO to £2OO (some of them had
turned grey in attaining the latter magnificent reward).
The most rigid apostle of thrift could hardly expect these
men to have saved a competency. There is absolutely no
chance of employment elsewhere —except on the Govern-
ment relief works for the unemployed. The Ministry
may proudly claim to have originated one great stroke of
economy—no fires have been allowed in the Govt offices
this winter 1
Old man M ‘lntyre, of Vic., after opposing
the Referendum bitterly all his useless, stodgy,
political life, was suddenly converted by the near
approach of reform, and demanded a popular vote
to decide whether there should be 46 or 72
M’s.L.A. and 23 or 36 M’s.L.C. He didn’t pro-
pose to let the people say whether they wanted
any other number, or whether they wanted any
Leg. Council at all; and if his idea had been
accepted —which it wasn’t—he would doubtless
have pointed tearfully to the absence of any popu-
lar mandate as a reason why the House of Land-
lords should keep all its obstructive fossils
intact. The Referendum is an institution that
the Tory highly approves of—when he has the-
fixing of the issues. What he would really like
would be to take a vote as to whether the
Assembly suffrage should be confined to people
with at least £5O a year income from property, or
to people with an income of at least £75 a year
from property, and whether Labor members should
be hanged or only drowned.
“It is the smart young man of the Vic.
public-service who suffers most severely under the
Shiels - Irvine retrenchment system,” writes-
“ Galleryite ” from Melbourne—
Everyone of the young fellows who have proved them-
selves able, energetic and gritty has thus been given the
worst kind of set-back. The private secretaries to Various-
Ministers supply cases in point. These advisers of the
usually stolid and unbusinesslike persons who attain to
political eminence in Victoria are all ambitious youngsters,
selected because they happen to possess the qualifications
mentioned, and they do more of the administrative
government of the country than their political chiefs
would have gumption enough to undertake by themselves.
Yet this is what happens to them under the Shiels-Irvine
policy. Peacock’s late secretary, a £2OO-a-year man with.
£5O a-year added in consideration of much extra;
work, lias just been shunted to a position in the Statist’s-
office, where his sole employment is the sort-
ing of cards with people’s names and other particu-
lars upon them, and his allowance has been knocked off :
in consideration of his untiring devotion to his late work.
Agriculture Minister Taverner’s secretary was due for a
small increment, and also received an allowance for-
spending half the nights of the year writing departmental
correspondence instead of going to choir-practice or join-
ing a two-up school. The reward of virtue in this case is-
something like £7O a year. Another young man, getting.
£6O a year, has for long done much gratuitous extra work,
as shorthander for successive Lands Ministers. Shiels.
sternly determined that he was not going to increase the
bloated opulence of the 225. 6d. a week man, and promptly
stopped his £lO rise. And I shouldn’t be at all surprised
to see the mouths of such unfortunates stopped with a
threat to deprive the public service of voting-rights,
before the whole thing’s settled.

“Aurora”: One little instance of the
economical methods of N.S.W. P.O. Department.
From a “ one-man ” post office, 20 miles from this
town, the P.M. left, and a man was sent from the
P.O. here temporarily. (That was 10 months
ago). For that he receives a “relieving allow-
ance ”as well as his salary. Another man in the
local P.O. was placed in charge of the work
usually done by the man sent “ relieving,” and he
also received a relieving allowance (goodness
knows what for). Now this last man wanted a
holiday, and headquarters sent a “reliever” to
remain here until the first man has returned to his
office from the little bush P.O. All these men are
doing the same work, and the country is paying
three relieving allowances and travelling expenses
when there was no necessity to pay any, as any of
the three could have been appointed to the one-
man office.

Quite beside the fact that she does come oit under
generous terms from Manager Musgrove, when all is
said, Melba makes a big financial sacrifice to visit her
native land. Her recent triumphant rontrie at Oovent
Garden in “ Rigoletto ” shows that, if she felt that way,
she might have filled in the time she will spend out here
in engagements at a far higher rate than she will ever get
in Australia. The trip means three months of idleness on
the water. Furthermore she will lose all private engage-
ment fees, there being few, if any, in Australia who would
or could disgorge her ordinary fee for attending a musical
“ at-home ” ; yet a big proportion of her income comes
from that source, as is proved by her agents lately
announcing that she is already engaged for 16 big musical
“ at-homes ”in London at 500 gs. a time. In each case the
host is a millionaire, local or imported, and members of
the Royal Family will be present—and the advt. resulting
from being reported on in such company is one which
even Melba would not sneeze at. Nellie Melba is not
coming out here to make herself richer. In Melbourne,
at any rate, she will spend a lot of money. She has taken
a big house with the intention of “ entertaining ” lavishly.
Her visit is to be a social event.
The amusing monkey-imitation that Edward Lauri
ogives when confronted with a real ape, in “A Chinese
Honeymoon,” recalls old times, when the Lauri Family
divided with the Martinettis the distinction of being the
best animal-imitants in London. The two troupes used to
be pitted against each other by rival panto, managers.
The Lauris seem to have split up completely, and the
remnant of the Martinetti crowd was here some time ago
doing clever dumb-show sketches, under Rickards’ man-
agement.
Oinquevalli is, with all his strength, a model of grace
and symmetry. There is no balloondike muscular develop-
ment : the limbs are smooth and graceful as a woman’s.
His is the strength of the panther—lithe, quick, sure—
rather than of the bull. The cannon-ball act, in which
Oinquevalli makes a heavy ball of iron play around his
arms and torso, as lightly as a will-o’-the-wisp around a
marsh, is a marvel of agility as well as of strength. The
cannon-ball runs from arm to arm, around the chest and
the back, propelled by the muscles, with swift certainty
—never a mistake, never too much or too little effort.
Since reaching Sydney, Oinquevalli has been suffering
from a knee-trouble, necessitating the care of a doctor
and a masseur, but he doesn’t let this interfere with his
act, nor with journeyings to the homes of Italian com-
patriots, who provide in his honor “maccaroni suppers.”
He can never resist the temptation of “ maccaroni cooked
by Italians,” says Cinque, “ and nobody else knows how
to cook it."
The announcement that Mrs. Langtry’s daughter,
Jeanne, was lately married to an ex-secretary of Lord
Salisbury came as a shock to most people. It is so long
since the “Jersey Lily” ceased to be identified with her
first husband. She bore his name into wondrous noto-
riety, but Langtry himself was never sufficiently in news-
paper evidence to suggest that his wife had borne him
any offspring. Nevertheless, it was Miss Jeanne Langtry,
daughter of the faded Lily, who married lan Malcolm,
M.P., the other week, and received a present from her
mother’s old friend, King Edward, with a note in his own
handwriting. By the way, what is the age of the bride ?
If the amateur “Runaway Girl” performances in
Melbourne produce £2OO profit for “the charities,” the
people who worried themselves for months in preparing
that vainglorious foolishness will have the satisfaction of
knowing that exes, were, roughly speaking, 60 per cent,
of the gross receipts. A much more successful dissipation
was the farewell afternoon-tea given to Grace Palotta at
Melb. Athenaaum. Receipts were considerably over £lOO
and expenses only 8 per cent. Included in the expenses
were light refreshments for 600 people who turned up.
The management of the “ charity ” entertainment in that
case was quite unimpeachable. The light refreshments
must have been light indeed.

“ Cyrano de Bergerac,” which Henry Lee has intro-
duced to Australia as a translation of the famous French
play by Rostand, is the subject of an interesting scandal.
Rostand was elected a member of the French Academy—
the almighty Academy—by virtue of his romantic drama;
but two months ago the U.S. High Court granted a
perpetual injunction against Mansfield’s production of
the English version on the ground that Rostand stole the
original from the work of one Eberly Gross, a Chicago
litterateur. Gross proved his case to the satisfaction of
the American Court, anyhow, though in European
critical circles there is a tendency to treat it with
ridicule. Evidence showed that he offered a play
called “The Merchant Prince of Corneville ” to the
management of the Porte St. Martin, Paris, in 1876.
In 1896, or thereabouts, he published his story in
book-form, and when Rostand’s play came to light a
year later, it proved to be more or less identical with
Gross’s yarn. Thus far Rostand is under suspicion of
having taken his hero, Cyrano de Bergerac, from the
Chicago man’s imaginative story of that saintly historical
personage. It may be that both authors got their
respective stories from one obscure source, but this
theory is in itself a satire on the Academy’s exaltation of
Rostand’s genius.
“ Voce ” : “|A Little Letter from London ”(8., July 19),
is incorrect in stating that Melba was never patronised
by Queen Victoria. She sang twice before the late
Queen at Windsor, the second time being the period
during which the then allegedly-contemplated divorce
proceedings were under public discussiom. After that
H.M. became too infirm to attend even private concerts.

golden shavings. Take me as I am. See what an
artist lam ! Unusual, but good.
“T.D.”: Re the legs of lady amateurs, I have joyous
recollections of an amateur performance of “ The Gon-
doliers” in a Maoriland town, and a mighty struggle
between the ladies of the chorus for the privilege of being
included in the section that wore tights. The contest
became so hot that the aspirants drew lots out of the
stage-manager’s hat, the blanks being condemned to the
wearing of dresses. My fun came in when listening to
the spiteful explanations by the disappointed ones of how
their chums in tights had overcome various defects in
their apparently solid foundations.

The High Price of Land.
A party of Victorian farmers are negotiating for
soil on the Darling Downs (Queensland), the
reason why they desire to shift being that land
prices and land rents in their own district have
become so high that their work has ceased to be
reasonably profitable. They have struck against
the same phenomenon which has overtaken many
other countries—which is even overtaking some
parts of thinly-peopled N.S. Wales. Country land
is only valuable for what it will grow, and after
a time it becomes so valuable that people can’t
afford to grow things on it. The landlord,
whether local or absentee, wants so much rent
that there is no sufficient margin of sustenance
left. If the owner is also the proprietor, then the
outgoing proprietor wants so much money from
the incoming one that the land doesn’t give a
sufficient return for the latter’s labor, and reason-
able interest on the money he paid for the
property. If the State is owner and only
charges a moderate rent, then, unless the
State absolutely prohibits transfers, the old
lessee sells out to the new one, or sublets and
exacts a bonus or rent which leaves the new man
in the position of a sweated victim. The State,
of course, can do all the sweating itself, and thus
leave no room for the exaction of a bonus, but
that is hardly the remedy sought for. The policy
of buying up large estates and selling them on
easy terms to small farmers is a remedy for many
evils, but it doesn’t touch this evil except in a
purely temporary way. The small man is usually
a poor man at. the beginning of his career, and for
a time he sticks to his allotment and toils to
pay off the liability on it. And, if he is
the right kind of man, he is probably suc-
cessful, for, as a rule, the State has sold to
him on easy terms which greatly simplify his
task. And in later years, when settlement there-
abouts has grown dense, he or his descendants
will turn landlords, and let their property to
someone else on quite as grasping terms as could
have been demanded by the original big pre-
decessor .whom the State ejected. The policy of
subdividing the. land among a multitude of small
resident. proprietors is beneficial to the com-
munity in many ways, but it only diminishes —
it doesn’t wholly remedy—the rack-renting evil.
A land-tax is a most commendable idea, but while
it makes the landlord give a part of his overcharge
to the State, it doesn’t prevent the tenant being
overcharged. The idea that the State should fix
a fair rent is good also, but while it prevents the
exaction of an excessive rent, it doesn’t wholly
prevent the sale of the land to the cultivator at an
excessive price—which is the same evil over again
—for interest is just rent under another name.
There is a great opening for the man who will
invent a perfectly effective way of keeping down
land values in a rich community. The increase in
the value of land does the community no good
and much harm. It goes along with general pros-
perity, and for that reason it has been supposed to
be beneficial; but it isn’t a cause of prosperity—it
is merely one of the evils that accompany it.
Affluence is a good thing, and is often accom-
panied by gout, but gout doesn’t cause affluence.
Wanted—the address of a wealthy, well-governed
community, prosperous in agriculture, manu-
factures and commerce, where the cultivator is not
sweated by anybody, and yet doesn’t rise to be an
offensive bounder and sweat somebody else.
Wanted also, full information as to how that com-
munity arrived at that condition.
A letter from a farming district:
■R« the exodus of many desirable colonists co b Africa
and the general begging of the question by the leading
newspapers, the matter lies in a nutshell. No prospect
of ever making a decent livelihood offers itself in any line
of life here. Australia is drifting from bad to worse every
year. The land in Queensland, N.S.W., and Victoria is
being rapidly let to tenants. The drought must in the
future cause a heavy rise in value where rainfall is
certain. As an evidence, a farmer on the* thirds system
on one of the estates lately offered to Vic. Govt, at 2s. 6d.
an acre, got as high as 15 bags of oats per acre last season.
At present price of oats, squatter’s share equalled £3.
The same land is now being rushed at from 10s. to 12s. 6d. per
acre rent, and only for wheat-growing, squatter reserving
the right to graze the stubble. These estates were mostly
hopelessly insolvent, but in a few years the tenants will
have lifted the mortgage, and the chance of the lifetime
of this State has been lost. Everything is tending
towards the introduction of the English tenant system,
and landlordism, which has laid its blistering curse on
England, Ireland and Scotland. One squatter has al-
ready started the good old annual dinner to the tenants,
and the line of demarcation between them and the so-
called lower orders is as sharply drawn as the line betweep
squatter and tenant. Another landholder who has recently
let his land to the toilers, publicly announced his inten-
tion of running for Parliament, in the tenants’ interest of
course, as “socialistic legislation is ruining the State.”
In Victoria the Man with the Laugh and the Parliament
with the Jingo frenzy had a unique chance, but preferred
to spend thousands welcoming royalty, and decking out
warriors in gold braid and cocks’ plumes and sending them
away to help take other peoples’ land for another class of
exploiters. While they were yelling these and other
curses were coming home to roost.

Another moan in the Sydney morning
press:—
Senator Drake’s visit to N. S. Wales and Queensland has
further impressed upon him the great disadvantages
these States are under, owing to there being no loan
money available to carry out needed postal works. In
Sydney, he says, there is great overcrowding of the staff
at the G.P.0., owing to the inadequate room. Additions
made to the office some time ago by the State Govern-
ment cannot be completed until the Federal Parliament
votes £12,000 and in the meantime these additions are
practically useless. He does not know what Sydney will
do if the Loan Bill is not passed.
Inference: This great country of “illimitable
natural resources ” can’t by any possibility be ex-
pected to find £12,000 for a post-office without
floating a loan ! Is there any other country in the
world as helpless as the great Loan Press rep-
resents Australia to be.
“Yul.”: .Rethepurloining of The Bulletin
in transit through the post. A friend of mine was
in regular correspondence with a chum residing in
an out-lying part, and, the latter’s habitation
being solitary, the postman had to walk a few
miles extra —with a mountain-climb thrown in—
to deliver his mails. The consequence was that,
as often as not, the mails failed to arrive. Then
the victim struck the bright idea of advising his
correspondents to mail their stuff without postage,
with the result that the letters turned up with
unheard-of regularity, guarded as if registered,
for the sake of the postage-to-pay. Since institu-
tion of local postage oil newspapers, writer has
applied the remedy to the case of The B. (which
is posted to him far out-back) with complete
success.
“ A. C.H.”: Re chronic deafness (“ Gal vo ”
in recent Bulletin). I have an extremely deaf
relative, who, nevertheless, hears voices well
while a piano is being played. In fact, that is
the only time she has ever heard her babies cry.
As to comprehension of a remark before the
repetition asked for is given, this occurs with non-
deaf persons as well as with deaf, and seems to
show that the sub-consciousness can correctly re-
gister word-sounds without the over-conscious-
ness immediately receiving the word-meanings.

‘. , „ . „ . „
The yarn that is going around as to Barton having paid
John Norton’s expenses at a Fitzroy (N.S. W.) bye-election,
hints at an interesting little bit of political history. At
the first referendum on the Federal Bill, John Norton,
then “anti-bill,” blackguarded Barton industriously, and
printed strange word-pictures of Toby going- on to plat-
forms “purple with food.” The Bill failed to get the
required number of votes, and a general election was
fought on the Federal issue, Barton opposing Reid in
King division. John Norton was a candidate for a
neighboring constituency (Fitzroy), and had to choose
between running under the Barton or the Reid flag. At
that time Norton was a power in Sydney politics, and in
every constituency there was a little band of Rosicrucians
(“ bottle-oh ” men et hoc genus) who would vote Norton
very time. Norton’s help, it was thought, would be useful
to Barton, and Barton’s useful to Norton, and a treaty of
peace was agreed to, John Norton giving his public
allegiance to Barton. Then Barton funked on his share
of the compact—saying a good word for Norton in
Fitzroy—and whilst there was no public breach between
would have been materially changed by Barton being
Premier of N.S.W. during the ultimate stage of the
Federal negotiations. There was no money-compact in
the matter? Barton wasn’t in a position to give any,
Norton to need any.

Ihat Russian writer whose opinion of the
German army has gone all round the world s
press, and who alleges that the German army is
a mere machine—and a bumptious and over-rated
machine at that—has at least, some precedent to
back up his opinion. The German army has
done great things under first-class commanders;
under bad commanders it has crumbled away as
few other European armies have done. It seems
to have a curiously-mechanical turn of mmd,
which makes it a splendid weapon when a master-
mind supplies the brains. But lacking that
master-mind it has no individual brain of its own ;
therefore, when badly-led it produces much worse
results than a much worse force might do. A few
commanders—Tilly, Wallenstein, Prince Eugene
(probably the greatest of them all), the Archduke
Charles, the Great Elector, E rede nek the Great,
even Blueher who had at least his indomitable
grit to recommend him—have led German armies
to the gates of perdition. And, on the other
hand, no country in Europe ever made a more
pitiable showing than Prussia after Jena, when
the dull old machine went to pieces and great,
sohd fortresses with garrisons 10,000 strong
would surrender to a few hundred French
cavalry the surrendering troops doing the most
intensely accurate goose-step to the last and
having every button in its proper place but not
hr.ng il An a r ay , d *T n , ■ fclun - k ’ led *! y
a general who can do all the thinking, is pretty
nearly invincible. An army that doesn't think,
led by a general that doesn’t think, is something
quite different. On the other hand, the army
that thinks for itself is apt to be the despair of. a.
good general, while it can turn guerilla and raise
Cain for its enemy with practically no general at
all.

It appears that a member of the local force was in pur-
suit of a lad who, with another lad, had just previously
committed a theft from a shop in Queen-street, Woollahra.
After a stern chase, in which the young culprit held his
own, the constable, intending to frighten the lad, drew
his revolver and shot high into the air. The lad inline-
diately fell down, and was captured and lodged in the
lockup. So far as could be ascertained, a loaf of bread i<
all that was missing.

GROSVENOR STREET
Patience has been rewarded at Mitchellstown,
Vic., where one Ponting, a local cocky, has just cut a
reef that scores of experienced mining men have been
searching for for over 18 years. The reef is called the
Wallaby, cocky having discovered the outcrop when deal-
ing with a “wally” that his dogs had pulled down.
Prospects are making mouths water all over the place,
and the rush has set in. The maddest man in all Goul-
burn Valley is the one whose year-old cutting stopped
just about a foot short of the lode ! There have been
even nearer things. At Mt. Moliagul, 30 years ago, a
sneeze in a deserted tunnel brought down a flake of reef
that exposed the golden stone.
« * * *
The two recent attempts to revivify Lady Loch,
Coolgardie having ignominiously failed through the
wrong medicine having been applied, a fresh pill has been
prepared. A new co. is to be launched with 299,993
shares of ss. each with a 2s. assessment.. The New
Century Trust is backing this scheme. Mine never was
and never will be any good for a big co. ; it might pay a
good co-operative party.
• * »
The long-threatened absorption of Hannan’s
Oroya by Hannan’s Brown Hill (W.A.) (for that is what
the amalgamation virtually means) was agreed to unani-
mously by shareholders of both co.’s. To show market
appreciation of the combine, Oroya shares dropped Bs., or
10 per cent., on meeting being convened, and as much
more on amalgamation being carried. Sooner or later
Associated Northern, and possibly Associated, which both
intervene between sections of the properties of the new
combine, will also be absorbed, as Algernon Moreing is
fairly on the warpath. To date Brown Hill has produced
340,5000 z. from 170,300 tons, and Oroya 32,8000 z. from
67,100 tons. The former co. has worked out the rich
chute while the latter has it intact.
Latest published return from Peak Hill (W.A.)
reads 31690 z. from 4843 tons. More than half comes from
the accumulated rich tailings, which are being exhausted
at the rate of nearly 9000 tons a month. Darlington Simp-
son, who recently visited the mine, professes to be horribly
shocked at the falling-off . He writes : “It becomes
difficult for one to explain the marvellous change that has
occurred in, I may say, almost everything since my last
visits in 1898-99.” It might not be so difficult to explain
the marvellous cessation of divs. after these visits, and
the drop in the shares from £6 to about par, but it would
not be an easy matter to demonstrate that the shares are
worth even par.
• * * *
Test crushing of 140 tons, from Golden Link’s
Eclipse lease, Kalgoorlie, yielded 150 oz. smelted gold.
Ore was taken from the extreme south-east corner of the
property, where the lode is split into two branches by a
fault. An engine-driver, named Berry, who long knew of
this patch, and had pitched his camp on the outcrop to
shepherd it, recently gave away the show out of pique, as
another miner, who had been granted a tribute, while
Berry was refused one, was warm on the scent. Result ■
Berry was told to remove his camp, and the fossicker’s
tribute was promptly annulled. As lode is underlying
east and chute dipping south into block held by Venture
Corporation, value to Golden Link co. is apparently of
smallest.
* * * *
Windsor Consols, Mt. Magnet (W.A.), like most
of Frank Gardner’s flotations, is to be wound up, as Zeb.
Lane is satisfied the co. has no prospects. In floating
co. 2J years ago Lane asserted that 9000 tons, containing
13,0000 z., were in sight, but 20-head battery knocked
bottom out of mine after treating 3611 tons for 9220 z.
» • * *
Manager Isles, of South Kalgurli (W.A.), once
estimated that there would be £5OOO a month profit from
the plant, but he has now reduced this to a paltry £2200.
This means that working expenses on 2700 tons of ounce
ore are £2 15s. per ton. He suggests adding further fur-
naces, but, unless they are suspended from a balloon, it is
hard to say where they are to be placed.
• * * *
Great Boulder and Ivanhoe (W.A.) were paying
unskilled labor Is. Bd. a shift less than other Kalgoorlie
mines, and the Stokers’ Association ordered them to level
up their rates. Managers combined and decided to level
down to Boulder-Ivanhoe rates. Case to come before
Arbitration Court.
» * * •
The crosscut'from the bottom level on Oroya’s
(W.A.) north block is out 90ft. without locating the rich
chute, and holders are in despair. That the chute will be
cut before long there seems little doubt, and the further
east it is the better for shareholders. The management
seems to be at fault for its dilatoriness in pushing
on explorations; had this chute been properly opened
up, the amalgamation would probably have been frus-
trated.

Kaufmann and Rothschild, who held a 12
months’ option on Fred. Merton’s 100,000 shares in Mer-
ton’s Reward, are said to have closed the bargain and
paid over the cash. With the £lO,OOO previously paid,
and the gold won, Merton has made a punch of £200,000
out of his find. Not so long ago, he was counterjumping
for Sam. Hordern at £2 a week, and knew naught ahont
gold-mining.

Some Vic. mining figures: Total gold to end of
'Ol, 65,136,1740 z. (£260,489,201) ; heaviest year’s output,
3,053,7440 z., in ’56; lightest, 576,3990 z., in ’9l ; average
of stone treated in ’Ol, Bdwt. 16gr. (9dwt. 23gr. in ’92) ;
total stone milled to end of ’Ol, 35,703,933 tons for
17,979,6640 z. ; tailings cyanided, 4,293,532 tons for
43,5690 z. Co.’s paying divs. in ’Ol, 44. South New Moon
paid £38,400, on a paid-up capital of £14,670, from
15.6dwt. stone ; Glenfine South, £35,875. New Moon paid
£22,200 on 6.4dwt. stone. Of the £427,897 Bendigo dis-
tributed £184,670 and Ballarat £101,650. At the end
of ’Ol eight shafts, all at Bendigo, were deeper than
3000 ft.; deepest, Victoria Quartz, 3750 ft. Outside Bendigo,
South Star (Ballarat), 2520 ft. ; North Long Tunnel (Wal-
halla), 2516 ft. In the last 20 years 790 miners have been
killed ; average per year, 39J : last year, 28. Total em-
ployed, 27,387 (lowest, 23,763, in ’92 ; highest in the last 15
years, 32,368, in ’96). Of 1295 men killed in 28 years 550
have been through falls of rock, &c., underground, 171
through falling down shafts, 99 through explosions, and
71 through falls of material down shafts. Flooding of
mines has killed 36.
• * * •
Vic. miners are snorting over the new Gold-
Buyers’ Act, which forces every man selling gold or
amalgam to fill in an imposing blue document giving no
end of details. There are about a score of questions to
be answered, and a declaration to be sworn, before a
fossicker can sell a few grains of gold. As a means of
detecting gold-stealers the documents aren’t worth their
weight in dead flies—the assumption that the man who
would steal gold would be above making a false declara-
tion being a cheerful mistake of the official mind ; but as
an instrument for driving many ancient and illiterate
fossickers to lunacy they are a brilliant success.
* » « *
English mining writer Curie, who so shocked
Melb. Argus with his criticism of Vic. systems of mining,
that it cut him short after his introductory article
appeared, has published another edition of his “ Gold-
fields of the World.” The new vol. contains the complete
criticism which the Argus declined to publish. There is
really not much in it. Curie seems to have a bee in his
bonnet re the dissection of working costs. It is true that
no Vic. co. employs a certificated accountant with a tribe
of clerks to dance attendance on him. The manager has
to act as his own tally-clerk and accountant, and be
satisfied with a smaller screw than a man of figures
would be content with. But the most elaborate tabulated
statement of costs would not be more convincing as to
results than the simple fact that in proportion to gold
won Victorian co’s. on an average return a higher rate of
profit than is shown in any other part of the world.
Curie is also strong on the necessity for an assay-plan of
a mine. Perhaps it was the absence of a correct assay-
plan which was responsible for the opinion which he ex-
pressed in his first book that the Associated (W.A.) was
the greatest mine in the world. The average assay-plan
is of no more value as a guide to the true inwardness of
things than the average written report—one can be
cooked and manipulated as readily as the other.
• • * •
■■ A novel legal point has been raised in connection
with the cyanide process. In England the Macarthur-
Forrest patents were held valid, but the courts of the
S’African Republic decided otherwise, and the mining
co.’s were freed from the payment of royalties. The
question now is : Will the English decision hold good in
the new Crown colony as applied to mines which were
“ located ” under the laws of the Republic ?

the mine-inspectors of the fielus. With the exception of

sons, as most of the inspectors have become too chummy
with the managers, and their zeal has waned.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The Steeplechase at Ballarat Miners’ Race-
course next Friday is causing more than usual interest to be
taken in the meeting. The club has been exceptionally
fortunate in attracting the splendid entry of ‘ a baker’s
dozen ’ for the race. The fences are not extra high, but
one or two of them are termed awkward—decidedly
‘ tricky ’ —so that should this large number of horses start
a few spills may naturally be expected.” (Par in Ballarat
Courier and Ballarat Star, July 22nd).
A neat little swindle went adrift lately on a
northern racecourse. Two ponies—A and B—were
reckoned certs, for a race. Owners foregathered and
.arranged that A should win, carrying underweight, while
B should run second, and, of course, gather in stakes and
bets on A failing to scale. The gentle public was sooled
on to A, and at the last moment a tidy commission was
worked for B at an outside price. All came off as
arranged, but, while the nags were weighing in, some
greedy individuals offered to lay 10 to 1 the winner
wouldn’t get the race ; and this haste to grab the last
Eossible copper gave the show away. The owner of third
orse lodged his protest and demanded “ No race,” while
the deluded punters yelled murder. Last of all, the
committeemen—possibly having backed A themselves-
took a firm grasp of the situation, gave the race to the
under-weighted winner, and, presumably, went out to
collect their own bets.
* • • ♦
Winner (Col. Shillinski) of Caulfield Grand
National Hurdles, Saturday, put up a weight-carrying
record for the race; he humped 12.5. Prior best was The
Agent’s 12.1, 17 years ago. Present owner of Colonel
Shillinski bought him for £5OO, and a few days later
gathered in a £5OO stake with him. Latest win means an
additional £9OO.

Extent to which horse-racing is carried on in
and around Sydney may be gathered from the fact that
something in excess of £50,000 has been disbursed in prize
money during the past season, aud £24,770 of this through
A.J.O. officials’ hands. Tatts. Club gave £3OOO, S.T.C.
£2llO, and City Tatts. £9OO, so beyond £30,000 was
won at Randwick alone. Rosehill Club stakes accumu-
lated £4400, Canterbury Park and Moorefield £3600 each,
and Warwick Farm £2700. Add to this Hawkesbury
Club’s t'looo and perhaps £20,000 provided by unregis-
tered pony clubs and there’s a grand total of somewhere
near £70,000. Parlous period the country has passed and
is passing through doesn’t yet seem to have been felt in
turfdom.
• * * *
Broken Hill now makes but one try to get
metrop. horses up there ; it offers three meetings, run at
week-intervals by three friendly clubs, with a fourth on
the S.A. side of the border where the dusty miner can
get a taste of the tote. As a matter of fact, though, the
dusty miner isn’t keen on racing ..anyway at Broken Hill;
his chances of going to races are so few that when they do
come he seems to have forgotten how to take them, and
so for the size of the place the attendances are poor. The
Sailor, last year’s Silver City Cup winner, did it again ;
and Quando'ng, just in from the mulga, took back the
B.H. Cup (200 sovs.) and what the books would lay at 20
to 1.
# * • *
J. E. Burgess, now waiting trial for the opal
mail robbery between White Cliffs and Broken Hill, is
one of the oldest and best-known racing men in the Far
West. They “ took ” him just before the annual carnival
at Broken Hill—escorted him across to White Cliffs the
day before the L.V. meeting, and committed him the day
before the B.H. J.C. meeting a week afterwards. Burgess
had two horses entered for the bigger things, Garnet and
Precaution ; but his luck was dead out on the course, as
elsewhere—he won a second and two thirds with Pre-
caution, and Garnet, with a good lead in the B.H. Cup,
broke a blood-vessel. Burgess ran horses at the first
meeting ever held at Broken Hill, and has had something
going in his name most of the time since.
• » • *
Well-performed-up-country nag Winoline won
her second race at Moorefield (Sydney) Monday this week,
humping top weight, and shaping promisingly. She is a
big, finely-formed mare by Blairgowrie from Winnie by
Warlike, who first saw light in the Richmond River
district, and figured in Sydney as a speedy traveller over
short stretches. Winoline has at different times annexed
Forbes, Albury, and Cootamundra Cups.

* • • •
A new dodge came to light at a meeting of
Queensland Tattersall’s Club, at Sandgate, t’other day,
when the officials accidentally found that some brilliant
mind had been at work on the scales in the weighing-
room. A string had been attached to the lever of the
scales and taken down below the floor, where a piece of
lead was affixed to keep it taut. From this, a length of
twine led to the outside world. On being followed to the
end. it was found to terminate at a fence, some distance
away. Here, apparently, the bright inventor had stood,
and, bj- hauling on the string, had altered the weights
materially of the jocks at the weighing-in. How long had
that ingenious device been in operation ?
» • • *
Recent raids on Sydney shop Mo. bore little
fruit, for most cases were upset on technicalities, the out-
come of ignorance in laying charges and wording informa-
tion. Full Court, t’other day, dealt with several and
decided against the police in each instance, allowing costs
to all but one, and then only because the points were not
taken in the lower court. Time the duty of prosecution
was placed with someone sufficiently learned in the law
to save this farce and expense.
* * *
The famous St. Albans stud farm and racing
establishment of the late W. R. Wilson has been con-
verted into a model dairy farm by the present owner, Mr.
R. Govett. The moo-cow roams where the thoroughbred
was wont to scamper, and now St. Albans reckons it has
won a good race when it gets the best market odds for its
milk and butter.

Progress of trotting in M.L. is evidenced by the
fact that stakes given during the past season were £l6OO
richer than previous year, while tote revenue showed an
excess of £20,000.
• • * •
Some bike and motor-car gossip : While he was
a fat and middle-aged P. of W., King Ned always had a
desire to do something on the bike, but was too short-
winded, and a poor devil of a Prince of Wales has no time
to train. He was rapidly becoming a soured old man
when the motor-car irrupted. Ned clutched at it
frenziedly, and proceeded to fill the beautiful English lanes
with odors of petrol and gasoline ; now, he has such a fleet
of automobiles that a new household dignitary has been
appointed, with a most gorgeous uniform, under the title
of “ Master of the King’s Motor-cars.” ... In Europe,
just now, there is a craze for light bicycles. Cor-
rect thing is aluminium, but the price makes
it a rare luxury. Machines over 281 b. are a drug in the
market, and the coming season’s casualty-list will be
heavy, when thousands of 15st. and 18st. men start road-
racing on machines which, all on, weigh no more than
321 b. Even the best steel and rubber can only stand a
certain strain. The English manufacturers have got a
big start of the Yankees with the duplex-gear machines.
The Yanks were so carried away with the free-wheel suc-
cess that the multiple-gear was ignored. With the
Yankee free-wheel, a man can coast in great comfort, but he
has to climb rises with the same gear that he floats along
the levels, and, if you want to appreciate the advantages
of a duplex-gear, just try to walk up a hill with the same
stride you get over the level street with. The new hub
will make comparatively little addition to the cost.
• * * •
They were illuminating at Temuka (M.L.) in
honor of the King’s illness, and the town being largely
composed of inflammable timber buildings, it was decided
by the city fathers to light up in a desert place, locally
spoken of as “ the Hills,” outside the township. While
the show was in full blast, a cyclist came to the top of
the ridge and looked down on the spectacle of rockets
and catherine-wheels. At the same moment he dis-
covered that sparks from the show had set his coat on
fire. While he grabbed at it to try and smother the fire
something went wrong with his free wheel, and the
machine promptly bolted with him, and as the last rocket
vanished in a spray of stars, the succeeding dark-
ness was lit up by a shrieking cyclist, who shot down
the slope on to the level ground with his coat-tail, which
streamed out on the wind behind him, a sheet of flame.
The audience cheered wildly, and marvelled at the stage-
management of the spectacle. Presently they found that
the apparition wasn’t part of the show, and extinguished
him with boiling tea from the refreshment booth. Then
the cyclist explained things—among other things that,
in his tail-pocket, there had been burnt a couple of
pound-notes, with which he had intended to shout for
the crowd. Just here the audience ceased to take any
interest in him, and faded quietly away before he had
time to propose that a collection should be taken up.
* * * •
Cyclists will learn with interest that a big
development in cycle tyre construction is promised by
the Dunlop Tyre Co. early in August. The big company
has secured the Australasian rights of a new process of
tyre manufacturing that will mean much to wheelmen,
in fact will almost result in a clearance of all cover
troubles. The principle of the Dunlop tyre attachment
is adhered to, but the new cover will be lighter, faster
and yet more durable than the existing one, and, despite
the new improvements, will be considerably cheaper.*
• * * *
Trumper’s pair of centuries against Essex must
have been almost the best batting achievement ever
recorded in first-class cricket. It was not as though the
other fellows had helped him to collar the bowling.
Sixteen wickets fell for 485 runs. Trumper’s contributions
were 109 and 119, whilst the next best were Hill 59 (2nd
innings) and Noble 33 not out (Ist innings). Thirteen of
the wickets that fell were accountable for 125 runs only,
an average of less than 10 runs per wicket. With
Trumper out of the way the bowling would have looked
pretty deadly. Two of the Essex trundlers—veteran
Mead and H. Young—were in the All England lot that
met Darling’s team three years ago, Young being then
regarded as the coming man. The match ended in a
draw.
On the other side, conspicuously good perform-
ances in the Essex match were M'Gahey with 72 and 59,
and Perrin with 45 and 58 (not out), M'Gahey, who also
put a couple of good wickets to his credit as a bowler, is
evidently a much better cricketer than he showed himself
in this country last summer. His average for three
innings against Australia on the Essex ground is 55.
Perrin has done better than that, and if batsmen were
chosen for their proven pluck and consistency of “ form”
against the terror-striking visitors Perrin would play for
All England in the final Test. One man of his type,
notorious for bucking-up when the occasion puts a
special strain upon the nerves, is worth a couple of
Ranjitsinjhis. The dark gentleman, at any rate, has
fairly earned the distinction of being carefully omitted
from the next A.E. team. His four Test innings this
season (he couldn’t play in the third match) have pro-
duced 13, 0, 2 and 4, yet he stands high on the batting
list for county matches.
* * *
Vic. Trumper’s two centuries against Essex
made his eighth time of netting three figures this tour,
and excels anything previously done by an Australian in
a first-class match. Not at any time, even in the history
of Anglo-Australian or inter-State cricket, has a rep. of
any portion of this continent accumulated centuries in
both innings of any one match. This brings Trumper
into line with (among others) W. G. Grace, R. E. Foster,
Stoddart, Ranjitsinhji, Fry, Maclaren, Tyldesley, Storer,
Brann, Poore, W. L. Foster, Jessop, Bosanquet, Chinnery,
M'Gahey and Carpenter. Grace and R. E. Foster accom-
plished the feat thrice, and Fry and Tyldesley twice. A
hundred in each innings of a match other than first-class
has been comparatively common, and Deane, Alex. Ban-
nerman, Eady and Burn have such achievements to their
credit. It is interesting, also, that the absolute hundred
has been obtained seven times. Up to meeting Essex,
Trumper had accumulated centuries in four successive
innings. Worth mentioning here, too, that Miss R.
Dean, of Sydney, once hit up two separate hundreds in a
ladies’ match.
The success of Relf at the outset of the Aus-
tralian innings against Sussex was just another illustra-
tion of the truth that a new second-rate bowler can beat
first-rate batsmen by the mere novelty of his manner of
attack. The man, not the ball, takes them down until
they are familiar with his delivery. Relf got the first four
wickets when the Australian total was 90 only. After
that hundreds of runs were made for the loss of one
wicket—Hopkins getting run out. The new bowler had
lost his character for being dangerous.
• * * *
Dull and threatening without rain at Brighton
during Australian-Sussex match last week. Toss favored
visiting side, who opened proceedings, and closed at 5 for
580, Noble making 284 and Armstrong (n. 0.) 172. Sussex
hit up 185 ; highest scores being 58 and 39 ; and, following
on, they had lost 1 for 130 when the match was declared
drawn. Peculiarly enough, the ’99 team against Sussex
closed its first innings, but with more runs to the good,
there were 4 down for 624. Sussex made 414 and 143 ;
then Trumper’s quota towards the general whole of his
side was 300 (n. 0.) : Worrall subscribed 128, Gregory 73,
and Darling 56 (n. 0.).
* * « •
Noble’s 284, achieved in the Sussex match, ranks
third among highest scores made in Anglo-Australian
cricket. Trumper’s 300 (n. 0.) against the same county in
’99 is ahead of all others, while Murdoch’s 286 (n. 0.), also
opposed to Sussex, is the second on the list; latter was
made in ’B2, and formed part of 643 which resulted in
Sussex’s vanquishment by an innings and 335 runs. Then,
as with Noble now, Murdoch’s was the highest score in
English cricket for that season. Noble has livened up
considerably with the bat lately, but seems to have fallen
off correspondingly in bowling.
* * * V
Apropos Trumble’s 100th wicket in test-matches.
Only man to accomplish this previously was C. T. B.
Turner. Trumble first played against England 12 years
since at Lords. A comparison of Turner’s, Spofforth’s
and Trumble’s figures show that the first-named was
the most difficult; his record gives 101 wickets for 1681
runs—an average of 16 64.
• * * *
In a cricket-match this season between Glaston-
bury and Langport (Eng.) the latter scored nil. Has
such a thing ever occurred in Australia ?
* ♦ * *
Football is immensely popular in Melbourne
again. At Brunswick, t’other day, after the match
Brunswick v. Prahran, the umpire was rushed by a homi-
cidal crowd, and the police who went to the rescue were
freely belted with rocks. The free fight lasted while the
police were getting the umpire* from the ground to the
tram, and the constables batoned the crowd with cheerful
indiscrimination. Says one conspicuous Melb. umpire :
“I don’t mind a mauling once in a way. When the
crowd’s bloodthirsty it shows they are taking an interest
in the game. It’ll be a bad look-out for football when the
crowd don’t want to kill an umpire now’n agen.”
* * • *
Obit. Sydney yachting enthusiast, Dr. Milford,
ordering on 70 years old. His first successes were with
the Young Jack, years ago ; then he owned the Doris,
and sailed her from Sydney to Melbourne, where she com-
peted more or less successfully. Subsequently the Sao
was built for him, then the Isea. Latterly the doc. was
content to cruise about Sydney Harbour in a 14-footer.
Valvular disease of the heart was the immediate cause of
death. The deceased gentleman was a son of Mr. Justice
Milford, whilom N.S.W. Chief Judge in Equity.
* # * •
Cabled that ’Ome ex-bike premier, M. A. Hol-
bein, unsuccessfully essayed the Channel swim last week,
and during 12£ "hours covered 39 miles. Same
man tried the same thing August last year, taking the
water at Calais, and getting within five miles of the Eng-
lish side when his friends hauled him out verging on
blindness. Though then in the sea 12} hours, he was
physically fit to complete the task. Among Holbein’s
best swims were 46 miles in the Solent, and to and from
Blackwall and Gravesend, which called for 12| hours
swimming.

Recent print of Sydney Sportsman, talking
“Coursing Reform,” suggests handicapping dogs to bring
them nearer each other. Yet coursing is among the
oldest sports known. Surely there’s some deep-down
joke here?
THE WARBLE OF IKEY MO.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
11 Roll up, you toffs as dearly loves the Sport of Kings,
Roll up I and patronise the Turf, an’ see what Fortune
brings ;
There’s thirteen ’orses on the card for this ’ere steeple-
chase,
And maybe some of them wot starts will perish in the
race.
‘ The timber ain’t so extra ’igh, but still, its ’igh enough,
A haccident might ’appen to the smartest bit o’ stuff,
For some of them there obstacles are just the sort, yersee,
To bring the favorite to grief and do the trick for Me.
“ Wif thirteen crowding in the field there’s bound to be a
show
For two to bump when up they jump, then down the
blankers go,
An’ three or four falls over them, a rorty, all-round
smash;
Then, if your rough outsider wins, yer taps me for the
cash.
“There’s nothing like a steeplechase to keep yer in
suspense ;
The safest jumper in the land may blunder at a fence
And kill his jockey on the spot; it’s very sad, of course,
Especially for blokes wot’s put their quids upon the
’orse.
" You back your fancies on their “ form,” and when
they’re in the air
I follows them with hopeful eyes, and offers up a prayer—
Ah ! you may larf; I’ve known the time when earnestly
I’ve prayed
That everything would fall except the one I ’adn’t laid.
“ And you don’t mind if all the rest get seriously ’urt,
Provided you can thank your stars you pick’d the
bloomin’ cert.
In fact, we’re Sports ; we play the game for shillings,
pounds, and pence—
Roll up, and give your luck a chance to clear the tricky
fence.”
The tricky fence provides the spills Ike “ natchrally
expecks,”
Men gather near to hear the crack of breaking legs, or
necks,
And faces blanch, whilst others beam, and bloodshot eye-
balls shine
When comes the cry, “There’s something down—thank
Gawd 1 it isn’t mine I ”

The British empire spends about
£82,000,000 a year on armaments; Russian empire,
.£42,000,000'; France, £38,000,000; German empire,
£32,000,000. Yet Britain has quite a fixed im-
pression that it is the other fellow who has the
bloated war-gear !

OPEN SESAME.— Hunyadi Janos Water is
the “open sesame” of the human organism.
Speedy, sure, and gentle, it fulfils every indication
in health and disease. It is a therapeutical
arsenal in itself. Indispensable to millions of
human beings all over the world.*
There’s a fine lesson for the un-Australian
patriots who heard John Bull’s call to S’Africa, in
the story of the Drayton Grange, now on Aus-
tralian coast. This “ converted cargo boat ” was
despatched from Durban with 2000 men aboard.
Measles had then appeared among the men, but
nevertheless she was rushed away. There was
one small medicine-chest aboard, and “a request
for further medical stores was grudgingly acceded
to.” From the first at least 400 men had to sleep
out on deck--—though the weather was bitterly
cold. When measles began to rage, and scores of
men fell sick of pleurisy and pneumonia, it was
impossible to secure hospital accommodation. To
quote Sydney Telegraph :
From end to end of the ship men, many of them worn
with privations and fatigue, are berthed under the most
insanitary conditions. On one troop deck alone 1500 men
eat and live. By night they sleep as best they can. On
the lower troop deck the balance are stowed. They have
little ventilation in fine weather; none in bad. So dense
is the crowding that many are unable to find room to
swing their hammocks, and perforce sleep on the mess
tables, while the still more unfortunate spread their
blankets on the befouled decks. . . . The men are unable
to take exercise or fresh air. Parades are .impossible,
except at the tables, where the men live and sleep, and
discipline is necessarily lax. . . . The troop hospital, with
accommodation for 40, was soon filled, and a temporary
hospital was established on the main troop deck, a space
amidships being divided off with canvas. Twenty-four
hours later that was full, and bunks were built in the
officers’ smoking-room, and filled as fast as they were
finished. The orderly-room came next, a wooden erection
measuring 15ft. by sft. by 7ft. high. In this 16 patients
were stacked, giving an average of 35 cubic feet of space
for each. To put another one there was impossible, and
the quarters occupied by troops were requisitioned.
Friction followed. The men, foreseeing further crowding,
declined at first to move. An appeal to them on behalf
of the sick men, however, was sufficient to move them,
and yet another hospital was established and filled.
And thus the pest-ship came into Australian
waters.
It looked at first as though the Aust. XI.
were going to be knocked fl t by the unusually
cold English Spring plus influenza. But, as
usual, the Boys have proved themselves equal to
any foe, as they always will so long as they remain
faithful to their old allj r , D.C.L. Whisky—taken
medicinally. A judicious appeal to D.C.L. at the
right moment, and in accurate quantum, gives the
strength of nerve which assures victory. The
Whisky of the Wise ; likewise the Triumph of
the Brave.*
The death, at the age of 87, of Win.
Hanson, first N.S.W. Govt. Printer under
Responsible Govt., recalls the woes of Geo. Howe,
the printer who first brought out the Sydney
Gazette in 1803. Sometimes he had to use paper
bags for printing his paper, and at others bnina
paper, which could only be printed on one side,
while arrears of subscriptions were only collected
with difficulty after the Governor had issued a
threatening proclamation.
Toni Bent, in his revived character of
Minister of Vic. Railways, justifies his action in
“shutting up” the sec. of the Engined rivers
Association who had called upon him with a depu-
tation. The sec. was disrespectful, says the auto-
crat. “ There is a proper method of addressing a
Minister, no matter who he is.” Bent apparently
recognises that a deputation of engine-drivers
would naturally be indisposed.to regard Bent with
the awe due to his official position, and yet he is
the only Minister of Railways who ever was an
ex-Speaker of the Leg. Assembly.
The unfortunate shooting of the poor
itinerant chair-mender, Joseph Heaps, recalls
another instance of mistaken zeal. During the
Kelly gang scare Father Scan 1 an was mistaken for
Dan Kelly and shot at a public-house in Urana.
The priest mistook the police and civilians for
burglars, or something, drew his. revolver,, and
was shot dead. His grave railing is. still pointed
out in Wangaratta (Vic.) cemetery, in a neglected
corner of which is also buried the headless body of
bushranger Morgan, shot at Peechelba Station on
the Ovens in the early sixties.

Several humanising measures lost in last
session of Vic. Parliament have again been intro-
duced. First is Dr. Maloney’s bill to legitimise
children by registration under the Registration of
Births, Deaths and Marriage Acts. This bill
passed -the Assembly on a former occasion, but
was shelved by the godly House of bald-headed
landlords. It provides that either parent of an
illegitimate may register it in the records of the
Govt. Statist and that upon such registration
being made the child shall be deemed to be
legitimised, and on the death of the father or
mother shall have all the rights pertaining to the
property of the parent who registered it as are
possessed by any legitimate child. Another
civilised measure is one introduced by Agar
Wynne for the registration and control of money-
lenders. This bill requires all persons carrying on
business as money-lenders to register and keep a
record of their transactions for inspection. It
further provides that the courts may review any
loan transaction in which the interest stipulated
for by the loan office man is harsh or unconscion-
able. The bill, however, exempts banks and
linancial institutions—which does not seem logical.
To judge from stories told by backblox squatters,
banks and financial institutions number among
them some very first-class vampires.
Knight M‘Eacharn, lately returned from a
trip to Japan, explains to the press that the
little brown Government over there feels very
sore about the Aliens Restriction Act, and will
probably withdraw the subsidy from the Aus-
tralian steamers of the Nippon Yusen Sneeze line.
Why so many persons should find it necessary to
tell us, with a terrified expression, that Japan is
very angry and feels much insulted over our anti-
alien legislation, it is difficult to say. The Jap
himself is a most insulting person, who classes
Australians, Chinese and niggers together as
persons who are unfit to own land in his country,
and he shuts them out of retail trade in a high-
handed way that Australia has never dreamt of in
its most restrictive moments. The brown person
began the insulting process of restriction ; he car-
ries it further than we do even now; and, if he
doesn’t like it, he had better lump it. As for the
Nippon Yusen Sneeze line of steamers, it matters
very little how soon they leave off. The trade
is exceedingly one-sided. Australia buys a great
deal from Japan, and Japan buys very little in
return. Knight M‘Eacharn admits that the pros-
pect of ever doing a large export business to the
Jap. isles is very limited. The Jap. raises horses
more cheaply than we do, and he lias already more
meat than he wants and is exporting a little. So
Adstralia might as well leave off dealing with the
Jap. and spend the money in encouraging France
or Germany or Belgium—countries that buy
liberally from us and deserve any encouragement
we can spare.

AUDITORS’ REPORT.
We have examined the foregoing Aggregate Balance Sheet, made up to 30th June, 1902, and report that we rind
the several items therein stated to conform to the Balances in the General Ledger at Head Office, and to the Balance
Sheets furnished by the Branches ; these latter being certified to by the respective Managers and Accountants.
We have counted the Cash, and examined the Bills held by the Bank at the Head Office in Brisbane, on the 30th
ultimo, and hereby certify that we have found them correct. The Securities held ; at the Head Office have, at the
request of the Directors, been examined by Messrs. Chambers, Bruce & McNab, Solicitors to the Bank, and we have
seen their certificate to the effect that they are in order.
We have further to certify that in our opinion the said Aggregate Balance Sheet is properly drawn up, and
exhibits a true and correct view of the Bank’s affairs as shown by the Books on the 30th June, 1902.
GEO. FRED. SCOTT, 8.A., F.1.A.Q.) Anditora
