The new material—magnalium—of which
the Austrian Govt, is hawing cavalry-swords made,
is a compound of aluminium and magnesium, and
is said to combine surprising lightness with great
strength and flexibility. It is with this material
that Maxim proposes in future to build flying-
machines.
A curious decision, given in a case decided
early last century, was cited in a Melbourne law-
court t’other day, in support of the contention
that foals are the property of the owner of the
mother
In this authority it was shown that the Roman law
established the right of ownership, with an extraordinary
distinction in the case of swans. While in all animals the
owner of the female was entitled to the progeny, the
cygnets belonged equally to the owners of the father and
the mother. The learned Judge is reported to have said,
“And the law thereof is founded on a reason in nature,
for the cock swan is an emblem of representation of an
affectionate and true husband to his wife above all other
fowls ; for the cock swan holdeth himself to one female
only, and for this cause nature hath conferred on him a
gift beyond all others, that is to die so cheerfully that he
sings sweetly when he dies, upon which the poet saith :
' Dulcia defecta modulator carmina lingua, Cantator,
cygnus, funeris ipse sui,’ &c.”
Also, Ribuck and Rumbo.
To such depths does the silliness of Aus-
tralian politics descend at times. Notice of ques-
tion in N.S. W. Assembly:
Dr. Ross to ask the Minister of Public Instruction—As
the Times reprint of the “ Encyclopaedia Britannica ” has
lately been submitted to the public of New South Wales
by the London Daily Mail, through the Sydney press, on
the most liberal terms, viz., a deposit of ss. and an instal-
ment of 12s. per month, can he furnish this House with
any information as to the respective Public Libraries and
various Schools of Arts in the State that are in possession
of this publication, or the number of copies, if any, such
institutions here are likely to order of this cheap and
most valuable publication ?
There should be an order of the House authorising
the Ministry, on extreme occasions, to set aside
its natural politeness and wink derisively at the
hon. member, instead of going into details. Dr.
Ross was the model for Phil May’s Bulletin pic-
ture of Diogenes looking for an honest man ; only
the limitations of the printing-press prevented the
old gentleman from being colored the bright green
which the case called for.
Another move has been made in Vic.
Legislature to unite the two rival country
parties, which have for their admitted, object
the filching of everything possible from the
Treasury for the country districts, and the
enabling of water trusts and other yokel
bodies to get hundreds of thousands of loan
money from Govt, and forget to pay it back. It
was an attempt to bring the country parties
together that resulted lately in the “chucking”
of Peacock, and billets in the Irvine Govt, for
Taverner and McKenzie, the heads of the two
factions. Those two cute politicians used their
followers to climb up, and then kicked the human
ladder from under them. And the new move is,
of course, the usual scheme of the disappointed
ones for themselves accumulating the influence
which leads to the good old Treasury benches.
Extract from a description of the British
military officer, condensed from the report of a
British official committee which recently sat to
inquire into that promising person :
Learning in the army is laughed at rather than en-
couraged, and female influence is more powerful than
ability and knowledge. “ Games ” have ruled the roost.
The man of sport is petted, and the man of science
snubbed and ignored. Candidates have got into the
cavalry who secured only 400 out of a possible 2000 marks.
In 1900, while the war was at its height, “ the staff at
Aldershot found it necessary to complain of the ignorance
of the junior officers sent to them. They were valueless,
yet some of them who had been warned that they must
leave the service owing to inefficiency and want of
knowledge were sent off to South Africa in charge of
men.” Dr. Maguire mentions, in the course of an inter-
view, the case of an officer who was advanced over the
heads of his seniors, who were better qualified, apparently
on account of his “ versatility in arranging tableaux
vivants.” The case of another favored officer is thus
described : —“ At noon he failed niiserablv in his pre-
liminary examination, but four hours later‘he had been
appointed to a regiment in South Africa. It is an open
secret that during the last few years any army candidate
who happened to be well connected troubled himself but
little about the preliminary, or indeed any subsequent,
examination. Whether he failed or passed was to him
immaterial, provided he had sufficient influence at his
back, for then he was sure to obtain his commission.”
The same witness told the committee that there has been
an enormous general deterioration in the men offering
themselves for army commissions as compared with the
class available some years ago. “They know nothing at
all prope: las a rule.” . . . According to the Sandhurst
systc. . of marks, a knowledge of French or German is
consi.: red of equal importance with tactics ! Musketry,
signalling and veterinary science are entirely neglected.
.Cadets, have to pay extra to' learn how to shoot. At
Woolwich the cadets are supplied with a muzzleloading
6.6-in. howitzer without a carriage to learn their business.
... . For . many years past there had been a great
difficulty in getting the class of officers “we want.”
“ You mean,” said a member of the committee, “that
there is a difficulty in getting officers both physically and
intellectually capable?” “I am sorry to say,” replied
the witness, “ that the officer wanted in tne army is only
one who can command from £l5O to £l5OO a year : there
is no room at all in the army—and this comes before me
every day—for the man who has got only £5O a year of
his own.” He confessed, further, that “it is, as a rule,
only the boys who will not work who go into the cavalry,
and we are obliged to take them, as there are no other
candidates.”

The death is announced at Adelaide of
Wm. Jacobs, of Moorooroya, S.A., a pioneer
of that State, aged 88. Ho arrived with Col.
Light in the Rapid and assisted in the first suryey
of Adelaide.
Herzog, the ex-Orange Free State judge,
who is not exercising a pacific influence just now,
is quite a young man. the beginning of the
war he resigned from the Bench and took charge
of the O.F.S. commissariat department, which
stood badly in need of an organiser, and in which
he did herculean work. After the capture of
Bloemfontein, and the subsequent evacuation of
Kroonstad, the commissariat department was
abolished (there wasn’t any regular food-supply),
and Herzog became commandant, achieving some
success. Judge Herzog’s wife was the first woman
to be forcibly removed to the refugee camp at
Port Elizabeth. Herzog was educated in South
Africa, and “finished” in England, where he
passed his exams, for the Bar.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the largest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage
to send their parcels direct to him. The firm has
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial par-
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance,
is solicited.
The colonial crawlers who imagine they
can see Prime-Ministerial capacity in Joe Chamber-
lain, are a misguided crew. With his com-
paratively limited opportunities for mischief as
Secretary for the Colonies, Chamberlain’s “ sand-
glass and revolver” diplomacy has already cost
his country countless millions and 20,000 lives.
Within five years he has publicly insulted three
great powers. Of the Czar he said—“ He who
sups with,the devil must sup with a long spoon.”
France he told “to mind her manners,” and only
recently he dilated on the “barbarity of the Ger-
mans in the Franco-German war.” It is good
that Prime Minister Chamberlain did not say
this, but only Colonial Secretary Joe. Much of
Salisbury’s time of late years was spent in cor-
recting Chamberlain’s blunders.

G. H. Reid said at Nuinurkah (Vic.) the
other night, “England’s navy needs men more
than money.” Truer words were never spoken.
Brassey, in his Naval Annual for ’92, expounds
the difficulty, with a violent alarum. He shows
how English sailors are rapidly decreasing in the
commercial navy, and how inadequate are the
naval reserves. Further, how the service goes to
the dogs through obstinacy in retaining aristo-
cratic privilege. Thus engineers, because they
do something useful, cannot be rated as officers.
A month or two ago the Admiralty implored the
Association of Head Masters of the great public
schools to recommend the entrance of students at
the Naval Engineering College, Keyham, Ply-
mouth. The masters sternly replied that privi-
leges must first be broken down. The engineer,
asked to join the navy, occupies a position like
that of the decently-educated and comely woman
who is advised to go into domestic service.

“E.F.”: The publication of Kruger’s
cypher correspondence with the German Govt,
might serve to show what was in Bismarck’s mind
when he predicted that S’Africa would be the
grave of the British Empire. It was Bismarck,
by the way, who is understood to have seen
nothing in Lord Salisbury but “a lath painted to
resemble iron.” Looking back at Salisbury’s dis-
creet way of treating the trouble in China what
tin ■ Britain was burdened with the S’African
War one is struck by the thought that Bismarck
was a poor judge. Tim Bismarckian dream of
empire-expansion by means of a vast mechanical
military force is a vanished dream, a buried illu-
sion. S’ Africa is its grave. It is also a place of
interment for Bizzy’s reputation as a prophet.
But Salisbury’s, drowsy wisdom justifies itself.
The fat Marquis, who dozed with his best eye
open, has gone into retirement, chuckling, whilst
German diplomacy awaits the revelation of its
failure.

The Mount Kembla Calamity.
When the earth roared and quivered like some
great beast in pain, the women of Mt. Kembla
township knew what was meant. Since coal was
first digged out of the ground the miner’s wife can
see, in the soft cascade of her bridal veil, the
wraith of the ‘ ‘ white-damp ” which may be
doomed to steal between her and her husband,
sundering irrevocably. The women knew; and
hurried with hearts aquake and gasping breath to
the pit-mouth. It was early in the afternoon of
July 31 when the roar was heard, and most of the
men-folk of the mining village which clings to the
steep side of Kembla and listens to the drowsy
hum of the Pacific beating on beaches a thousand
feet below, were in the mine : some hewing coal,
others going to their working places (for the time
was at the “change of shifts”)—close on three
hundred souls in all. Down in the town of
Wollongong a Court of Justice was making
inquiry as to what wages these men deserved
who went into the bowels of the earth for coal,
and a witness was saying that Kembla pit “ was
the safest in the world ” when, dulled by distance,
the sound of destruction was heard, and from the
sea-jetty a watcher noticed a tongue of blue flame
leap from the earth to tell of the death below.
• • • a
At the pit-mouth, after the first great roar,
absolute stillness, no groan or shriek to tell of
the carnage, the air bruised only by the sobs of
women, and the deep panting of men rushing up
to the rescue. There is no Victoria Cross to be
won in a coal-pit battle with Death, vet, when
men are being mown down by the white-damp
with more deadly sureuess than by any artillery,
eager volunteers are ever ready to rush in to the
rescue. Heroism is commonplace in this grimy-
calling of coal-getting, and whilst the long, long
list of men who have given up their lives for their
fellows’ sake is year by year lengthened, there
never creeps into the miner’s mind a thought of
timidity when the call comes for succour.
“Are you going in?” asked the colliery-manager
at Kembla of a miner, who had come up from Wol-
longong, and was seen near the pit-mouth getting
a lamp ready.
“Aye,” said the man. Not a touch of concern,
not a thought of any heroics.
“ Do you know the mine ? ”
“Aye, I think so.”
And the smudged hero slouched away on his
iron-shod feet—to save others, or to sacrifice him-
self, as Fate decreed.
* • * *
“There was a sad lack of proper organisation,”
wrote one critic of the rescue work. He had
found it hard to get figures or facts. With scores
dying underground, and other scores on the sur-
face eager to rush in to help, there was in truth
“a lack of organisation.” No man had a mind to
wait until he was drawn up in line, and called to
attention, and photographed before rushing to the
rescue. So eager was the heroic spirit that the
men went to work without any formality at
all. Irregular, yes, but surely not “sad.”
In all, about 60 men took part in the
rescue operations. Two of them lost their
lives, M'Cabe and M'Murray. M'Cabe was a
mining surveyor, and held, also, the rank of
Major in the Wollongong Garrison Artillery. A
man of truly heroic mould, he was one of the fore-
most in rescue-work at the Bulli pit disaster in
1887. Early on the scene at the Kembla calamity
his eagerness to save life lured him to his death.
AT THE PIT MOUTH AFTER THE EXPLOSION.
Dr. Nash, M.L.0., and Dr. Paton, Government
Health Officer, were sent by the State Premier to
Kembla when the news of the calamity reached
Sydney. They at once put themselves under the
command of Dr. Robertson, a medical doctor by
training, but now a chief man in New South Wales
coal-mining, and generally leader of the rescue
workers when a roar from the pit-mouth bellows
of disaster. A dour Scotchman, with a call for
prompt obedience in every note of his stern, per-
emptory speech, Dr. Robertson is a trusted
general to .whom the coal-men turn when perils
have to be faced. He knows the coal-mines and
their gase3, as other men know flowers and their
perfumes. With as hearty a contempt for danger
as for sloth, Robertson carries out heroic work
with no grace at all —almost uncouthly ; and can
do fine things better than he can talk of them.
He came to Kembla Colliery almost straight from
a sick bed, but the dregs of an influenza did not
prevent him from strenuous, perilous work through
a night and a day for 3ome 20 hours. When Drs.
Nash and Paton enlisted under his command, Dr.
Robertson ordered the one to accompany him, the
other to go by the side of McCabe into the under-
ground workings, and then, reflecting, counter-
manded these instructions. The men of medicine
would be of little use in the mine ; on the surface
their skill might revive some almost smothered
spark of life. No use risking their lives. But he
went in himself, and with him the heroic M'Cabe.
“If I had gone in,” said Dr. Paton, “I would
almost certainly have shared McCabe’s fate.” As
MAJOR M'CABE,
A hero who sacrificed his life to save others.
events resolved themselves, the two doctors were
able to do but little ; the miners brought out were
invariably dead, whisked out of life by the fumes
of “white-damp,” the most deadly poison known
to man.
When coal gas explodes or burns in the open
air, carbonic acid gas or carbon dioxide (C. 0.2) is
formed, and this, though not poisonous, will not
support life. In an atmosphere made up wholly
of it man quickly smothers, but should it be mixed
with some fresh air, life may continue. A close,
ill-ventilated room contains an excess of carbonic
acid gas, and is hurtful, though not necessarily
fatal. But when coal gas explodes where there is
but little o; ygen and much carbon, there are too
few atoms of oxygen to enable each atom of carbon
to attach to itself two of them ; and so they mate
together, carbon and oxygen, on equal terms.
That forms carbon monoxide (C. 0.), “ white
damp ” —most relentless of murderers. A breath
of it, and the blood, paralysed, forgets its func-
tions ; the heart beats out a wild alarm, flutters
faintly, stops, and life gently passes away. The
gas kills most mercifuPy A little weariness
a little faintness, a desir : to rest, no thought of
struggling, no anxiety for rescue, but swift, silent
Death
—which more softly falls
Than tired eyelids upon tired eyes.
« * * *
All that Th. rsday afternoon at the pit-mouth,
where only the pitifully-scarred earth suggested
the horror of what was below, tense faces of women
and frightened ones of children were mocked by
the bright gay sunshine and the most fair and
beautiful, scene. Rescue parties began to bring
corpses to the surface before nightfall, and
they were put in rows in a rough shed,
to be scanned by wives and mothers, who
would not surrender hope until the dead faces of
their loved ones compelled them to believe in the
cruelty of Fate. One mother, a babe in her arms,
was at the pit-mouth by five on Thursday after-
noon. The sun went down, and from the
Pacific below there came a gale moaning
over the mountain, as chilling, almost, as
Death itself. She waited on, stubborn not to
leave the spot until he was brought out, alive or
dead. Hours passed and the wind moaned with
deeper tone as the corpses in the shed grew row by
row. The woman waited on, rushing forward as
each streuher came up from the underworld,
scanning the bodies for his face. The wind went
down sinking its voice to a sigh, and the sun
suddenly showed up: still the woman waited on.
At high noon she was yet there, pushing forward to
each stretcher, deaf to all entreaty, dumb with
her weight of anxiety. Shadows lengthened on the
mountain and the chill of evening was again on the
land before they brought to her her husband,
dead, and she turned home in despair.
* • * »
In the house of the dead the sight was grim
beyond what words can tell. Here was the
slaughter of a battle-field but no red flags of
wounds to flaunt defiance to pale Death. Most of
the victims had, as it were, fallen asleep, and their
grimy faces were still life-like. One mouth clenched
a pipe; on several there hovered a smile as of rest
after much toil. A few bodies were scarred by
burns; in only three cases had Death shed blood.
Major M'Cabe, killed by the carbon monoxide,
had, in falling prone, cut his forehead ; the wound,
bled for hours after he was dead. The gas kills
without congealing the blood.
There were some 260 men in and about the mine
when the hand of calamity fell on Mt. Kembla.
Of these, some 87 perished, and two men lost their
lives in the work of rescue. The death roll totals
near ninety, so far as is known T the greatest of
any mining accident in Australia. There is in
N.S.W. a State fund for the support of the
widows and orphans of miners; this will be
largely aided by private charity, and those
who have lost their bread-winners will not
be rendered destitute. The cause of the
accident was clearly an explosion of gas—-
probably of a great accumulation of gas since the
wrecking force was so terrible. The mine was
worked with naked lights, and was thought to be
quite free from inflammable gases. But Mt.
Kembla was originally a shale mine, and there was
a great area of abandoned workings. Possibly an
accumulation of gas therein, and its sudden flow
to the new workings, was directly responsible for
the disaster. Whether the responsibility can be
traced further back, to some human neglect of
precaution, is a point to be settled by an inquiry ;
and there seems to be a general wish that that
inquiry should be most full and searching. This
hecatomb may thus save more lives in the future
than it has cost in the present. Death is very
impressive when he strikes suddenly into the
thick of men.
The vigorous and enthusiastic “ Puyakka,”
Dr. “Jack” Elkington, well-known to medical
and journalistic Melbourne, has just secured an
appointment after his own heart. A year or two
ago he left Victoria for Europe to further enlarge
Ins knowledge of sanitary science, and last week
he celebrated his negotiation of the D.P.H.
degree by being appointed to the charge of a
plague-district in the Punjaub (India).
Vic. Mines Department has completed its
measurements of rock-temperatures. At surface
the average temperature of the ground is
about ; there is an increase of 1° for every
80ft. sunk to 3700 ft. At 3624 ft. in the New Chum
Railway, under very good conditions for experi-
ment, 106 - 95 J was registered. Local action of
water, etc., may, of course, interfere with the
regularity of the increment. The average surface
temperature is almost the same as the average of
the air above the surface.
At Inglewood (Vic.), t’other day, a young
unmarried woman was charged with having aban-
doned the usual illicit baby, five weeks old, in an
open paddock. In evidence it transpired that the
mother had left the unfortunate child in the
paddock for two frosty nights while she spent the
evenings in playing music and dancing at a friend’s
house. When asked if she had put the child there
to die, she answered “Yes.” The strangest part
of the case was that the five-weeks-old infant,
after its two nights’ experience, was found in the
best of health. Some responsibilities take a lot of
killing.
Samuel St. John Topp (“Sinjin” to a
very wide and appreciative circle of friends) is
another good Victorian died too young. It is
easy to reckon a dozen of the same cult who have
passed within a decade, and all a long way the
youthful side of the grand climacteric. Topp
was but 52. He will make a gap at the Equity
Bar which no man now appearing can fill, and
will be missed by more friends than most men
make and keep. His work was all in the law,
but he never lost touch with literature, from the
time he won the Shakespeare prize at Melbourne
University. Duncan Gillies outlasts him, which
is strange.

Musolino, the Italian brigand, found
guilty at Rome of murdering eight travellers,
and sentenced to life imprisonment, the first
eight years in solitude, justly fears the sen-
tence, which is equivalent to a slow death in the
dark. In appealing for a revision Musolino writes :
“I will promise never to kill another person, and
I will also, swear to leave the country and seek my
fortune in America, the home of Liberty and
Justice! ” Sense of humor, hypocrisy, or plain,
ordinary front! Musolino, by the way, while
awaiting and during trial, received thousands of
amorous letters from women. Many were from
Englishwomen and residents in England.
One of Queen Victoria’s early despatches
to Gov. Gipps puts clearly the foolishness of the
nowadays desire to have Australian representa-
tives in the House of Commons. At that period
N.S.W. had appointed Francis Scott, M.P. fcr
Roxburghshire, at a salary of =2500 a year, to
represent N.S.W. in the Commons. The despatch
says that H.M., while having no objection to a
member of the Commons acting for Australia,
would yet point out that members of the Commons
not only represent the seats they fill but all her
dominions, and that one particular member would
add little to that representation. The Parlia-
mentary agent was later on abolished.
Prof. Gregory, of Melbourne University,
in a reoent lecture on the boundaries of Victoria,
mentioned that—
In the original document providing (or the separation
of Victoria from N.S. Wales, it was expressed that the
Murrumbidgee should be the boundary between the two
colonies. The clerk in the Colonial Office, however, who
had to copy the document several times, and who saw
references in it to the Murray also, was uncertain how to
spell the longer name, and also wished to save himself
trouble, so he calmly substituted Murray for Murrum-
bidgee in the document. Prof. Gregory declined to
vouch for the truth of the story, though he indicated
that it was not improbable. Prof. Kernot, at a subse-
quent stage, said that Mr. John Quick had informed him,
as the result of his researches, that. Sydney interest in
the House of Lords was responsible for altering the boun-
dary from the Murrumbidgee to the Murray, thus
depriving Victoria of the Riverina.

Perth (W.A.) Morning Herald greets
new Premier James in an indignant leading
article: —
Mr. James proposes that for the future the railway
receipts shall pav working: expenses, interest and main-
tenance, and that anything over shall go towards a
sinking fund. A far more statesmanlike policy would oe
to apply any surplus towards a reduction in freights,
and it is safe to say that this is what Parliament will
insist upon.
Thus does the damning suspicion of a base intent
to pay off part of the country’s debt fasten like a
caterpillar on Premier James’s character. And if
he doesn’t take the Herald’s warning that it is
“unstatesmanlike” to pay off anything, and that
the only true statesmanship lies in getting more
deeply into debt, there is no knowing where
James may come to. In Australia a politician
may begin his downward career by homicide, or
bigamy, or being a K.C.M.Gr., and then slowly
degenerate to unreliability, dissipation and other
sins, till he reaches the nethermost depths and
starts a sinking fund.
The Irish Independent on English pro-
posals for reciprocal trade relations with Australia:
It needs but little argument to show that once any
colony adopts the principle now so strenuously advocated
by certain English politicians and economists, the result
will be to deprive it of all power to protect its own manu-
factures against the import of British wares. It will, no
doubt, be pointed out, on the other hand, that the colony
in question would gain by a reciprocal free introduction
of its manufactures into Great Britain. This is precisely
the theory which was worked for all it was worth, and for
a great deal more than it was worth, in this country, at
the time of the passage of the Act of Union and of the
abolition of the Irish Exchequer. Our lost commerce and
ruined industries attest the honesty of those who put for-
ward the theory in question, and it is impossible to doubt
that any colony which is fascinated by its attractions will
experience precisely the same effects as were felt in
Ireland.

Unfulfilled Desires.
The men who die regretting that so much of
the work they purposed to do is left undone are
generally those who have achieved the most.
Charles Dickens was a marvel' of industry, always
working or planning future work ; but he died at
58, almost with the pen in his hand, an unfinished
novel on his desk, and with the earnest wish to
see Australia—the country where one of his sons
made his home—unfulfilled. Cecil Rhodes, the
man of millions (using the phrase in a double
sense, for among the millions of Britain’s sons
we look in vain for the like of him), dies at 49,
regretting that he has accomplished so small a
portion of the task he set himself to perform. It
is tragic when Death stops the worker. When
Sickness postpones the work it is sad enough,
though iij that ease Hope is always near by. Mr.
August Jansan, of Karabury Station, near Hay,
N.S.W., wrote, on January 20th, 1902:—“My
occupation (that of station carpenter) compelled
me to spend part of my time exposed to the heat
of the sun during the extremely hot summer of
1899, which caused me much subsequent suffer-
ing It began with sickness at the stomach and
a distaste for food. Then I was alarmed by acute
pains in the region of the heart. The bowels,
too, were irregular, costiveness alternating with
purging. Each week marked a change for the
worse. Sick headaches and pain at the chest,
with want of appetite, added to my other troubles,
made me nervous, depressed, and irritable. From
patent medicines, and a doctor whom I consulted,
I derived no .benefit whatever. Mine was a case
of acute indigestion, and a friend, who had used
Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup with much benefit,
persuaded me to try it. I agreed, and found
relief from almost the first dose. By the time I
had taken two bottles I knew that I was fairly
on the road to health. My strength returned
much quicker than it had departed. Befoie the
sixth bottle was emptied I was restored to a
thoroughly healthy and robust condition. I
could eat, sleep, and work without distress or
even discomfort. It is now six months since my
cure was completed, when I gave up taking-the
Syrup regularly; but, as a precaution, I still
take a couple of doses every week. Many other
persons in this district have taken it on my
recommendation, and in no case has it failed.”*
« SWEET NELL OF OLD DRURY.”

HAIR TONIC-^
tS the result of careful study, and this study was suggested in the first place by the appearance of
costly and considerable advertisements in respect of hair restorers or hair foods. . Having had
years of experience in the treatment of the hair of both sexes I thought that it might be to my
interest, and necessarily the interest of the public generally, to experiment. I experimented
st very modestly, and found that my endeavor was well received. I carried the matter further, and
entually placed upon the market a hair restorer which has been accepted by the public in a spirit
r beyond ray expectations. By sheer merit, hard work, and fair introduction by advertisement my
siness has increased considerably and continues to increase. I have never conceived any such
ieme as soliciting testimonials, and therefore find it quite unnecessary to offer any inducement to
tain these. The testimonials I have published come in in the ordinary way. They are from people
the ordinary course of life. Thev are not from princesses, but they are very genuine. I he dis-
,ctive feature of C A PILL A HAIR TONIC is that it is a medicinal preparation, and not a useless
rfume which the unwary are only too ready to purchase, and this cannot be too strongly impressed
on you. As a scalp-cleanser it is vouched for by the medical faculty, and unless the scalp is made
altliy no preparation ever yet introduced can possibly have the slightest effect. Its successful treat-
mt depends largely on the individual. Oftentimes some tonic treatment must be employed. Those
be taken internally, such as iron, arsenic, and quinine, which tend to improve the. nutritive powers
the scalp should never be had recourse to unless by the direction of one’s physician ; but stimu-
lts for external application which arouse the energies of the skin tissues, may be usefully and
rmlessly employed. The testimonials I refer to are all of recent date, showing that the public have
t been at all hasty in their judgment of my preparation, and I will only be too pleased to forward
pies of these upon application. Sold by all Chemists 2s. 6d. per bottle.

The Vic. Govt.’s purchase of the cyanide rights
for £20,000, and the subsequent charge of 2£ per cent,
for its use, is panning out all right. In little more than
18 months £O2OO has been paid in on 41,9900 z. recovered
from 482,278 tons of tailings.
• * » *
At Mount Morgan mine the open cut has
been carried down from the summit to 254 ft., and it is
intended to cut clean down to the Linda level—a depth
of 450 ft. This will mean that the quantity of waste rock
per ton of pay ore to be shifted will be very much greater
than at present, and now it is in the ratio of almost 1 to
2. This work is to be done without any increased cost
per ton mining, by means of the American steam shovel.
th ® s ?~ a 65-tonner, along with a complete plant-
will be put into commission within a few months. The
shove l picks up a load somewhere about that raised by 50
or 60 navvies and deposits it into cars each of a two-ton
capacity. These will be drawn by two 10-ton loco’s in
place of hand-trucking. Operations will be begun on the
N.E. and S.W. sides of the hill-top and the whole of the
oxidised ore body will be uncovered and also a very con-
siderable body of the sulphides. Exploratory work is to
be pushed ahead and three diamond drills of latest type
will shortly be on the job. Two of these are for inside
the mine and will cut a ljin. core 400 ft. in any direction,
up or down. The third will penetrate 1500 ft. and will be
used on outside work. Electrical drills are also to form
portion of the new equipment, the outlay for which will
be just on £lO,OOO. To provide for the treatment of the
tremendous quantity of extremely low-grade ore, which
will be available when the open cut is in full swing, tem-
porary works are to be erected—chiefly on the site of the
old upper works recently dismantled.

* • * *
There are ohanges in the management of the
Sulphide Corporation. O. F. Courtney, hitherto Broken
Hill manager, becomes general manager, with head-
quarters at Melbourne, midway between the mine and
the Cockle Creek works. J. Hebbard (till lately Govt,
inspector of mines) becomes Broken Hill manager ; and
A. E. Savage retires from the Cockle Creek works—the
last, it is suggested, on account of a difference of opinion
as to the Sulman-Pickard zinc process, in which Mr.
Savage still believes. Notwithstanding its big business,
the Sulphide Corporation has no directors this end of the
world ; up to now, Gibbs, Bright and Co. have been the
connecting link between the co.’s various works. Mr.
Moss succeeds Mr. Savage as metallurgist.
* * • *
Miner: Chillagoe hasn’t got over shock of last
Nov. I much question if it will ever be the place it was
12 or 18 months back. Redcap is doing a little developing
work, and in some places the lode looks real well (this is
the only place where they have a defined lode that I
know of). The Penzance tram-line is almost completed.
This is the third time the rails have been laid—and now
it is narrow-gauge. What it has cost the co. to put down
this road would look startling on paper. Boomerang,
Oalcifer and the Ruddigore are pottering away with a few
men. Mungana dead to the world ; its air-compressor
looks like a great iron monument to the memory of
Stewart. Great talk of the 0.K., but quite possibly it
will be forgotten in six months. Tartana, with good
management, should prove the best of them all. This is
about as bad a place as any on the continent for a man to
seek work of any sort. No wages, and contracts at
starvation prices; some parties being cut twice in one
week for making a little over wages.

“ Ham ” : Latest from Chillagoe is that gold has
been discovered here (Mungana). This ought to tickle
the shareholders when they think of the many thousands
dropped in these alleged copper-mines. Re this latest
discovery, already it is said that a strong (always
“ strong ”) syndicate has been formed with 4000 shares at
£1 per share ; and it is also rumoured the O.K. property
is on the English market at a fabulous figure. Apparently
anything can be floated up here that doesn’t need water.

A study in contrasts ! Whyte River Dredging
Co. (Tas.)prospectus : “Vendors feel confident that it is
one of the best and safest investments that has been
offered for many years. There is no prospecting, develop-
ing, or experimental work to be done. Three nuggets
were found, aggregating 4270 z., on part of the claim.
Good prospects can be got from the wash for the whole
distance (five miles). The bottom for the most part is
singularly free from rocks.” Manager’s report; “We
have dredged 90 hours, and have put through a great
amount of stuff, but, unfortunately, with very little
result. I washed the mats, and think there is about 2oz.
The wash is very tight, but I cannot get a prospect. The
ground is very rough, and is giving the machinery all it
can do.” Dredge won altogether about 200 z., and is now
idle and likely to remain so. A whyte frost!

Eaglehawk Consolidated, Maldon (Vie.), is a
striking example of bull-dog tenacity. Co. floated 15
years ago by John MTntyre. It has been twice recon-
structed, and after Spending nearly £lOO,OOO in explora-
tory work has just reached the stage when there is a
hopeful prospect of profits. .
* * * *
The failure of the New Chum Railway Co.
■(Bendigo) to find a lode at the 3770 ft. level will be a ter-
rible disappointment to the plucky shareholders, who
have had.to face a long succession of shilling shockers.
It will also be a blow to the field. Manager Rowe, who
has grown grey, in the co.’s service, recommends sinking
350 ft. more, to get into “settled country.” What says
the daylight miner of N.S.W. to that ? He has an idea
that earth disturbances are confined to a piecrust thick-
ness on the surface, and has golden visions about his lode
when “ she ” gets into, settled country at about 4000 ft. If
there be any merit in the system of Government advances
for mining development, New Chum Railway stands out
prominently as a case which should demand attention.

Noteworthy coincidence that Bendigo and
Charters Towers have, had several simultaneous : booms
and that at such times Ballarat has always been under a
cloud. When Ballarat’s turn comes it can be relied on to
make a meuntain out of a molehill. Its mining “ push ”
have studied their confidence-trick and can play it per-
fectly.
* * * •
“Alone”: The landlord curse sits heavily on
the chest of Vic. miners still, in spite of the Mining on
Private Property Act, with its cumbrous and (to poor
men) expensive circumlocution. Practically certain that
there would be fair developments at Mount William if
it was not for the way the absentee co. has been able to
block the miners. Again, near Kyneton lately, a small
gully was opened. It wag poor, but still tucker and a
bit over was being rooted out of it, and the run of the
gold made it about certain that the reef would be located
soon. Then, just as the men were settling down to graft
along comes the sheep cocky, with prohibitive royalty
demands, and notices to quit. The hungry bit of land
where the gold was being got wouldn’t feed a goat; yet
the owner is allowed by Vic. Govt, to block the possible
development of a goldfield. “ Sheep before men,” should
be the motto of Vic. Mining Department and its six
unspeakable “Mining Boards.”

Last- financial year 29 Victorian co.’s received
from the Govt, loans amounting to £43,413 for development
of mining ; 13 co.’s were rash enough, or hcmest enough,
to pay interest totalling £938, or about 2 per cent, on the
total. The interest outstanding at the end of the
year was £lOl7. Not a single advance was made to
Bendigo gold-field, where immediately north and south
of present div.-paying mines extensive prospecting would
be justifiable. The Jamieson Quicksilver 00. got the
smallest loan (£250), and Prentice United the largest
(£10,000).

Soko * an< * m tae has produced £170,000, so that, in all,

Living is very costly at Johannesburg and the

* * * •
Ivanhoe (W.A.) now leads the way of the Kal-
goorlie co.’s by publishing details of costs as follows :
Mining, 12s. fid. ; reduction, 12s. fid. ; general, 3s. 2d. ;
capital, 4s. 6d.; development, 7s. lOd. This totals 41s. a
ton, exclusive of London exes and depreciation. The reduc-
tion costs less than the Diehl plants are paying for water
and cyanide alone, and less than the furnace plants are
paying for firewood, water, and cyanide, leaving entirely
out of account cost of treatment and royalty. Mine
development is well ahead of plant, and is being well
maintained. Monthly profit fully £20,000, or 2s. a share,
so that enhanced divs. are probable ere long.
* * » *
Melb. Chamber of Mines (which, like the
Chambers of Commerce, Manufactures, and Agriculture, is
an anti-labor organisation solely, and therefore should
receive no more consideration from Govt, than does the
hod-carriers’ association, or any kindred body) fell in
badly on its recent appeal to Mines Minister Cameron
against the ventilation provisions. The mild request of
the mine bosses was that instead of stipulating that an
allowance of 100 cubic feet of air per man should be made
each minute, the quantity should be cut down to about
20ft. Also, one or two- of their number harped at great
length upon the theme that the owners and men were
the best judges in such matters, and led an unsuspecting
Minister to infer that the men objected to so much fresh
air because it was prejudicial to their health, or because
they had a keen relish for foul air, or something. But it
so happened that some of the men themselves were hover-
ing round that day, and they were actually waiting on
the Minister’s doorstep until the bosses came out. And
when they appealed for more, instead of less, ventilation,
all that Mr. Cameron could do was to gasp his astonish-
ment and look up the law on perjury to see whether it
affected deputations.

W.A. gold-yield for June is officially given as
189,6200 z., which brings up total for half-year to
1,035,7350 z. These figures are from mint and export
returns, and show enormous monthly fluctuations —
as much as 3G,9380z. between maximum and mini-
mum. These great fluctuations are caused, not by see-
saw production, but by the erratic despatch of gold
escorts.

The sensational return of 13500 z. from 7 tons of
picked stone from an abandoned show, known as the
Prince of Wales, in the Mt. Malcolm district (W.A.), shows
what some of the pioneers missed. The late Alick Forrest
was interested in the venture, but his luck was out.
Judge Paul (Q.), addressing a prisoner
whose sentence had been suspended :
A gaol is not a reformatory. Punishment never reforms
anybody. A prison is a place to keep a man out of mis-
chief, and a man always comes out of prison a worse man
than when he went in.
Gospel truth—every word !
Wily Tom Bent, Minister for Railways,
has already caught the Victorian Parliamentary
Labor party with a little chaff—not goo'd healthy
oaten chaff, but just common bedding-straw stuff
cut for the occasion. Bent has promised that one
man from among the unemployed shall be
appointed as an inspector to see that the rates of
wages paid by Govt, on earthwork jobs are fair.
This individual is supposed to represent the men,
but as he receives 14s. a day from the Depart-
ment, and will, no doubt, want to hang on' to a
soft thing when he has it, lie will undoubtedly
represent himself first, the department next, and
the men a long way afterwards. A few unthink-
ing members of the party are willing to take
the whole Tory Govt., of which Bent is such a
tower of strength, to its bosom without question
because of this alleged concession. It is clear,
too, that Bent is bidding for the support of one
or two Oppositionists by promises to do away
with the local rates system on the railways.
The dishonesty of abolishing local rates is as great
a scandal as the wiping off of Water Trust
liabilities a few years ago. When certain rail-
ways were constructed in Victoria the producers
guaranteed to pay a local rate in order to secure
the facilities of railway communication. Now
these producers, most of whom are State financial-
reformers, desire to repudiate their liability in the
matter, and Bent is the man to meet their wishes
if temporary political capital can be made out of
it.

Near Hell’s Gate.
IN SUMMER-TIME AT NYNGAN, N.S.W.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A white flame beat down on the place. The
dust, fine and soft as flour and nearly as
white, glared back at the sun till one’s eyes
smarted to look at it. The leaves of the pepper-
trees frizzled as they hung limp and breathless
before the deep verandah. The iron roofs of the
houses gave back heat for heat, and the rays struck
below into the coolest room till the head ached.
Several men sprawled on the boards of the
verandah. Some squatted on one heel, and the
form by the weatherboard wall held five. These
men gasped, and drew their shirt-sleeves over
their foreheads. A number of horses were hitched
to the rail in front where the sun-flames beat;
they hung their heads low and whisked the sticky
flies away with their tails.
Over on the plain the heat shimmered like steam.
Further on, it looked like Sydney Harbor waters
dancing beneath a north-east wind. The fringe
of trees seemed as if supported by the horizon-
line, the black trunks standing in silhouette
against the sky.
_ Patches of the plain were pale yellow, where a
little grass still scorched beneath the breath from
hell; but the rest showed only the reddish-brown
of the sunburnt soil. Not a breath of air stirred,
but the saffron whirlwinds raised spouts which
moved slowly along like mournful phantoms of
the plain. How do they rise when not a wind
stirs but theirs, which is like a rush of air from a
speeding locomotive ?
Scattered myalls alone studded the expanse,
their silvery-green and wavy foliage pleasing the
eye. Away in the heat crawled a team of horses,
dragging a table-top waggon loaded with wool,
the teamster riding by the side.
A group of emus stalked leisurely near a few
low bushes; some native-companions swung along
with their hoarse cries, and a flock of ibis went
through their alphabetical evolutions in flight.
The.grey and pink of the galah parrot glinted as a
few hundred birds rose at an alarm.
Behind the bar a short woman, of middle age,
watched the men outside, possibly wondering why
they did not spend their money faster. A sore-
eyed servant-girl leant against the door-post with
her arms folded, and gazed at the team in the
distance. She looked as dry as a ring-barked sap-
ling. and as slender; a typical product of Out-
Back.
A man with a twisted arm and club-foot lurched
up and down in front of the form, pleading for a
fight. He was not fastidious as to who took up
his challenge, but he favored a bleary-eyed son of
Erin who treated him tolerantly. Two other men
held an argument upon some legal point m steal-
ing. One was intellectually persuasive, the other
belligerent.
“Now, iflwuz to go into yer back yard and
steal a chicken ”
“By Gawd, only let me Catch you, and I’ll
break your back ! ”
A man and a woman on bicycles sped by,
leaving a thin line of dust behind them. The
man carried a swag and a rifle ; the woman a
swag, a billycan, and a dish. Three shearers fol-
lowed slowly, leading a pack-horse a-piece. Then
a man and a boy came in sight, both carrying
swags, but the man had a violin in his hand. The
sight of the violin created enthusiasm on the
verandah. A fillip to fun was scented. A cry is
set up of “ Hawker ! ” and “ Coo-ee ! ”
A limber shearer conducted the man to the
shade of a pepper-tree, and the people about
formed for an audience. The musician had sore
eyes, red-raw face, and a bleached beard. He put
his billy carefully where none could dent it, slung
his swag anywhere, and then began to slowly tune
up.
“ Play The Wearin’ of the Green.’ ”
It was not good music, but the travelling
musician played to the best of his ability, and he
played with great faith in his own powers. Every-
body was silent, saving the man with the club-
foot and the twisted arm, who still did his best to
persuade the blear-eyed Irishman to show what
was in him.
As he played, a woman with a clean face and
oiled hair, and a blue-and-red print dress, and
wearing a brown straw hat trimmed with purple,
paused to listen. She stood apart from the men,,
as one who respects herself.
“ What’ll I play now? ” asked the musician.
“ Play the ‘ Boilin’ Wather,’ ” said a wag.
At this moment the man with the club-foot had
his fist within two inches of the tip of the blear-
eyed Irishman’s nose.
“There’s a man insulting ould Ireland,” said
the Irishman. “If yez want to foight, go and
foight him ! He’s playin’ the ‘ Biling Wather.’ ”
“I’ll let ’em strap both hands behind me, Pat,
and I’ll best you then with my head.”
“ What’ll I play now ? ” asked the violinist.
“Play ‘Killarney,’” said the woman in the
purple-trimmed hat.
“Look here, Ned, cart your twisted carcase
out of this! ” said the lithe shearer to the man
with the club-foot. “If you was a proper-
formed man you wouldn’t be so eager for a fight.
Clear off, or I’ll sool Pat on to yer ! ”
“Ah, do play ‘ Killarney,’ won’t yer?” pleaded
the woman. Then she started to sing, driving
the tune forcibly through her nose to give it the
proper bush-ring:
By Killarney’s lakes and fells,
Emerald isles and mossy dells.
Then the violin got into swing with her, and
when the music was done the owner of the in-
strument took his hat round for the collection.
When he reached the woman he said : “ Didn’t
I see yer at Corkamidgera a couple of months
ago?”
“No, it must lia’ bin another woman, not me.
I’ve just come in from West’s. I cooked for the
shearers there. I cut-out with a cheque for £14.”
“ You must be a new woman, eh ? taking to
cooking for shearers. My word ! It must ha’ bin
a small shed ? ”
“Three shearers and three rouseabouts. They
was all bare bellies of sheep, too ; they cut-out
pretty quick, though the boys did say that the
wool was very hard —it had no yoke in it.”
“ Come an’ have a drink, missus.”
“ I don’t mind if I do have a lemonade,” she
said, bashfully.
She had evidently “had a few” already, by the
scent in her breath.
* * * *
By day, there is too much light and heat; by
night, the darkness is thick as the blackness of
Erebus. The heat is always there. The lights
from the windows and doors glow across the
gravelled pavement like shafts of gold. Boards
are thrown across the horse-trough, and a number
of men sit there to get the coolness of the water
beneath them. Others sit on the wooden kerb.
Limp victims to alcohol strew 'the verandah. A
piano tinkles in the parlor, which is railed-off on
the verandah. Under the window of the pajlor
reclines a drunken shearer, who does his best, to
make a pillow of the wall. He looks so uncom-
fortable that, in theory, his neck should crack.
I sat beside the streamlet and watched the water flow,
Where we together watched it one little year ago.
It sounds cool out there to hear of anything
flowing other than the usual throat cascades. So
the silence of the night is intensified to listen to a
girl in the house sing with her head back, mouth-
ing the ceiling, and throwing her arms up and
down the keyboard as if she were striking an
anvil. A commercial traveller stands beside her,
turning the leaves of the music. Now and again
she remembers to ogle him, but otherwise she
toils painstakingly through the song and finishes
up with a run on the keyboard.
“ Oh, thank you ! ” says the commercial travel-
ler.
“Oh, blarst yer !” yells the shearer under the
window.
There is a painful silence for a few seconds,
then the girl tosses her head and slams the window
down.
Somebody in the bar drags the musician of the
afternoon from his support on the counter to play
“ Willy Reilly.” His violin is on a chair. He
seizes the bow and sits on the chair and draws the
bow across his arm.
“He’s squashed the music-box!” cries
somebody in horror.
Then the owner of the instrument topples over
and is carried reverently into a bedroom where
there is a man tracing snakes on the wall, the
floor and the ceiling with his forefinger, and only
leaving off to ask piteously for a drink. “Just
one ! and s’elp me Gawd I’ll not ask fer another.”
Outside there comes suddenly a pattering as of
large drops of rain falling. At the same instant
the local band begins its weekly practice.
“ Here’s a plague of grasshoppers ! ” somebody
exclaims.
The grasshoppers have come into town in
millions. They settle into the clothes of the men ;
they cover the floor and crowd into the bar, settle
on the seats, the tables, the counter and the
shelves. Business at the bar becomes slack. The
men feel a repugnance to walking over these
things, each step crushing the insects under
foot; so they keep in one place.
The band plays, however; the cornet gurgles
and splutters. There is a grasshopper in it. The
trombone begins to sound in fits and starts, and at
last drops out of the practice. The drum plays
correctly all the time, but there is a constant
rattle between-whiles of a sort of kettledrum
accompaniment as the grasshoppers keep throwing
themselves on the parchment. At last practice
ends. Then people begin to step off to bed-
gingerly and slowly—to give the grasshoppers a
chance to get from under. What is the good of
waiting up when a plague has taken possession of
the town ?
And this is a picture of one day and night at
Nyngan.
n.s.w.
ROBERT BROTHERS.
At the Telegraph-Counter.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At the receiving-room counter of a central tele-
graph-office the waves of life—its sorrows, and joys
-—break first. That young man with an air of alert
weariness about him, looking at you with pathetic
resignation or subdued desperation, is the receiv-
ing clerk: he sees more of the emotions of life
than a popular Father-confessor.
All the troubles of the world come first to the
telegraph-counter: “ Mother is sinking, uncon-
scious.” Mother is really dead ; it is easy to
guess that from the look of the black-robed,
trembling figure that hands up the scrawl. Peter’s
sentence, Jim’s suicide, deaths, broken limbs,
fires, failures, “ for God’s sake send few pounds ”
—these come crowding in hour by hour, day by
day. The attendant from a hospital calls with a
heavy burden of woe—a budget of deaths, dyings,
accidents, fevers; the collection of a big institu-
tion, to be distributed all over Australia. A
body has been identified at the morgue. The
receiving-clerk is the first of the public to know
of whom it is. A girl comes in crying ; the hope-,
less case was over in the small hours of the morn-
ing, and a batch of telegrams will speed the news
to sons and daughters, brothers or sisters.
But births come to lighten the wife’s load of
sorrow. The proud young father (if it is a first
offence) looks furtively but fixedly at the counter-
man to see if he is going to grin at the wording
of the message. He does not —not even at twins,
or at the most ingenious comicalities invented to
convey the glad tidings without using plain
English.
Appointments are made every day, for the
theatre, for supper ; occasionally, the indications
point, for something worse. But these are mere
bubbles of sin on a stream of fate, wide and deep,
which flows through the telegraph-office—a stream
of joy and sorrow, of trouble and triumph, of
business and of daily-bread. A smart girl comes
in with two wires. One cancels an appointment
—sickness, or some other excuse. The second i <
to the other fellow—she is going to meet him. A
young woman comes in staggering. Her telegram
is a drunken scrawl. She is a constant visitor,
and is pestering the man who made her what she
is. Hubbies from the country lurch in and send
messages to wives at home. Luckily, the latter
don’t see any writing but that from the hand of
the strictly sober operator at the other end.
Some of the returned cohentingenters use-up a
lot of telegram-forms before they make them-
selves understood. One warrior made 17 attempts
to establish a line of communication with his rh
latives, but alcohol is a ruthless enemy, and hail
him ambushed badly, and lie was cut off from his
reinforcements every time. He attempted to
explain verbally to the patient man at the counter,
but his utterance was less intelligible than his
writing. After he c-leared-out we inspected his
pile of manuscript, and found that, through it all,
“one increasing purpose ” ran, till, at the finish,
it clearly revealed itself as “ send six squids.” All
else was mystical.
The country cousins at show-time are good
company, but rather trying. One wet show-day
they all caught colds, and, with a Hash o’ bucol o
humor, the saying got. starte 1 that they had “ the
pleuro.” We got that joke from every honest
bumpkin who came in during two whole
days, accompanied by some specimen coughing
and the thunderous mirth of the man who laughs
at his own wit. It grew monotonous atlast, and
the clerk had to brace himself every time, he saw
a farmer-looking man putting Iris head in t' o '
door; sure as death, he would walk up and cough,
and remark that he rather thought he had “the
ploorer ” and laugh boisterously.
All kinds of people come in—swells who are
nasty, swells who are civil, gorgeously-attired
young men who write horribly and spell worse, an
infrequent member of Parliament who does ditto.
“Is this word intended for ?” “Yes;
pens very bad in this office.” “ Must be,” thinks
the clerk, “ when they spell so damnably,” but he
only thinks it. Working-men, business-men,
loafing men, drunken men, bookies’ clerks, and
jockeys drop in. So do fine ladies, plain women,
and occasionally the female denizens of Celestial-
Alley or Exhibition-Lane, who use shocking
language and smell obtrusively of beer or stale
rum. Madmen come in to wire for £lO,OOO remit-
tances. The English Johnny lavishes his super-
fine accent. A dozen turbaned Hindus slink in to
assist one of their number to ask a question, and
slink out again, leaving a friendly “good-bye”
from each to the man at the counter. Chinamen
come daily to wire to the banana centre of Queen-s
land or to cable to Hongkong. Pidgeon-English
talkers get the boys to write their messages.
Others write a good fist and talk like University
men.
Almost all foreigners, including Indians and
Chinese, are courteous, though the latter are
approximating more to the English manner,
dashed with some familiarity and more friendli-
ness. Cowper partially excused the boorishness
of his countrymen on the peculiar ground that
they were free men. They have still the charac-
teristic. Most of the so-called better-class men of
our breed cannot be polite without being con-
descending ; the ordinary kind are clumsy and
awkward in their manners, though some are
good-natured enough and some are surly. They
never learn to say “good-day” or “good-bye.
Somehow or other, no foreigner of any breed, no
matter how highly placed, seems ever to treat you
as less than an equal.

A Picture.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My friend, the portrait-painter, said to me,
“ Who was the girl. . . with you at such a place ?
It does a fellow worlds of good to see
So pure a face.
Her name ? ” he asked, and at my smile he smiled,
And listened, standing sideways with bent head.
“That is my secret, not to be beguiled
From me,” I said.
He swore by all the gods whom, though they be
Dead gods, deaf-eared, men swear by none the
less,
That I misjudged his motives, and that he
Sought only—guess
An artist’s triumph—leave to take and trace
Her beauty on his canvas, painter-wise,
To capture—at his soul’s command—her face
And her dear eyes.
I heard him out, then said, “The thought occurred
To one, an artist and your equal, sir ;
He saw, and, to his depths of being’stirred,
He painted her.”
“His name?” he asked, and quietly I smiled,
While ha stood there with eager, leaning head,
“That is my secret, not to be beguiled
From mo,” I said.
“He painted Well; but, first, let me recall
That God who fashioned us, in every breast
Placed brush and colors and the wherewithal
To paint what best
Could please the heart within; and some have
lined
Their souls with pictures, fair and manifold,
But some have painted only one, and shrined
That one in gold. *
Let me recall : She sat beside the fire,
Her chin upon her hand, her forehead set
With furrows, set, who knows, by what desire,
By what regret ?
There lies the pain of it—to be not part
And parcel of the secret, moving train
That stirs a loved one’s mind and fills her heart
With joy or pain.
Her frail, sweet body, girlish in its shape,
Seemed lifted in the firelight—half to float.
Her hair, light brown, so dressed that it might
drape
Her shapely throat ; ;
Her small red mouth that I had given all
My heart, my soul—and all my soul calls mine,
To crowd with kisses—in the rise and fall
Of that fireshine
Seemed such a picture, so worth looking at,
That my soul, wondering, seized a brush and
made
A portrait of her on such canvas that
No time shall fade.
That portrait lights a gallery not of stones,
By stars unvisited, by suns unlit ;
Its sweetness sacred to the mind alone
That painted it.”
RODERIC QUINN.
“Dutchy” and the Fat Baby.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“When I come to Australia, my modder say to
me, ‘ Fritz Stofel, you keep avay from der fraulein
larrikin near de jam-factory.’ Und I keep avay,
und keep avay, ondil, one day, I see a crowd of
fraulein larrikin shtandin’ oudside, und I go over
und sthand by, mit my head durned avay. Der
vas a row among dem, und one girl mit a leedle
fat baby did sthrike anoder girl. ‘Don’d pake
de borak ad my bloke, Beel,’ she say, ‘ because he
haf de boddle-card.’ Und de odder girl sthrike
her back. Den de larrikin fraulein mit de leedle
fat baby say do me, ‘ Dutchy, hold mein kid,’ und
I hold de kid, und de two girls fighd und smash
de big window nexd door. Und dey was covered
mit blood. Den a boliceman catch dem both
und run dem along to de gaol. I rush up und
say, * Hi! here, boliceman, here vas de one girl’s
leedle baby.’ De boliceman say, ‘Go vay, you
peeg, or I lock you up too.’ De beople laugh ad
me holdin’ de baby, because I look a dam fool.
Den I go indo a baker’s shop und ask de man if
he vaut a fat baby. He say, ‘Oh, hell ! go vay—
de missus is in dere ! ’ Und Igo vay. Und de
beople laugh again.
“Den I see de clergyman come along de
sthreed, und I ask him to take de leedle baby.
‘You rascal !’ he say—‘how dare you say such
thing ! ’ Den id begin to rain, und I cover up de
baby, und go home to my modder ; und she say :
‘ Fntz Stofel, vhere you get de fat baby ?—kviclc
tell ! I tole you to keep from de jam-factory und
de larrikin fraulein. Den I tell modder about de
fight, und de neighbors come in und tell modder to
take de baby to de gaol. But my modder say
nothin’. It vas cold, und de rain did make us
shiver. My modder light de fire, und hold de leedle
fat baby in her arms. Und der vas my leedle
brudder Wilhelm in der cradle just by. ‘ Take de
baby to de gaol,’ says de neighbors. But my
modder again say nothin’. ' ‘ Let me take it to de
prison, modder,’ I say. ‘Hush! Fritz Stofel,’
she say—‘hush!’ Den me hear that the two
larrikin fraulein get one month in gaol. Und
still my modder not go to de gaol mit de baby.
She do not like de gaol, my modder. Der hang-
man live there, she say, und der flogger, und der
mad murderer, und she say it was no place for de
leedle shild.
“De neighbors say my modder was dam silly
Dutch fool. But my modder put de leedle baby
along mit my brudder Wilhelm in der cradle, und
der month was -up shoost when dey learn to cry
togedder. Und in der mornin’ Igo mit modder
to de gaol, and modder carry de baby. Den we
wait by de gate ondil de two fraulein larrikins
did come oud. Und I did point to de leedle
fraulein und say, ‘ Modder, der was the fraulem
dot own de child.’ Und I valk away. Und a
man named Beel de Larrikin come up to me und
say, ‘ Are you de blanky Dutchy dot did keep my
kid for de month V Und he walk round me ondil
I tink he go to hit me de. stoush. Und Isay,
‘ Yes, Beel; it vas me keep de lad de month.
Den he look at me boots und me vaiscoat, und 1
tink again that he go hit me de stoush, but he
say, ‘ Come mit me, Dutchy, und have de blanky
beer.’ Und Igo und have de beer. He say,
‘Dutchy, your modder vas crying; und I look
over to de gaol und see dot de fraulein mit de
baby did laugh und cry both togedder.”
N> s . w . Albert Dorrington.
His Pretty Sister.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Arthur! me lad! just jump over to the
wharfinger’s hut, and ask him if he would mind
stepping alongside when he’s got time.” Jones,
pleased at the mate’s familiarity—he’d never
before been anything but Jones—obeyed, with
alacrity, and the pretty sister he was so proud of
was left at the mercy of Mr. Smears’ blue eyes and
dimpled chin, an armament the guileful owner
had oftimes proved irresistible in attack on the
feiriale heart.
Jones, the flat-chested and insignificant, had, in
common with many ugly brothers, a pretty sister,
and now, with aIL a youngster’s pride in his first
ship, he had brought her down to the wharf where
the vessel lay—not the wisest proceeding, perhaps,
when one’s sister happens to be as ingenuous as
she is pretty, and the mate of the vessel is of Mr.
Smears’ stamp.
Smears was on deck when the two happened
along, and noting with pleased surprise the come-
liness of Miss Jones, received them affably.
Miss Jones had big, round, grey eyes with a
questioning look in them. Mr. Smears’ wicked
blue ones raked their very depths and told them
something of what they asked. When her brother
returned from his quest she was blushing prettily
at the mate’s inanities. Jones felt quite proud of
this handsome, masterful man—the mate of “his
ship.”
Mr. Smears received an invitation to take tea at
the Jones’, which lie accepted. When a man like
Smears drinks weak tea with such a very ordinary
old lady as Jones’ ma, a young cub like her son,
and so prepossessing a daughter as hers, it is to
some purpose—a purpose such as Smears loved.
They had left Sydney, with its attractions and
detractions, bound for the East Indies. Now
that the ship was at sea again, Jones found that
the mate, instead of relaxing in his usual vigorous
treatment of the ship’s boys, in liis case rather
intensified it.
The lad was surprised and indignant; the sight
of him seemed to enrage the mate. 'Jones, with
all his ugliness, was strangely reminiscent of the
pretty sister at home. He had struck-him once,
and the same look of pained reproach looked out
at him from the boy’s eyes—a thousand miles from
her, he wished to forget—the same look he had
seen in her eyes; on their‘last meeting. He struck'
him in the dark, after that.
Mr. Smears had been imbibing freely of the
country’s arrack, and his temper see-sawed
between the jocular and the aggressive. The first
batch of letters had arrived from the shore. The
mate looked over the ship’s counter to where tha
boy was seated alone on a staging, painting, - i
“Letter for you, Jones,” he said, “from ola
woman—got letter self from sister—pretty girl.
Sister, ay? lots‘o’ love, in it for me, and you,
Jones ’’—then with drunken portentousness: * ‘Say.
Jones! what price me as proud father, ay? and
you as proud uncle ? ”
The boy looked up. at the red, leering face with
its swollen veins, his own ablaze with shame and
hatred. Mr. Smears closed one eye, and presently
threw one leg over the ship’s rail. “ Damn yer,
Jones,” he said, “I’ll teach yer show me proper
respect,” and he slid down the stage ropes on to
the frail planking.
The boy instinctively shifted his position to the
other end, and the mate, crossing over on to the
ceiitre, reached after him.'
The plank, creaking with the man’s weight,
bent and snapped, and the boy, swinging by the
stage ropes against the hot iron of the ship’s side,
saw the man borne away on the swift current,
which, strong swimmer though he was, seemed
certain to prove too much for him. Then,
dropping into the warm water, the boy swam
through a shoal of monster pink jelly-fish, heading
up stream towards the mate, just as he turned his
face to the “Paddy” husks lining the shore.
“ Keep away, boy 1 Curse you ! keep off me ! ”
he gasped, panting like a spent dog. Then he
struck the boy, and, struggling in the foam, they
were carried seaward on the swift flood.
Some few of those aboard the vessel watched
them as they swirled and sunk and rose again, the
mate striving to beat the boy off, till they sank
for the last time—together—and so they were
buried. For, on the bodies being recovered, it
was found impossible to separate them.
Jones had avenged his pretty sister.
M.L.
F. MARRYAT NORRIS.
A Parliamentary return shows that 17
barristers have been hopping on and off N. 8. W.
major and minor benches this last couple of years
—seven hopped on to the Supreme and ten on to
the District Bench.
A Bulletin correspondent:
The proposed retrenchment of Yic. railway employees
will involve some cases of much hardship. Take the case
of one fireman (doubtless one of many) with almost II
years’ service—the best years of his life. In Feb, ’92, he
reached his nine “ bob ” a-day (nine hours). Not a rise
since, but plenty of reduction and deduction (the Dept,
makes a subtle difference between the two forms of
robbery). It is said lhat railway men are as well-paid as
other men for similar work. But are there any other
men who have been over 10 years working at the same
wage? If a railway-man is not worth more to his
employer (the public), he ought to be “retrenched”
altogether to make room for a better man. And the
daily work (of enginemen at least) has much increased
of late. Trains are heavier, engines bigger—more coal is
used, and more skill required in handling. Also, the
coal is inferior to what it was, and the engines are in
worse order and dirtier than they were, all meaning
harder work and greater vigilance. The landholders are
getting the benefit of “ starvation-rates ” for the carriage
of fodder. The Railway Department is doing work for
Fatman at a loss, and said Fatman wants to make up
the loss by retrenching the employees; Again, £24,000
(approximately) would be the saving by the proposed
stoppage of increments, and £24,000 (approximately) is
also the estimated loss caused to the railway revenue by
the abuse oi privilege by members of the Defence force.
Heaven knows what is the cost of the legitimate privi-
leges. Discouragement and disgust, in a word, prevail at
present throughout the whole Vic. railway-service.
